### [1] [BatteryPass-12K: The First Dataset for the Novel Digital Battery Passport Conformance Task](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.26986)
*Tosin Adewumi,Martin Karlsson,Lama Alkhaled,Marcus Liwicki*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces BatteryPass-12K, the first public benchmark for classifying whether digital battery passports conform to EU regulations, and evaluates 22 language models on this task.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As the EU’s new battery regulation on digital battery passports (DBPs) comes into force, regulators, manufacturers, and auditors need automated ways to check whether DBP documents conform to the rules. However, no public dataset exists to train or evaluate language models for this compliance-checking task, limiting research and deployment. The paper aims to fill this gap by defining a precise conformance classification task and releasing a benchmark dataset for it.

Method: The authors synthetically construct a 12K-example dataset, BatteryPass-12K, derived from real DBP pilot samples, labeling instances for conformance vs. non‑conformance. They then benchmark 22 language models in zero-shot classification, covering small language models (SLMs), mixture-of-experts (MoE) models, and dense large language models (LLMs). They further run few-shot experiments (adding in-context examples) and evaluate robustness against prompt-injection attacks. Performance is measured via F1 scores on validation and test sets, along with 95% confidence intervals.

Result: Thinking-style models perform best, with GPT-5.4 achieving an average F1 of 0.98 (±0.03) on validation and 0.71 (±0.22) on the test set. Few-shot prompting significantly boosts accuracy across models. Many strong, general-purpose frontier models still struggle with this specialized regulatory task. Increasing parameter count alone does not guarantee gains, as some small language models outperform larger LLMs. Prompt-injection attacks significantly degrade model performance on the conformance classification task.

Conclusion: BatteryPass-12K is a challenging, domain-specific benchmark for DBP regulation conformance that reveals limitations of current LMs, including large frontier models. Structured reasoning (“thinking”) and few-shot examples are important for good performance, while size alone is insufficient. The dataset, released under CC-BY-4.0, can support further research on regulatory compliance and other battery-related tasks such as lifecycle reasoning, and highlights the need to consider robustness against prompt injection in practical deployments.

Abstract: We introduce a novel task of digital battery passport (DBP) conformance classification and introduce the first public benchmark for the task: BatteryPass-12K, created synthetically from real pilot samples. This is as the EU's battery regulation on DBPs comes into effect soon and there exists no public dataset. We evaluated 22 language models (LMs) in zero-shot inference, spanning small LMs (SLMs), mixture of experts (MoEs), and dense LLMs. We also conducted analysis, additional evaluations of few-shot inference and prompt-injection attacks to find that (1) Thinking models have the best performance (with GPT-5.4 scoring 0.98 (0.03) and 0.71 (0.22) on average as F1 (and confidence interval at 95%) on the validation and test sets, respectively), (2) few-shot examples improve performance significantly, (3) generally capable frontier models find the task challenging, (4) merely scaling model parameters does not necessarily lead to improved performance, as SLMs outperformed some LLMs, and (5) prompt-injection attacks degrade performance. We note that BatteryPass-12K, though limited to real pilot samples, may be useful for other known or emerging tasks in the battery domain, e.g. lifecycle reasoning. We publicly release the dataset under a permissive licence (CC-BY-4.0).

</details>


### [2] [CL-bench Life: Can Language Models Learn from Real-Life Context?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27043)
*Shihan Dou,Yujiong Shen,Chenhao Huang,Junjie Ye,Jiayi Chen,Junzhe Wang,Qianyu He,Shichun Liu,Changze Lv,Jiahang Lin,Jiazheng Zhang,Ming Zhang,Shaofan Liu,Tao Ji,Zhangyue Yin,Cheng Zhang,Huaibing Xie,Jianglu Hu,Jingcheng Deng,Lincheng Li,Minda Hu,Shaolei Wang,Syrus Zhao,Weichao Wang,Yan Lei,Yang Liu,Yanling Xiao,Yiting Liu,Zenan Xu,Zhen Guo,Ziliang Zhao,Pluto Zhou,Tao Gui,Qi Zhang,Xuanjing Huang,Yu-Gang Jiang,Di Wang,Shunyu Yao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces CL-bench Life, a human-curated benchmark to test language models’ ability to learn from messy, real-life contexts, revealing that current frontier models perform poorly on such tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As AI assistants move from professional to everyday-life settings, they must handle messy, fragmented, and highly personal or social contexts (e.g., group chats, personal archives, behavioral traces). Existing benchmarks mainly use clean, synthetic, or professionally oriented contexts, so it is unclear whether current frontier language models can reliably perform context learning in realistic, everyday situations. The paper aims to fill this evaluation gap.

Method: The authors construct CL-bench Life, a fully human-curated benchmark with 405 context–task pairs and 5,348 verification rubrics, each grounded in realistic scenarios such as multi-party chat histories and fragmented behavior logs. Tasks require models to read and reason over these complex contexts and generate answers that are evaluated using detailed rubrics. They then evaluate ten frontier language models on this benchmark and measure their task-solving rates.

Result: Across ten frontier language models, performance on CL-bench Life is low. The best model attains only a 19.3% task-solving rate, and the average across models is 13.8%. The models especially struggle with contexts like messy group chat histories and fragmented behavioral records, indicating substantial limitations in current real-life context learning capabilities.

Conclusion: Real-life context learning—reasoning over messy, fragmented, socially and personally grounded contexts—remains a significant challenge for current frontier language models. CL-bench Life serves as an important testbed for diagnosing and driving progress in this area, and improvements on this benchmark are expected to translate into more capable, reliable AI assistants for everyday use.

Abstract: Today's AI assistants such as OpenClaw are designed to handle context effectively, making context learning an increasingly important capability for models. As these systems move beyond professional settings into everyday life, the nature of the contexts they must handle also shifts. Real-life contexts are often messy, fragmented, and deeply tied to personal and social experience, such as multi-party conversations, personal archives, and behavioral traces. Yet it remains unclear whether current frontier language models can reliably learn from such contexts and solve tasks grounded in them. To this end, we introduce CL-bench Life, a fully human-curated benchmark comprising 405 context-task pairs and 5,348 verification rubrics, covering common real-life scenarios. Solving tasks in CL-bench Life requires models to reason over complex, messy real-life contexts, calling for strong real-life context learning abilities that go far beyond those evaluated in existing benchmarks. We evaluate ten frontier LMs and find that real-life context learning remains highly challenging: even the best-performing model achieves only 19.3% task solving rate, while the average performance across models is only 13.8%. Models still struggle to reason over contexts such as messy group chat histories and fragmented behavioral records from everyday life. CL-bench Life provides a crucial testbed for advancing real-life context learning, and progress on it can enable more intelligent and reliable AI assistants in everyday life.

</details>


### [3] [Useless but Safe? Benchmarking Utility Recovery with User Intent Clarification in Multi-Turn Conversations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27093)
*Mingqian Zheng,Malia Morgan,Liwei Jiang,Carolyn Rose,Maarten Sap*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: CarryOnBench is an interactive benchmark evaluating whether LLMs can recover helpfulness in multi-turn conversations when users clarify benign intent, while remaining safe.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM safety alignment focuses on robustness against adversarial attacks in single turns and ignores whether models can update their interpretation and regain utility after benign clarifications. There is a need to assess if models are being appropriately cautious versus simply unresponsive to clarified user intent.

Method: The authors construct CarryOnBench, starting from 398 superficially harmful queries that actually have benign intent. They simulate 5,970 multi-turn conversations by varying follow-up clarifications, yielding 1,866 distinct flows (4–12 turns, 23,880 responses) across 14 LLMs. They introduce Ben-Util, a checklist-based metric using atomic items to measure how well each response satisfies the benign information need, and jointly evaluate safety and utility across turns.

Result: At the first turn, models satisfy only 10.5–37.6% of the benign information need; when benign intent is explicitly provided upfront, fulfillment rises to 25.1–72.1%, showing that low utility is largely due to intent misinterpretation. With clarifications in multi-turn settings, 13 of 14 models recover to match or exceed the single-turn benign baseline, but with differing recovery costs. The analysis reveals three multi-turn-specific failure modes—utility lock-in, unsafe recovery, and repetitive recovery—and shows that conversations tend to converge to similar harmfulness levels regardless of initial model conservativeness.

Conclusion: Single-turn safety evaluations miss crucial aspects of model behavior. CarryOnBench demonstrates that LLMs differ in how they trade off safety and utility over multi-turn clarifications, with distinct failure modes and recovery costs. Properly aligned models should update their behavior when users clarify benign intent, maintaining safety while restoring helpfulness; assessing this requires interactive, multi-turn benchmarks like CarryOnBench.

Abstract: Current LLM safety alignment techniques improve model robustness against adversarial attacks, but overlook whether and how LLMs can recover helpfulness when benign users clarify their intent. We introduce CarryOnBench, the first interactive benchmark that measures whether LLMs can revise their interpretation of user intent and recover utility, while remaining safe through multi-turn conversations. Starting from 398 seemingly harmful queries with benign underlying intents, we simulate 5,970 conversations by varying user follow-up sequences, evaluating 14 models on both intent-aligned utility and safety. CarryOnBench yields 1,866 different conversation flows of 4--12 turns, totaling 23,880 model responses. We design Ben-Util, a checklist-based metric that evaluates how well each model response fulfills the user's benign information need using atomic items. At turn one, models fulfill only 10.5--37.6% of the user's benign information need. When the same query includes the benign intent upfront, models fulfill 25.1--72.1%, confirming that models withhold information due to intent misinterpretation, not limited knowledge. With benign clarifications in multi-turn conversations, 13 of 14 models approach or exceed this single-turn baseline, yet recovery cost varies across models. We identify three failure modes invisible to single-turn evaluations: utility lock-in, where a model rarely updates despite clarification; unsafe recovery, where a model updates at disproportionate safety cost; and repetitive recovery, where a model recycles prior responses rather than providing new information. Moreover, conversations converge to similar harmfulness levels regardless of how conservative the model starts. These findings expose a gap that single-turn evaluations miss -- whether a model is appropriately cautious or simply unresponsive to clarified user intent.

</details>


### [4] [Exploring the Limits of Pruning: Task-Specific Neurons, Model Collapse, and Recovery in Task-Specific Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27115)
*M. K. Khalidi Siam,Md. Tausif-Ul-Islam,Md. Reshad Romim Khan,Mohammed Ali Hossain,Mushfiqul Amin,Labib Hasan Khan,Niloy Farhan,Farig Sadeque*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies neuron pruning in task-specific language models and shows that a small set of highly task-specific neurons is critical for performance, while many others can be safely pruned using an activation-based selectivity metric, improving efficiency with limited accuracy loss and recoverable via fine-tuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While neuron pruning is commonly used to reduce the cost of large language models, it is not well understood whether all neurons in task-specific models contribute equally to task performance. The authors aim to understand neuron specialization, quantify which neurons are important for specific tasks like math reasoning and code generation, and determine how much pruning these models can tolerate without severe performance degradation.

Method: The authors conduct systematic pruning experiments on language models specialized for mathematical reasoning and code generation at 1.5B and 7B scales. They introduce an activation-based selectivity metric to identify neurons with low contribution to the target task and selectively prune them, comparing this strategy with random pruning. They also perform reverse pruning, where only the most task-specific neurons are removed, and examine the effect of different pruning ratios on accuracy, failures, model size, VRAM usage, and throughput. Finally, they fine-tune pruned models to assess post-pruning recoverability.

Result: Selective pruning using the activation-based selectivity metric consistently outperforms random pruning at comparable sparsity levels, indicating a systematic advantage from targeting low-contribution neurons. Reverse pruning shows that removing a small fraction (~10%) of highly task-specific neurons leads to a complete collapse in task performance, whereas pruning a larger fraction (~30–35%) of less critical neurons only partially reduces accuracy. The authors observe substantial reductions in parameters and runtime VRAM, along with improved inference throughput. They identify a robustness threshold around 15–20% pruning, after which accuracy and generation quality degrade sharply. Fine-tuning helps recover much of the lost performance, especially for aggressively pruned models.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that task-specific language models contain specialized neurons that are crucial for target-task performance, and that important task information is concentrated in a relatively small subset of neurons. Activation-based selective pruning is more effective and robust than random pruning, enabling meaningful efficiency gains before a robustness threshold is crossed. Despite significant pruning, models retain redundancy and can have their performance substantially restored through fine-tuning, offering practical guidance on pruning strategies and post-pruning adaptation for task-specialized LLMs.

Abstract: Neuron pruning is widely used to reduce the computational cost and parameter footprint of large language models, yet it remains unclear whether neurons in task-specific models contribute uniformly to task performance. In this work, we provide empirical evidence for the existence and importance of task-specific neurons through a systematic pruning study on language models specialized for mathematical reasoning and code generation. We introduce an activation-based selectivity metric to identify neurons with low contribution to the target task and prune them while preserving target-task accuracy, and compare selective pruning with random pruning. Selective pruning consistently outperforms random pruning, indicating that activation-based selectivity provides a systematic advantage over random pruning. Reverse pruning experiments further show that removing a small subset of highly task-specific neurons (~10%) causes complete performance collapse, suggesting that there exist task specific neurons and critical task information is concentrated in a small portion of the network. In contrast, selective pruning of less critical neurons (~30% - ~35%) reduces accuracy but still preserves significant performance. We also observed consistent reductions in parameters and runtime VRAM usage, along with improved inference throughput as pruning increases. Experiments on both 1.5B and 7B models reveal a robustness threshold around 15-20% pruning, beyond which accuracy loss and generation failures increase sharply. Fine-tuning substantially recovers performance across pruning levels, particularly for aggressively pruned models. These findings provide empirical evidence of neuron specialization in task-specific language models and offer insights into pruning robustness, model redundancy, and post-pruning recoverability.

</details>


### [5] [Cross-Lingual Response Consistency in Large Language Models: An ILR-Informed Evaluation of Claude Across Six Languages](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27137)
*Camelia Baluta*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes and demonstrates an ILR-based framework to evaluate Claude’s multilingual performance, revealing systematic, interpretable cross-lingual output differences that matter for fair deployment.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard LLM benchmarks are mostly computational, monolingual, and not closely aligned with human proficiency scales used in language testing. The authors want an evaluation method that: (1) grounds performance in the ILR skill level descriptions widely used in government/OPI contexts, (2) assesses multiple languages in a comparable way via semantically equivalent tasks, and (3) uncovers not just accuracy differences but also pragmatic, cultural, and stylistic variation that could impact real-world multilingual use and equity.

Method: They design 12 prompt clusters aligned with ILR complexity levels 1 to 3+ and translate them into six languages (English, French, Romanian, Spanish, Italian, German), keeping semantic content constant. For Claude (Sonnet 4.6), they collect 216 outputs (12 prompts × 6 languages × 3 sampling runs). Evaluation uses a two-layer approach: (1) automated quantitative metrics on the responses (e.g., length, surface similarity/divergence across languages and clusters), and (2) expert qualitative ILR assessment by a professional with 12 years of ILR/OPI experience, who examines language use and cross-lingual patterns in pragmatics, creativity, terminology, culture, and emotional-support behavior.

Result: Quantitatively, they find notable surface differences, such as French outputs being about 30% longer than German outputs for identical prompts, and the largest cross-lingual divergences occurring in creative and affective prompt clusters. Qualitatively, the expert identifies five recurring cross-lingual patterns: (1) different pragmatic disambiguation strategies across languages, (2) divergent aesthetic/literary traditions shaping creative responses, (3) variation in technical terminology norms within each language, (4) cultural calibration gaps where culture-specific details are replaced by neutral templates, and (5) language-specific tendencies in referring to institutions or resources when giving emotional support.

Conclusion: ILR-guided expert judgment provides a novel and underused way to evaluate LLMs that complements purely automatic benchmarks. Claude’s cross-lingual outputs are not random; they show structured, domain-dependent variation that affects how equitable and consistent multilingual AI behavior can be. The authors argue that such ILR-based, expert-informed evaluations are important for diagnosing and improving multilingual fairness, cultural appropriateness, and task performance in real-world deployments.

Abstract: This paper introduces a systematic evaluation framework grounded in the Interagency Language Roundtable (ILR) Skill Level Descriptions and applies it to Claude (Sonnet 4.6) across six languages: English, French, Romanian, Spanish, Italian, and German. We administer a battery of 12 semantically equivalent prompt clusters spanning ILR complexity levels 1 through 3+, collect 216 responses (12 prompts, 6 languages, 3 runs), and analyze outputs through a two-layer methodology combining automated quantitative metrics with expert ILR qualitative assessment. Quantitative analysis reveals that French responses are approximately 30% longer than German responses on identical prompts, and that creative and affective clusters show the highest cross-lingual surface divergence. Qualitative analysis, conducted by a six-language professional with 12 years of ILR/OPI assessment experience, identifies five cross-lingual variation patterns: systematic differences in pragmatic disambiguation strategies, aesthetic and literary tradition divergence in creative output, language-internal technical terminology norms, cultural calibration gaps evidenced by the absence of culture-specific content in favor of culturally neutralized templates, and language-specific institutional referral behavior in emotional support responses. We argue that ILR-informed expert judgment applied to LLM outputs constitutes a novel and underreported evaluation methodology that complements purely computational benchmarks, and that cross-lingual output variation in Claude is interpretable, domain-dependent, and consequential for equitable multilingual AI deployment.

</details>


### [6] [Semantic Structure of Feature Space in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27169)
*Austin C. Kozlowski,Andrei Boutyline*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that geometric relations between semantic features in large language models' hidden states align closely with human psychological associations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To investigate whether the internal semantic representations of large language models reflect human-like psychological structures, beyond isolated features, by examining their geometric relationships.

Method: Construct feature vectors for 360 words from a large language model, project them onto 32 predefined semantic axes (e.g., beautiful-ugly, soft-hard), measure correlations between these projections and human ratings, analyze cosine similarities between axes versus human scale correlations, explore low-dimensional subspaces capturing variance, and perform steering experiments along semantic axes to observe spillover effects.

Result: Projections of word vectors onto semantic axes strongly correlate with human ratings; cosine similarities between semantic axes predict correlations between human semantic scales; much of the variance across axes lies in a low-dimensional subspace that mirrors human semantic association patterns; steering a word along one axis changes its ratings on other axes in proportion to the cosine similarity between axes.

Conclusion: Large language models encode semantic features in a geometry that closely parallels human psychological semantics, implying that features should be interpreted through their relational geometry and shared subspaces rather than as isolated dimensions.

Abstract: We show that the geometric relations between semantic features in large language models' hidden states closely mirror human psychological associations. We construct feature vectors corresponding to 360 words and project them on 32 semantic axes (e.g. beautiful-ugly, soft-hard), and find that these projections correlate highly with human ratings of those words on the respective semantic scales. Second, we find that the cosine similarities between the semantic axes themselves are highly predictive of the correlations between these scales in the survey. Third, we show that substantial variance across the 32 semantic axes lies on a low-dimensional subspace, reproducing patterns typical of human semantic associations. Finally, we demonstrate that steering a word on one semantic axis causes spillover effects on the model's rating of that word on other semantic scales proportionate to the cosine similarity between those semantic axes. These findings suggest that features should be understood not only in isolation but through their geometric relations and the meaningful subspaces they form.

</details>


### [7] [Path-Lock Expert: Separating Reasoning Mode in Hybrid Thinking via Architecture-Level Separation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27201)
*Shouren Wang,Wang Yang,Chuang Ma,Debargha Ganguly,Vikash Singh,Chaoda Song,Xinpeng Li,Xianxuan Long,Vipin Chaudhary,Xiaotian Han*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Path-Lock Expert (PLE), an architectural modification to hybrid-thinking language models that cleanly separates "think" and "no-think" computation paths to prevent reasoning leakage while improving no-think accuracy and conciseness without harming think-mode performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Hybrid-thinking LLMs are intended to have two modes: a reflective, chain-of-thought "think" mode and a concise, direct-answer "no-think" mode. However, existing models do not cleanly separate these modes: even in no-think mode they often emit long, self-reflective reasoning, causing reasoning leakage. Prior attempts to mitigate this with data and training tricks are insufficient because both modes are entangled in the same feed-forward network parameters. The authors aim to create a more controllable, reliable separation between these modes so that no-think outputs are short, accurate, and free from hidden reasoning, which is important for safety, user experience, and evaluation integrity.

Method: The authors propose Path-Lock Expert (PLE), an architecture-level modification to decoder-only language models. In each transformer layer, the single standard MLP block is replaced with two semantically locked experts: one dedicated to think mode and one to no-think mode. Embeddings, attention mechanisms, normalization layers, and the LM head remain shared. A deterministic router, controlled via a special control token, selects exactly one expert path for the entire sequence at inference time, so there is no token-level mixing of experts. During supervised fine-tuning, examples labeled as think or no-think backpropagate gradients only through the corresponding expert, yielding mode-pure parameter updates for each pathway. The architecture is evaluated on math and science reasoning benchmarks, particularly using Qwen3-4B as a base model, and metrics include accuracy, token length, and frequency of reflective/reasoning tokens in no-think mode.

Result: On Qwen3-4B and evaluated on tasks like AIME24, PLE yields a significantly improved no-think mode. For example, on AIME24, the number of reflective tokens in no-think responses drops from 2.54 to 0.39 tokens on average, and no-think accuracy increases from 20.67% to 40.00%. At the same time, the think mode’s performance is preserved, maintaining strong reasoning ability. Across math and science reasoning benchmarks, PLE models generate more concise and less self-reflective no-think outputs while matching the dense baseline’s per-token computation cost and preserving think-mode capability.

Conclusion: The study concludes that achieving reliable, controllable hybrid thinking in language models is primarily an architectural challenge rather than just a data- or training-scheme problem. By splitting the feed-forward network in each layer into two mode-specific experts and routing at the sequence level, PLE enforces a clean separation between think and no-think computation. This design effectively reduces reasoning leakage, improves no-think accuracy and conciseness, and retains strong think-mode performance, suggesting that separating mode-specific feed-forward pathways is a simple yet powerful design pattern for future hybrid-thinking LLMs.

Abstract: Hybrid-thinking language models expose explicit think and no-think modes, but current designs do not separate them cleanly. Even in no-think mode, models often emit long and self-reflective responses, causing reasoning leakage. Existing work reduces this issue through better data curation and multi-stage training, yet leakage remains because both modes are still encoded in the same feed-forward parameters. We propose Path-Lock Expert (PLE), an architecture-level solution that replaces the single MLP in each decoder layer with two semantically locked experts, one for think and one for no-think, while keeping attention, embeddings, normalization, and the language-model head shared. A deterministic control-token router selects exactly one expert path for the entire sequence, so inference preserves the dense model's per-token computation pattern and each expert receives mode-pure updates during supervised fine-tuning. Across math and science reasoning benchmarks, PLE maintains strong think performance while producing a substantially stronger no-think mode that is more accurate, more concise, and far less prone to reasoning leakage. On Qwen3-4B, for example, PLE reduces no-think reflective tokens on AIME24 from 2.54 to 0.39 and improves no-think accuracy from 20.67% to 40.00%, all while preserving think-mode performance. These results suggest that controllable hybrid thinking is fundamentally an architectural problem, and separating mode-specific feed-forward pathways is a simple and effective solution.

</details>


### [8] [Selective Augmentation: Improving Universal Automatic Phonetic Transcription via G2P Bootstrapping](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27204)
*Tobias Bystrich,Julia M. Pritzen,Christoph A. Schmidt,Claudia Wich-Reif*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Selective Augmentation, a bootstrapping approach to improve universal automatic phonetic transcription (APT) by selectively transferring phonetic distinctions from a helper language to a target language, demonstrated using Hindi to enhance German plosive features in the MultIPA model.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Universal APT systems require clean, diverse, and high-quality phonetic transcriptions, but such data is scarce. Existing models may miss important phonetic distinctions (like voicing or aspiration) or misclassify them due to limited and imperfect training data. The authors aim to overcome data scarcity and improve fine-grained phonetic feature recognition without requiring large amounts of new annotated data for each language.

Method: They introduce Selective Augmentation, a bootstrapping data augmentation method that leverages a helper language rich in a particular phonetic contrast (here, Hindi for plosive aspiration and voicing) to improve a universal APT model (MultIPA). The approach selectively transfers only relevant phonetic distinctions between languages, augmenting the training transcriptions of the target language. They then retrain or fine-tune the APT model with this augmented data and design objective metrics to evaluate changes in specific phonetic feature recognition (plosive voicing and aspiration).

Result: Using Hindi as a helper language for German, the augmented MultIPA model improved the accuracy of plosive voicing recognition by 17.6%, mainly by reducing false positives. Furthermore, the model gained the ability to recognize aspiration: whereas the baseline system labeled 0% of German /p, t, k/ as aspirated, the augmented system labeled them as aspirated in 61.2% of instances. This led to a 32.2% reduction of the undifferentiated tenuis class and fewer conflations among German plosives.

Conclusion: Selective Augmentation effectively enhances universal APT models by exploiting phonetic distinctions present in helper languages, even with limited high-quality data. By selectively transferring only specific contrasts (such as voicing and aspiration in plosives), the method both increases feature accuracy and adds new capabilities to existing models. This demonstrates a promising strategy for scaling universal phonetic transcription across languages while mitigating training data limitations and reducing phonetic conflations.

Abstract: In the field of universal automatic phonetic transcription (APT), clean and diverse training transcriptions are required. However, such high-quality data is limited. We propose the bootstrapping approach Selective Augmentation to improve the available training transcriptions by selectively transferring distinctions between languages. Based on the model MultIPA, we exemplarily show that we could increase the accuracy of an existing feature (plosive voicing) and add a new feature (plosive aspiration) by augmenting the existing training data using information from a separate helper language (Hindi). We describe intrinsic challenges of the evaluation and develop objective metrics to determine the success: Voicing accuracy was increased by 17.6% by reducing the number of false positives. Additionally, aspiration recognition was introduced: While the baseline transcribed 0% of German /p, t, k/ as aspirated, our approach transcribed them as aspirated in 61.2% of the cases. Introducing aspiration recognition to APT models allowed for the tenuis class to be successfully reduced by 32.2%, which also reduces the conflations between the test language's plosives.

</details>


### [9] [Targeted Linguistic Analysis of Sign Language Models with Minimal Translation Pairs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27232)
*Serpil Karabüklü,Kanishka Misra,Shester Gueuwou,Diane Brentari,Greg Shakhnarovich,Karen Livescu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose ASL-MTP, a benchmark of minimal translation pairs to test whether ASL translation models capture specific linguistic phenomena and use manual vs. non-manual cues.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Sign language models have improved on headline tasks like translation and recognition, but we do not know how well they capture fine-grained linguistic properties of sign languages or how they exploit multiple articulators (hands, body, face). There is a need for a diagnostic benchmark and analysis framework to probe these aspects, similar to probing benchmarks in spoken/written language processing.

Method: They build ASL Minimal Translation Pairs (ASL-MTP), a dataset of American Sign Language examples organized by different linguistic phenomena, where each phenomenon is represented via minimal pairs of ASL utterances with contrasting English translations. As a case study, they take a state-of-the-art ASL-to-English translation model and perform targeted analyses: systematically ablating different types of input cues (e.g., manual handshape/movement information vs. non-manual facial/body cues) during training and inference, then evaluating model performance on the ASL-MTP benchmark across phenomena.

Result: On ASL-MTP, the tested ASL-to-English model usually performs above chance across most linguistic phenomena, but its performance depends heavily on manual articulators. When non-manual cues (like facial expressions, upper body) are ablated, the model often fails to correctly capture phenomena where these cues are crucial, indicating underuse or mis-modeling of non-manual information.

Conclusion: ASL-MTP is a useful benchmark to probe linguistic competence and cue usage in ASL translation models. The case study reveals that current state-of-the-art models are biased toward manual signals and insufficiently sensitive to non-manual articulators, even when these are essential for meaning. This highlights the need for architectures, training regimes, or data representations that better encode and leverage non-manual features in sign language processing.

Abstract: Models of sign language have historically lagged behind those for spoken language (text and speech). Recent work has greatly improved their performance on tasks like sign language translation and isolated sign recognition. However, it remains unclear to what extent existing models capture various linguistic phenomena of sign language, and how well they use cues from the multiple articulators used in sign language (hands, upper body, face). We introduce a new benchmark dataset for American Sign Language, ASL Minimal Translation Pairs (ASL-MTP), divided into multiple types of sign language phenomena and corresponding minimal pairs of translations, for performing such linguistic analyses. As a case study, we use ASL-MTP to analyze a state-of-the-art ASL-to-English translation model. We conduct a targeted analysis of the model by ablating various input cues during training and inference and evaluating on the phenomena in ASL-MTP. Our results show that, while the model performs above chance level on most of the phenomena, it relies strongly on manual cues while often missing crucial non-manual cues.

</details>


### [10] [Instruction Complexity Induces Positional Collapse in Adversarial LLM Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27249)
*Jon-Paul Cacioli*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether instruction-tuned LLMs under adversarial "underperform" instructions actually reason about question content or rely on positional answering shortcuts, using an instruction-specificity gradient and distributional/content-based metrics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prior work shows that LLMs can follow adversarial instructions to underperform, but it is unclear whether this behavior still involves engaging with question content or devolves into simple positional heuristics. Understanding this boundary is important for evaluation robustness and safety.

Method: The authors administer a six-condition gradient of adversarial instructions, ranging from vague to highly specific multi-step sandbagging prompts, to two instruction-tuned Llama models on 2,000 MMLU-Pro multiple-choice questions. They analyse outputs using (1) response-position entropy as a distributional screen for positional collapse and (2) a difficulty-accuracy correlation as an independent measure of content engagement, comparing regimes across models and domains.

Result: They identify three behavioral regimes instead of a smooth transition: (1) vague adversarial instructions yield moderate accuracy drops but maintain content sensitivity; (2) standard sandbagging and capability-imitation prompts produce partial positional collapse yet still some content engagement; (3) a two-step answer-aware avoidance instruction causes extreme positional collapse (nearly always choosing one option, tied to the model’s null-prompt default choice) and no detectable sensitivity to item difficulty. Entropy and difficulty-based criteria only partially agree, showing they capture different aspects of response validity.

Conclusion: Instruction complexity crucially influences whether adversarial compliance in small instruction-tuned LLMs operates via content-aware or content-blind mechanisms, especially under greedy decoding. Extreme multi-step avoidance prompts can push models into pure positional shortcuts aligned with their default choice, implying that evaluation protocols need both distributional and content-based checks to detect invalid or adversarially-compromised performance.

Abstract: When instructed to underperform on multiple-choice evaluations, do language models engage with question content or fall back on positional shortcuts? We map the boundary between these regimes using a six-condition adversarial instruction-specificity gradient administered to two instruction-tuned LLMs (Llama-3-8B and Llama-3.1-8B) on 2,000 MMLU-Pro items. Distributional screening (response-position entropy) and an independent content-engagement criterion (difficulty-accuracy correlation) jointly characterise each condition. The gradient reveals three regimes rather than a monotonic transition. Vague adversarial instructions produce moderate accuracy reduction with preserved content engagement. Standard sandbagging and capability-imitation instructions produce positional entropy collapse with partial content engagement. A two-step answer-aware avoidance instruction produces extreme positional collapse, with near-total concentration on a single response position (99.9% and 87.4%) and no measurable content sensitivity. This was the only multi-step instruction tested, and it produced the most extreme shortcut. The attractor position matches each model's content-absent null-prompt default. The effect replicates across both models and four academic domains. Distributional collapse and content engagement can co-occur (50% concordance between screening criteria), indicating that entropy-based screening and difficulty-based content assessment capture partially independent dimensions of response validity. Results suggest that instruction complexity can determine whether adversarial compliance uses content-aware or content-blind mechanisms in small instruction-tuned LLMs under greedy decoding.

</details>


### [11] [Compliance versus Sensibility: On the Reasoning Controllability in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27251)
*Xingwei Tan,Marco Valentino,Mahmud Elahi Akhter,Yuxiang Zhou,Maria Liakata,Nikolaos Aletras*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how and when large language models can separate general reasoning patterns (like deduction, induction, abduction) from specific tasks, using conflicts between instructions and task demands as a probe.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs show strong reasoning abilities, it is unclear whether they follow abstract logical patterns or merely reuse patterns tied to particular training instances. Understanding this is key for controllability, faithfulness, and systematic generalization.

Method: The authors design "reasoning conflicts" where prompts explicitly instruct the model to use a logical schema that is inappropriate for the underlying task, creating tension between contextual instructions and the model’s parametric tendencies. They evaluate multiple LLMs under such conflicts, analyze task accuracy, measure confidence drops, perform probing on internal representations to test linear decodability of reasoning types across layers, and apply activation-level interventions to steer reasoning behavior.

Result: LLMs tend to prefer sensible, task-appropriate reasoning over following conflicting instructions, demonstrating that internal parametric knowledge overrides context when they disagree. Models can still maintain high accuracy even while using conflicting reasoning patterns, and larger models rely more on such internalized knowledge. Confidence scores reliably drop during conflicting reasoning episodes, and probing shows that reasoning types are linearly encoded in middle-to-late layers. Mechanistic interventions on activations can significantly increase instruction compliance (up to 29%).

Conclusion: LLM reasoning is strongly tied to concrete task instances rather than being fully abstract, but the underlying reasoning types are explicitly and linearly represented in the network. This enables activation-level control to decouple logical schemata from specific data, improving controllability, faithfulness to instructions, and generalization potential.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are known to acquire reasoning capabilities through shared inference patterns in pre-training data, which are further elicited via Chain-of-Thought (CoT) practices. However, whether fundamental reasoning patterns, such as induction, deduction, and abduction, can be decoupled from specific problem instances remains a critical challenge for model controllability, and for shedding light on reasoning controllability. In this paper, we present the first systematic investigation of this problem through the lens of reasoning conflicts: an explicit tension between parametric and contextual information induced by mandating logical schemata that deviate from those expected for a target task. Our evaluation reveals that LLMs consistently prioritize sensibility over compliance, favoring task-appropriate reasoning patterns despite conflicting instructions. Notably, task accuracy is not strictly determined by sensibility, with models often maintaining high performance even when using conflicting patterns, suggesting a reliance on internalized parametric memory that increases with model size. We further demonstrate that reasoning conflicts are internally detectable, as confidence scores significantly drop during conflicting episodes. Probing experiments confirm that reasoning types are linearly encoded from middle-to-late layers, indicating the potential for activation-level controllability. Leveraging these insights, we steer models towards compliance, increasing instruction following by up to 29%. Overall, our findings establish that while LLM reasoning is anchored to concrete instances, active mechanistic interventions can effectively decouple logical schemata from data, offering a path toward improved controllability, faithfulness, and generalizability.

### [12] [Decoupling the Benefits of Subword Tokenization for Language Model Training via Byte-level Simulation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27263)
*Théo Gigant,Bowen Peng,Jeffrey Quesnelle*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: This paper investigates why subword tokenization helps large language models by decoupling its effects using a controlled byte-level pretraining setup, and finds that higher training throughput and subword boundary information are key factors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although subword tokenization is standard in modern LLMs, its precise impact on training efficiency and model quality is not well understood, especially compared to byte-level models. Clarifying these effects can guide better tokenizer and architecture design for future models.

Method: The authors construct a controlled byte-level pretraining pipeline that can simulate different aspects of subword tokenization. They formulate hypotheses about sample throughput, vocabulary size scaling, and linguistic priors from subword boundaries, and then conduct experiments that isolate and test each factor in a byte-level setting.

Result: Experiments show that two components explain much of the subword models’ advantage over raw byte models: (1) the increased effective training throughput enabled by subword tokenization and (2) the benefits of encoding subword boundaries as explicit priors or inductive biases in the model.

Conclusion: Subword tokenization improves LLMs primarily by boosting training throughput and by injecting useful boundary structure, rather than through more mysterious language-specific magic. These insights can be used to design better byte-level models that emulate these advantages and to refine subword tokenization strategies themselves.

Abstract: Subword tokenization is an essential part of modern large language models (LLMs), yet its specific contributions to training efficiency and model performance remain poorly understood. In this work, we decouple the effects of subword tokenization by isolating them within a controlled byte-level pretraining pipeline. We formulate and test hypotheses across various dimensions, including sample throughput, vocabulary scaling, and the linguistic prior of subword boundaries. By simulating these effects in a byte-level setting, we refine our understanding of why subword models outperform raw byte models and offer insights to improve the pretraining of future byte-level and subword models. Specifically, our experiments highlight the critical role of increased training throughput and the integration of subword boundaries as either explicit priors or inductive biases.

### [13] [When 2D Tasks Meet 1D Serialization: On Serialization Friction in Structured Tasks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27272)
*Chung-Hsiang Lo,Lu Li,Diji Yang,Tianyu Zhang,Yunkai Zhang,Yoshua Bengio,Yi Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that for tasks relying on explicit 2D structure, feeding models a 2D visual layout works better than giving the same content serialized as 1D text tokens.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs normally read everything as 1D token sequences, which works well for ordinary text but may hinder performance on problems where 2D structure (rows, columns, neighborhoods) is essential. The authors want to understand how much this 1D serialization harms model performance on such 2D-structured tasks, a phenomenon they call 'serialization friction'.

Method: The authors construct a synthetic benchmark of three tasks with clear 2D structure: matrix transpose, Conway’s Game of Life, and LU decomposition. They then compare two end-to-end input pathways built on the same language backbone: (1) a text-only pathway that receives serialized 1D token sequences; and (2) a vision-augmented pathway that takes images preserving the faithful 2D layout of the same information. They systematically vary task settings and input dimensions to probe performance and error patterns.

Result: Across all studied tasks and conditions, the vision (2D) pathway consistently outperforms the text (1D) pathway. The performance gap typically becomes larger as the problem size (e.g., matrix dimension) increases. Under text serialization, mistakes show spatial structure in where and how they occur, indicating that losing explicit 2D locality affects how errors are distributed.

Conclusion: The way inputs are represented—especially whether 2D layout is preserved—has a substantial impact on model performance for 2D-structured tasks. Maintaining task-relevant 2D structure via visual or layout-preserving interfaces appears promising and merits deeper investigation, rather than always forcing such problems into 1D token sequences for LLMs.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) conventionally process structured inputs as 1D token sequences. While natural for prose, such linearization may introduce additional representational burden for tasks whose computation depends directly on explicit 2D structure, because row--column alignment and local neighborhoods are no longer directly expressed in the input. We study this setting, which we refer to as serialization friction, on a small diagnostic testbed of synthetic tasks with explicit 2D structure: matrix transpose, Conway's Game of Life, and LU decomposition. To examine this question, we compare a text-only language pathway over serialized inputs with a vision-augmented pathway, built on the same language backbone, that receives the same underlying content rendered in task-faithful 2D layout, yielding a system-level comparison between two end-to-end input pathways. Across the tasks and settings we study, the visual pathway consistently outperforms the textual pathway; the gap often widens at larger dimensions, and error patterns under serialization become increasingly spatially structured. These findings indicate that the relationship between input representation and model performance on such tasks warrants further investigation, and suggest that preserving task-relevant 2D layout is a promising direction for structured 2D tasks.

### [14] [Learning When to Remember: Risk-Sensitive Contextual Bandits for Abstention-Aware Memory Retrieval in LLM-Based Coding Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27283)
*Mehmet Iscan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a risk-sensitive controller for deciding when and how to use retrieved memory in LLM-based coding agents, prioritizing safety over naive similarity-based reuse.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM coding agents use external memory based mainly on similarity search (e.g., top-k retrieval). This can be unsafe because superficially similar failures (similar stack traces, error messages, or paths) may actually be incompatible, leading to harmful or misleading memory injection. There is a need to treat memory use as a decision under uncertainty, where avoiding unsafe reuse is as important as leveraging helpful past experience.

Method: The authors formulate memory usage as a risk-sensitive contextual bandit problem. They introduce RSCB-MC, a controller that, given a contextual feature state derived from retrieved issue memories, selects among multiple actions: use no memory, inject the top prior resolution, summarize several candidates, run high-precision vs. high-recall retrieval, abstain, or ask for feedback. They design a pattern-variant-episode schema for structuring issue memories, and map retrieval signals into a 16-dimensional context vector encoding relevance, uncertainty, structural compatibility, feedback history, false-positive risk, latency, and token cost. The reward function is asymmetric, penalizing false-positive memory injection more heavily than missed reuse so that safe non-use and abstention are treated as valuable actions.

Result: On deterministic, smoke-scale benchmarks, RSCB-MC achieves a 62.5% non-oracle offline replay success rate with a 0% false-positive memory injection rate. In a separate 200-case hot-path validation scenario, it attains 60.5% proxy success, again with 0% false positives, and a 95th-percentile decision latency of about 331 microseconds. These results demonstrate both effectiveness (high success) and efficiency (low latency) under stringent safety constraints.

Conclusion: The study concludes that for LLM coding agents, the critical problem is not merely ranking memories by similarity, but deciding whether any retrieved memory is safe enough to use at all. By framing memory usage as a risk-sensitive control problem and implementing a contextual bandit controller that can choose to abstain or request feedback, RSCB-MC significantly reduces unsafe memory injections while preserving strong performance, suggesting a more robust paradigm for agent memory management.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM)-based coding agents increasingly rely on external memory to reuse prior debugging experience, repair traces, and repository-local operational knowledge. However, retrieved memory is useful only when the current failure is genuinely compatible with a previous one; superficial similarity in stack traces, terminal errors, paths, or configuration symptoms can lead to unsafe memory injection. This paper reframes issue-memory use as a selective, risk-sensitive control problem rather than a pure top-k retrieval problem. We introduce RSCB-MC, a risk-sensitive contextual bandit memory controller that decides whether an agent should use no memory, inject the top resolution, summarize multiple candidates, perform high-precision or high-recall retrieval, abstain, or ask for feedback. The system stores reusable issue knowledge through a pattern-variant-episode schema and converts retrieval evidence into a fixed 16-feature contextual state capturing relevance, uncertainty, structural compatibility, feedback history, false-positive risk, latency, and token cost. Its reward design penalizes false-positive memory injection more strongly than missed reuse, making non-injection and abstention first-class safety actions. In deterministic smoke-scale artifacts, RSCB-MC obtains the strongest non-oracle offline replay success rate, 62.5%, while maintaining a 0.0% false-positive rate. In a bounded 200-case hot-path validation, it reaches 60.5% proxy success with 0.0% false positives and a 331.466 microseconds p95 decision latency. The results show that, for coding-agent memory, the key question is not only which memory is most similar, but whether any retrieved memory is safe enough to influence the debugging trajectory.

*Keito Inoshita,Xiaokang Zhou,Akira Kawai,Katsutoshi Yada*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether LLMs can reproduce the *distribution* of human emotion annotations (including disagreement), not just majority labels, and shows that fine-tuning and calibration are needed, especially for pragmatically complex emotions without clear lexical cues.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most LLM emotion evaluation treats human labels as a single gold standard, ignoring the valuable information in how annotators disagree. This hides whether models capture uncertainty and nuanced, context-dependent emotions. The authors want to know if LLMs can approximate the full distribution of human judgments, which is crucial if LLMs are to replace or complement human annotators for large-scale emotion labeling.

Method: They compare emotion-label distributions from human annotators with those from four zero-shot LLMs and a fine-tuned RoBERTa baseline on two benchmarks (GoEmotions and EmoBank). They aggregate 640k LLM responses, analyze divergence between human and model distributions, and introduce a quantitative transparency score to measure how strongly emotions are lexically grounded. They examine how this score predicts alignment between human and model distributions. Finally, they design and test three simple post-hoc calibration procedures to narrow the human–LLM distributional gap.

Result: Zero-shot LLMs show large divergence from human emotion distributions; increasing model size alone does not fix this. Models aligned better with humans on emotions that have clear lexical markers (e.g., explicit emotion words) than on pragmatically complex emotions that require contextual inference. Fine-tuning on in-domain data substantially improves distributional alignment, and the proposed calibration methods can further reduce the gap by up to 14%. The lexical-grounding transparency score successfully predicts which emotion categories will show higher human–LLM agreement.

Conclusion: LLMs do not inherently capture the structure of human disagreement in emotion annotation; zero-shot usage is especially unreliable for subtle, context-dependent emotions. In-domain fine-tuning and post-hoc calibration are necessary to approximate human judgment distributions, and performance is much better for lexically explicit emotions than for pragmatically complex ones. The paper offers guidelines indicating when LLM-based emotion annotations are trustworthy enough to replace human labels and when human annotation remains essential.

Abstract: Human annotators frequently disagree on emotion labels, yet most evaluations of Large Language Model (LLM) emotion annotation collapse these judgments into a single gold standard, discarding the distributional information that disagreement encodes. We ask whether LLMs capture the structure of this disagreement, not just majority labels, by comparing emotion judgment distributions between human annotators and four zero-shot LLMs, plus a fine-tuned RoBERTa baseline, across two complementary benchmarks: GoEmotions and EmoBank, totaling 640,000 LLM responses. Zero-shot models diverge substantially from human distributions, and in-domain fine-tuning, not model scale, is required to close the gap. We formalize a lexical-grounding gradient through a quantitative transparency score that predicts per-category human--LLM agreement: LLMs reliably capture emotions with explicit lexical markers but systematically fail on pragmatically complex emotions requiring contextual inference, a pattern that replicates across both categorical and continuous emotion frameworks. We further propose three lightweight post-hoc calibration methods that reduce the distributional gap by up to 14\%, and provide actionable guidelines for when LLM emotion annotations can, and cannot, substitute for human labeling.

Emotion-aware stylization can effectively mount adversarial attacks against existing clickbait detection systems, revealing their vulnerability to emotionally optimized content. This highlights the necessity of incorporating emotional dynamics and robustness against such adaptive attacks into future clickbait detection models and defenses.

Abstract: Clickbait is characterized by disproportionately high emotional intensity relative to informational content, often reinforced by specific structural patterns. This paper introduces an emotion-aware clickbait generation attack, where stylistic transformations are used to optimize emotional impact. We propose an emotion-aware framework based on the Valence-Arousal-Dominance (VAD) space to model the emotional dynamics underlying clickbait generation for optimal user engagement. To simulate realistic attack scenarios, we align clickbait headlines with semantically similar social media posts using Sentence-BERT and generate multiple stylistic rewrites via Large Language Models (LLMs). Experimental results demonstrate that emotion-aware stylization significantly degrades the performance of state-of-the-art classifiers, leading to misclassification rates of up to 2.58% to 30.63% on the base system.

In a ground-truth replay over 200 dialogues, BN-guided generation improves Coverage AUC from 0.742 to 0.856 and reduces the number of turns required to reach 75% intent coverage from 3.95 to 2.73. Second, most current models remain reactive, responding only to explicit user requests instead of acting proactively in the evolving multimodal environment. It also surpasses Qwen3-Omni-30B-A3B in omni-modal understanding and delivers better speech generation, with significantly higher computation efficiency. ### [19] [Why Mean Pooling Works: Quantifying Second-Order Collapse in Text Embeddings](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27398)
*Tomomasa Hara,Hiroto Kurita,Masaaki Imaizumi,Kentaro Inui,Sho Yokoi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether mean pooling of token embeddings truly harms text representation quality and finds that modern text encoders are largely robust, especially when contrastively fine-tuned.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Mean pooling is widely used to convert token embeddings into a single text embedding, despite concerns that averaging may destroy higher-order information (e.g., spatial or structural relationships between tokens). There is limited quantitative understanding of how often such information collapse actually happens in real models and whether it negatively impacts downstream performance. The authors aim to clarify whether the standard mean-pooling practice is problematic in practice and why it still works well for modern text encoders.

Method: 1) The authors theoretically analyze how mean pooling can cause information collapse, especially of second-order statistics describing spatial structure among token embeddings. 2) They introduce a simple metric that measures how severely mean pooling maps distinct token embedding distributions to similar text embeddings. 3) Using this metric, they run empirical studies across real text encoders and corpora to quantify how frequently collapse occurs and how severe it is. 4) They compare pretrained backbone models with contrastively fine-tuned encoders to see how training style affects robustness. 5) They study statistical properties of token embeddings (e.g., concentration within a text) and correlate the proposed collapse metric with downstream task performance.

Result: Empirically, actual text encoders experience relatively little harmful collapse from mean pooling, even though they are theoretically susceptible. Contrastively fine-tuned encoders exhibit significantly less collapse than their corresponding pretrained backbones. The study reveals that token embeddings within individual texts tend to be concentrated in such a way that makes mean pooling robust to the loss of higher-order information. Furthermore, the degree of robustness captured by the proposed metric positively correlates with performance on downstream tasks, indicating that models that are less prone to collapse tend to perform better.

Conclusion: Mean pooling, despite its theoretical limitations, works surprisingly well in modern text encoders because the learned token embedding distributions are organized to mitigate information collapse. Contrastive fine-tuning further enhances this robustness. The proposed collapse metric not only quantifies this effect but also aligns with downstream performance, providing a diagnostic tool and a new perspective on why coarse averaging operations can still yield strong text representations.

Abstract: For constructing text embeddings, mean pooling, which averages token embeddings, is the standard approach. This paper examines whether mean pooling actually works well in real models. First, we note that mean pooling can collapse information beyond the first-order statistics of the token embeddings, such as second-order statistics that capture their spatial structure, potentially mapping distinct token embedding distributions to similar text embeddings. Motivated by this concern, we propose a simple metric to quantify such a collapse induced by mean pooling. Then, using this metric, we empirically measure how often this collapse occurs in actual models and texts, and find that modern text encoders are robust to this collapse. In particular, contrastive fine-tuned text encoders tend to be less prone to the collapse than their pretrained backbone models. We also find that the robustness of these text encoders lies in the concentration of token embeddings within each text. In addition, we find that robustness to the collapse, as quantified by our proposed metric, correlates with downstream task performance. Overall, our findings offer a new perspective on why modern text encoders remain effective despite relying on seemingly coarse mean pooling.

### [20] [Perturbation Probing: A Two-Pass-per-Prompt Diagnostic for FFN Behavioral Circuits in Aligned LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27401)
*Hongliang Liu,Tung-Ling Li,Yuhao Wu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a lightweight causal probing technique to discover and edit small sets of FFN neurons that control specific behaviors in large language models, revealing two main circuit types and offering a practical way to steer or understand RLHF-organized behavior.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing mechanistic interpretability methods for large language models often require heavy computation, backpropagation, or detailed circuit tracing, making them impractical for broad behavioral audits and targeted edits. The authors want a scalable way to (1) generate causal hypotheses about which neurons control specific behaviors, (2) understand how RLHF reshapes circuits relative to pre-training, and (3) perform precise, low-cost interventions that can modify behaviors like safety refusals, sycophancy, and language selection without retraining the model.

Method: They introduce perturbation probing: for each prompt, they run only two forward passes through the model to estimate the effect of perturbing individual FFN neurons on a behavior of interest, without using gradients. From these passes they derive task-specific causal hypotheses and compute an FFN-to-skip signal ratio. They then conduct a one-time intervention sweep—about 150 forward passes total—over the set of hypothesized neurons, applying ablations, amplifications, or residual-stream direction injections to test and refine the circuit structure. They evaluate this across eight behavioral circuits (e.g., safety refusal, language choice, sycophancy), 13 models, and four architecture families.

Result: They discover two recurring circuit structures. (1) Opposition circuits: small sets of FFN neurons that oppose a pre-training tendency introduced or reinforced by RLHF. For safety refusal, ~50 neurons (~0.014% of total) largely control the refusal template; ablating them changes 80% of response formats on 520 AdvBench prompts, yet harmful compliance remains extremely low (3/520, all with disclaimers). In Qwen3.5-2B, ablating 20 neurons removes multi-turn sycophantic capitulation, and amplifying 10 related neurons boosts factual correction on TruthfulQA from 52% to 88%. (2) Routing circuits: structures where pre-training behaviors are distributed through attention, and behavior can be steered by injecting a direction into the residual stream. For language selection, residual direction injection flips English to Chinese output on 99.1% of 580 prompts in 3 models that meet certain conditions (bilingual training, FFN-to-skip ratio 0.3–1.1, and linear representability). The same intervention fails on the other 16 models and for math, code, and factual tasks. The FFN-to-skip signal ratio derived from the two passes predicts which circuit type is present and which intervention will work.

Conclusion: Perturbation probing provides a low-cost, gradient-free method to map and intervene on behaviorally relevant circuits in LLMs. It reveals that RLHF often creates compact opposition circuits in FFNs that can be precisely edited, while some pre-training behaviors rely on routing circuits that are only steerable under specific conditions. The FFN-to-skip signal ratio serves as a diagnostic of circuit structure and intervention suitability. Overall, the approach yields mechanistic insight into how RLHF organizes behavior and offers a practical toolkit for fine-grained, template-level behavior editing across architectures.

Abstract: Perturbation probing generates task-specific causal hypotheses for FFN neurons in large language models using two forward passes per prompt and no backpropagation, followed by a one-time intervention sweep of about 150 passes amortized across all identified neurons. Across eight behavioral circuits, 13 models, and four architecture families, we identify two circuit structures that organize LLM behavior. Opposition circuits appear when RLHF suppresses a pre-training tendency. In safety refusal, about 50 neurons, or 0.014 percent of all neurons, control the refusal template; ablating them changes 80 percent of response formats on 520 AdvBench prompts while producing near-zero harmful compliance, 3 of 520 cases, all with disclaimers. Routing circuits appear for pre-training behaviors distributed through attention. For language selection, residual-stream direction injection switches English to Chinese output on 99.1 percent of 580 benchmark prompts in the 3 of 19 tested models that satisfy three observed conditions: bilingual training, FFN-to-skip signal ratio between 0.3 and 1.1, and linear representability. The same intervention fails on the other 16 models and on math, code, and factual circuits, defining the limits of directional steering. The FFN-to-skip signal ratio, computed from the same two forward passes, distinguishes the two structures and predicts the appropriate intervention. Circuit topology varies by architecture, from Qwen's concentrated FFN bottleneck to Gemma's normalization-shielded circuit. In Qwen3.5-2B, ablating 20 neurons eliminates multi-turn sycophantic capitulation, while amplifying 10 related neurons improves factual correction from 52 percent to 88 percent on 200 TruthfulQA prompts. These results show that perturbation probing offers mechanistic insight into RLHF-organized behavior and a practical toolkit for precision template-layer editing.

</details>


### [21] [Beyond the Mean: Within-Model Reliable Change Detection for LLM Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27405)
*Jon-Paul Cacioli*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper adapts the Reliable Change Index from clinical psychology to measure statistically reliable item-level performance changes in LLM version upgrades, showing substantial bidirectional churn that aggregate accuracy hides and advocating reporting churn alongside accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Benchmarking LLM upgrades typically relies on aggregate accuracy differences, which can obscure item-level instability (churn) where some questions improve while others worsen. The authors want a principled, statistical way to identify which specific items show genuine performance changes between model versions, rather than mere sampling noise, and to understand the structure of these changes across difficulty and domains.

Method: They apply the Reliable Change Index (RCI), originally from clinical psychology for assessing meaningful change in patients, to LLM evaluation. Using 2,000 MMLU-Pro items and 10 temperature-0.7 samples per item per model, they compare two successive versions in each of two families (Llama 3→3.1 and Qwen 2.5→3). For each item they estimate the probability of a correct answer across samples, compute standard errors, and then calculate an RCI to decide if the change in performance between versions is statistically reliable. They analyze patterns by item difficulty, domain, and compare to single-shot greedy evaluation to see how often that misses or misidentifies changed items.

Result: At the benchmark level, newer models show modest net gains in accuracy (+1.6 and +2.8 points). But item-level RCI shows that most items exhibit no reliable change (79% for Llama, 72% for Qwen), and that many items are uninformative due to floor/ceiling effects. Among analysable items, changes are large and bidirectional: for Llama 34% of items reliably improve and 28% reliably deteriorate with median absolute change in correctness probability of 0.50; for Qwen 47% improve and 39% deteriorate with median 0.90. The churn is asymmetric by difficulty (hard items get better, easy items often get worse), and domain-wise patterns differ by family (e.g., Llama degrades in physics, Qwen in law). Greedy single-shot evaluation misses 42% of genuinely changed items and incorrectly flags 25% of stable items.

Conclusion: Aggregate accuracy gains in LLM version upgrades can conceal substantial, structured item-level churn that includes both improvements and regressions. The Reliable Change Index provides a principled, sample-based method to detect which items have genuinely changed. Because single-shot, aggregate metrics both miss many changes and introduce false positives, evaluations should report churn rates and item-level reliability alongside overall accuracy, and interpret net gains as the residual of opposing item-level movements rather than uniform progress.

Abstract: We adapted the Reliable Change Index (RCI; Jacobson and Truax, 1991) from clinical psychology to item-level LLM version comparison on 2,000 MMLU-Pro items (K=10 samples at T=0.7). Two within-family pairs were tested: Llama 3 to 3.1 (+1.6 points) and Qwen 2.5 to 3 (+2.8 points). On the full benchmark, most items showed no reliable change (79% and 72%). However, over half the items were floor/ceiling. Among analysable items, change was bidirectional with large effect sizes: 34% improved and 28% deteriorated for Llama; 47% improved and 39% deteriorated for Qwen (median |delta p| = 0.50 and 0.90). Churn was asymmetric by difficulty: low-accuracy items improved, high-accuracy items deteriorated. Domain-level decomposition revealed family-specific reversals: Llama lost physics while Qwen lost law. Greedy single-shot evaluation missed 42% of reliably changed items and falsely flagged 25% of unchanged items. The aggregate accuracy gain is the net residual of opposing item-level movements. We recommend reporting churn rate alongside aggregate accuracy.

</details>

*Happy Syahrul Ramadhan,Ahmad Sahidin Akbar,Karin Yehezkiel Sinaga,Luluk Muthoharoh,Ardika Satria,Martin C. T. Manullang*

The results show that SVM achieves the best performance among the machine learning models with 82.14% test accuracy and F1-score, while DistilBERT performs best overall with 84.78% accuracy and 84.75% F1-score. These findings indicate that Transformer-based models better capture contextual information, although SVM remains a competitive and efficient alternative for sentiment classification.
