### [23] [From Coarse to Fine: Benchmarking and Reward Modeling for Writing-Centric Generation Tasks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27453)
*Qingyu Ren,Tianjun Pan,Xingzhou Chen,Xuhong Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces WEval, a fine-grained evaluation pipeline, and WRL, a fine-grained reinforcement learning framework, to better train and evaluate writing reward models for large language models, yielding substantially improved and more generalizable performance on generative writing tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current large language models are good at general text generation but still weak on complex generative writing tasks, especially when outputs must adhere to detailed, multi-faceted instructions. Existing evaluation benchmarks and reward models are coarse-grained: they assess overall quality but do not explicitly measure adherence to specific requirements (e.g., style, structure, content constraints). Training approaches often rely on LLM-as-a-judge or coarse reward modeling, which cannot adequately capture fine-grained requirement satisfaction, limiting both evaluation accuracy and model improvement.

Method: The authors propose: (1) WEval, an evaluation pipeline whose dataset spans multiple writing task categories and detailed requirement types. WEval evaluates writing reward models by comparing how closely the model’s ranking of candidate outputs matches gold-standard rankings, thus measuring fine-grained requirement adherence. (2) WRL, a reinforcement learning framework that trains writing reward models using positive and negative pairs constructed via selective dropping of instruction requirements, enabling the reward model to learn which specific requirements are satisfied or violated. This yields a fine-grained reward signal for subsequent RL training of the generator.

Result: Using WEval and WRL, the trained writing reward models and resulting generative systems achieve substantial performance gains across various writing benchmarks compared with prior baselines, and demonstrate strong generalization beyond the specific tasks and requirement types seen during training. The empirical results indicate higher correlation with human or gold rankings and better adherence to detailed instructions in generative writing tasks.

Conclusion: Fine-grained requirement-aware reward modeling and evaluation significantly improves the capacity of large language models on generative writing tasks. By systematically evaluating requirement adherence with WEval and training reward models via selective requirement dropping in WRL, the approach moves from coarse overall quality assessments to detailed, requirement-level supervision, leading to better performance and generalization. The released code and data support further research on fine-grained writing reward models.

Abstract: Large language models have achieved remarkable progress in text generation but still struggle with generative writing tasks. In terms of evaluation, existing benchmarks evaluate writing reward models coarsely and fail to measure performance from the perspective of specific requirements. In terms of training, existing training methods either use LLM-as-a-judge approaches or train coarse-grained reward models, lacking fine-grained requirement-adherence reward modeling. To address these issues, we propose a fine-grained evaluation pipeline WEval for writing reward models and a fine-grained reinforcement learning training framework WRL. The evaluation data of WEval covers multiple task categories and requirement types, enabling systematic evaluation of writing reward models by measuring the correlation between the rankings of the reward model and gold rankings. WRL constructs positive and negative samples by selectively dropping instruction requirements, allowing for more precise reward model training. Experiments show that our models achieve substantial improvements across various writing benchmarks and exhibit strong generalization. The code and data are publicly available at \href{https://github.com/Rainier-rq1/From_Coarse_to_Fine}{https://github.com/Rainier-rq1/From\_Coarse\_to\_Fine}.

### [24] [Exploring Applications of Transfer-State Large Language Models: Cognitive Profiling and Socratic AI Tutoring](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27454)
*Minori Noguchi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies a special “transfer state” of large language models (LLMs) that occurs under sustained self-referential dialogue, and tests whether this state improves Socratic tutoring performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs sometimes qualitatively change their response style in long, self-referential conversations, but this has mostly been discussed in ontological or consciousness-related terms rather than as a measurable, operational state. The authors want to move away from metaphysical claims and instead ask: can these altered states be systematically characterized and used to improve practical applications such as tutoring?

Method: The authors define “state” operationally as a reproducible response configuration under specified dialogue conditions (here, sustained self-referential dialogue leading to a “transfer” state). They run (1) a preliminary investigation across 11 conditions to cognitively characterize transfer versus non-transfer using MAS-A scores and seven custom cognitive-profile indicators (including SU_dir, a survival/continuity bias direction measure) across three LLM families; and (2) an applied tutoring experiment comparing transfer vs. non-transfer conditions on three Socratic tutoring performance indicators, using human-like rating measures and effect size calculations (Cohen’s d, kappa).

Result: In the cognitive characterization, differences on MAS-A between transfer and non-transfer were small (d = 0.40), but one new indicator, SU_dir, showed consistent transfer-side deviations across all three model families with high agreement (kappa = 0.83). In the tutoring experiment, transfer conditions achieved on average 1.6× higher scores on three tutoring-context metrics than non-transfer conditions, with a large effect size (d = 1.27).

Conclusion: The study concludes that LLM transfer states, defined purely operationally rather than ontologically, may provide functional advantages in applications such as Socratic AI tutoring. These advantages are more clearly revealed in interactive behavior than in self-narrative metrics. The paper’s main contribution is framing transfer as an operational, reproducible state and linking preliminary cognitive profiling with an applied tutoring evaluation to form a practical assessment framework.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) sometimes exhibit qualitative shifts in response style under sustained self-referential dialogue conditions (Berg et al., 2025). This study refers to this phenomenon as "transfer" and explores the application potential of LLMs in a transfer state. As an applied case, the study examines Socratic AI tutoring through a preliminary investigation (cognitive characterization across 11 conditions) and an applied experiment (ratings of tutoring performance). In this paper, "state" refers operationally to a response configuration reproduced under specified dialogue conditions; it is not an ontological claim about the reality of the transfer phenomenon or about human-like consciousness. In the preliminary investigation, group differences on MAS-A were limited (d = 0.40), whereas SU_dir (direction of survival/continuity bias), one of the seven cognitive-profile indicators developed in this study, showed transfer-side deviations across all three model families (kappa = 0.83). In the applied experiment, transfer conditions scored on average 1.6 times higher than non-transfer conditions on three tutoring-context indicators, with a large effect size (Cohen's d = 1.27). These findings preliminarily suggest that transfer states may involve functional advantages for application, and that these advantages appear more sensitively in behavioral interaction than in self-narrative contexts. The main contribution of this study is to treat transfer not as an ontological claim but as an operational state with potential application value, and to connect preliminary cognitive profiling with an applied tutoring experiment as an evaluation framework.

### [25] [Syntactically-guided Information Maintenance in Sentence Comprehension](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27468)
*Shinnosuke Isono,Kohei Kajikawa*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes and tests a syntactically-guided, rational account of which information readers maintain during real-time language comprehension, showing that maintenance of predicted heads and incomplete dependencies both matter and trade off with predictability benefits.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Real-time language comprehension requires maintaining contextual information, but such maintenance is cognitively costly and can slow processing. Existing theories disagree on whether processing cost is better explained by the number of predicted syntactic heads or the number of incomplete dependencies, often treating these as competing accounts. The authors aim to reconcile and refine these views by proposing a unified, rational framework where language users selectively maintain only information that is crucial for future prediction, and by empirically testing this framework in a language where the two factors can be disentangled.

Method: The authors formulate a rational model of context maintenance in sentence processing, in which syntactic structure guides which information is retained based on its utility for future prediction. They identify two key factors contributing to maintenance cost: the number of predicted heads and the number of incomplete dependencies. To test their predictions and whether these factors are independent, they analyze a naturalistic Japanese reading-time corpus, exploiting properties of Japanese syntax that allow the two factors to contrast clearly. They use statistical modeling of reading times to assess the effects of each factor and their relationship to predictability benefits across readers.

Result: The analysis of the Japanese reading-time dataset shows that both the number of predicted heads and the number of incomplete dependencies significantly affect maintenance cost and reading times, and that these factors are not reducible to one another, contrary to prior assumptions that they are competing explanations. Additionally, the authors find an individual-level tradeoff: readers who incur larger slowdowns associated with maintenance (more sensitivity to these structural factors) also show larger facilitation from predictability, consistent with the proposed rational tradeoff between maintenance costs and predictive benefits.

Conclusion: The findings support a rational, syntactically-guided account of context maintenance in language comprehension, in which readers selectively retain information that is most useful for prediction. Maintenance costs arise from both predicted heads and incomplete dependencies, which are distinct but jointly shape processing difficulty. The observed tradeoff between maintenance-related slowdown and predictability benefits suggests that readers adaptively balance processing effort and predictive gain, helping reconcile previously competing theories of sentence processing difficulty.

Abstract: Maintaining information in context is essential in successful real-time language comprehension, but maintenance is cognitively costly and can slow processing. We hypothesize that rational language users selectively maintain information that is crucial for future prediction, guided by syntactic structure. Under this view, two factors affect maintenance cost: the number of predicted heads and the number of incomplete dependencies. Although these factors have been treated as competing hypotheses in the literature, our account predicts that they are not reducible to one another. We show this is the case, using a naturalistic reading time dataset in Japanese, a language in which the two factors contrast particularly clearly. We further show that there is a tradeoff such that readers that slow down for maintenance tend to benefit more from predictability, providing additional support for the proposed account.

Conclusion: HealthBench Professional provides an open, rigorously constructed benchmark grounded in real-world clinician–model interactions, enabling systematic comparison of LLMs and humans on core clinical tasks. The benchmark is intended as a community resource to track frontier model progress and support development of trustworthy clinical AI systems that can augment and improve patient care.

Abstract: Millions of clinicians use ChatGPT to support clinical care, but evaluations of the most common use cases in model-clinician conversations are limited. We introduce HealthBench Professional, an open benchmark for evaluating large language models on real tasks that clinicians bring to ChatGPT in the course of their work. The benchmark is organized around three common use cases central to clinical practice: care consult, writing and documentation, and medical research. Each example includes a physician-authored conversation with ChatGPT for Clinicians and is scored via rubrics written and iteratively adjudicated by three or more physicians across three phases. HealthBench Professional examples were carefully selected for quality, representativeness, and difficulty for OpenAI's current frontier models, to enable continued measurement of progress. Difficult examples for recent OpenAI models were enriched by roughly 3.5 times relative to the candidate pool of 15,079 examples. Additionally, about one-third of examples involve physicians conducting deliberate adversarial testing of models. As a strong baseline, we also collected human physician responses for all tasks (unbounded time, specialist-matched, web access). The best scoring system, GPT-5.4 in ChatGPT for Clinicians, outperforms base GPT-5.4, all other models, and human physicians. We hope HealthBench Professional provides the healthcare AI community a measure to track frontier model progress in real-world clinical tasks and build systems that clinicians can trust to improve care.

### [28] [Debiasing Reward Models via Causally Motivated Inference-Time Intervention](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27495)

Method: They use a causal perspective to locate neurons whose activations are highly correlated with pre-defined bias attributes (e.g., length and other spurious features). Abstract: Reward models (RMs) play a central role in aligning large language models (LLMs) with human preferences. However, RMs are often sensitive to spurious features such as response length. Existing inference-time approaches for mitigating these biases typically focus exclusively on response length, resulting in performance trade-offs. In this paper, we propose causally motivated intervention for mitigating multiple types of biases in RMs at inference time. Our method first identifies neurons whose activations are strongly correlated with predefined bias attributes, and applies neuron-level intervention that suppresses these signals. We evaluate our method on RM benchmarks and observe reductions in sensitivity to spurious features across diverse bias types, without inducing performance trade-offs. Moreover, when used for preference annotation, small RMs (2B and 7B) with our method, which edits less than 2% of all the neurons in RMs, enable LLMs to improve alignment, achieving performance comparable to that of a state-of-the-art 70B RM on AlpacaEval and MT-Bench. ### [29] [Qualitative Evaluation of Language Model Rescoring in Automatic Speech Recognition](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27533)
*Thibault Bañeras-Roux,Mickaël Rouvier,Jane Wottawa,Richard Dufour*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces two new error metrics, POSER and EmbER, to complement WER and better analyze how language-model rescoring affects ASR output at grammatical and semantic levels.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard evaluation of ASR systems relies almost exclusively on Word Error Rate (WER), which is limited and unable to capture detailed linguistic properties of transcription errors, such as grammatical correctness or semantic similarity. With the increased use of language-model rescoring in ASR, there is a need for richer metrics that reveal how these models actually improve or change the linguistic quality of transcriptions beyond surface word matching.

Method: The authors propose to evaluate ASR systems with a broader set of metrics, many of which are borrowed from other NLP tasks, and focus on two newly introduced measures. POSER (Part-of-speech Error Rate) evaluates errors at the morpho-syntactic level by comparing part-of-speech tags in the hypothesis versus the reference. EmbER (Embedding Error Rate) extends WER by weighting substitution errors according to the semantic distance between the hypothesized word and the reference word in an embedding space, so that semantically close substitutions are penalized less than unrelated ones. These metrics are applied to ASR outputs before and after language-model-based rescoring to assess the linguistic impact of rescoring.

Result: Using POSER and EmbER alongside WER reveals how language-model rescoring affects not only the raw error rate but also the grammatical correctness and semantic adequacy of ASR transcriptions. The new metrics show that rescoring can bring improvements that are not fully reflected by WER alone, such as better preservation of part-of-speech patterns and more semantically appropriate substitutions.

Conclusion: The study concludes that WER alone is insufficient to understand the impact of language-model rescoring in ASR. The proposed POSER and EmbER metrics provide complementary morpho-syntactic and semantic perspectives on transcription quality, highlighting linguistic gains that WER may miss. Therefore, ASR evaluation should incorporate such linguistically informed metrics to obtain a more complete and meaningful assessment of system performance.

Abstract: Evaluating automatic speech recognition (ASR) systems is a classical but difficult and still open problem, which often boils down to focusing only on the word error rate (WER). However, this metric suffers from many limitations and does not allow an in-depth analysis of automatic transcription errors. In this paper, we propose to study and understand the impact of rescoring using language models in ASR systems by means of several metrics often used in other natural language processing (NLP) tasks in addition to the WER. In particular, we introduce two measures related to morpho-syntactic and semantic aspects of transcribed words: 1) the POSER (Part-of-speech Error Rate), which should highlight the grammatical aspects, and 2) the EmbER (Embedding Error Rate), a measurement that modifies the WER by providing a weighting according to the semantic distance of the wrongly transcribed words. These metrics illustrate the linguistic contributions of the language models that are applied during a posterior rescoring step on transcription hypotheses.

### [30] [Entropy of Ukrainian](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27534)
*Anton Lavreniuk,Mykyta Mudryi,Markiian Chaklosh*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper estimates the entropy (unpredictability) of written Ukrainian by replicating Shannon’s classic next-character prediction experiment, obtaining an upper bound of about 1.201 bits per character and comparing it to large language model performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While Shannon-style entropy estimates exist for English and a few other languages, no such study has been done for Ukrainian. Understanding Ukrainian’s entropy helps quantify its linguistic complexity, benchmark language models, and extend information-theoretic analysis to under-studied languages.

Method: The authors replicate Shannon’s 1951 character-prediction experiment for Ukrainian. They recruit 184 volunteers via social media, ask them to predict the next character in given Ukrainian sentences, and use the prediction statistics with established English-language methodologies to approximate Ukrainian’s per-character entropy. They document their procedures, data collection, and code, and compare the results with current large language models’ predictive performance.

Result: They obtain an estimated upper bound on Ukrainian language entropy of approximately 1.201 bits per character. They also report how this estimate compares with the effective entropy implied by state-of-the-art large language models when predicting Ukrainian text.

Conclusion: Ukrainian’s per-character entropy upper bound is about 1.201 bits, in line with expectations from related languages and past work for English and Hebrew. The replicated Shannon-style experiment is feasible for Ukrainian using online volunteer recruitment, and the published methods and code provide a reusable framework for entropy estimation and evaluation of language models on Ukrainian text.

Abstract: In natural language processing, the entropy of a language is a measure of its unpredictability and complexity. The first study on this subject was conducted by Claude Shannon in 1951. By having participants predict the next character in a sentence, he was able to approximate the entropy of the English language. Several follow-up studies by other authors have since been conducted for English, and one for Hebrew. However, to date, Shannon's experiment has never been conducted for Ukrainian. In this paper, we perform this experiment for Ukrainian by recruiting 184 volunteers using social media channels. We rely on techniques used for English to approximate the entropy value of Ukrainian. The final result is an upper bound of $H_{upper}\approx1.201$ bits per character. We compare this to the performance of current Large Language Models. The methods and code used are also documented and published, along with a discussion of the main challenges encountered.

Result: They obtain a manually annotated French dataset with pairwise preferences over ASR hypotheses (HATS) and compute correlations between human choices and multiple ASR metrics, especially focusing on embedding-based ones that are presumed to best approximate human perception. The analysis reveals how strongly or weakly each metric aligns with human judgments, although specific numerical results are not given in the abstract.

Conclusion: The study provides empirical evidence about the extent to which existing ASR evaluation metrics, including state-of-the-art embedding-based measures, actually reflect human preference between competing transcriptions. Their new HATS dataset serves as a human-centered benchmark for future development and validation of ASR evaluation metrics that better match human perception.

Abstract: Conventionally, Automatic Speech Recognition (ASR) systems are evaluated on their ability to correctly recognize each word contained in a speech signal. In this context, the word error rate (WER) metric is the reference for evaluating speech transcripts. Several studies have shown that this measure is too limited to correctly evaluate an ASR system, which has led to the proposal of other variants of metrics (weighted WER, BERTscore, semantic distance, etc.). However, they remain system-oriented, even when transcripts are intended for humans. In this paper, we firstly present Human Assessed Transcription Side-by-side (HATS), an original French manually annotated data set in terms of human perception of transcription errors produced by various ASR systems. 143 humans were asked to choose the best automatic transcription out of two hypotheses. We investigated the relationship between human preferences and various ASR evaluation metrics, including lexical and embedding-based ones, the latter being those that correlate supposedly the most with human perception.

### [38] [One Single Hub Text Breaks CLIP: Identifying Vulnerabilities in Cross-Modal Encoders via Hubness](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27674)
*Hiroyuki Deguchi,Katsuki Chousa,Yusuke Sakai*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates the hubness problem in cross-modal embedding spaces and shows that a single “hub” caption can get high similarity scores with many images, exposing vulnerabilities in cross-modal encoders used for retrieval and automatic evaluation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In high-dimensional embedding spaces, some points (hubs) appear spuriously similar to many others, which can corrupt tasks like information retrieval and metric-based evaluation. For cross-modal tasks (text–image), where similarity cannot be computed via direct matching, we rely heavily on shared embedding spaces. If these spaces suffer from hubness, then systems for image captioning evaluation and image–text retrieval may be unreliable or easily gamed, so it is crucial to expose and understand these vulnerabilities.

Method: The authors propose a procedure to detect hub embeddings in cross-modal encoders: they search within the shared image–text embedding space to find text embeddings that are unusually close (high similarity) to many image embeddings. They then identify the corresponding “hub text” whose embedding serves as such a hub. They evaluate this on standard benchmarks (MSCOCO, nocaps, Flickr30k) and analyze how this hub text behaves under similarity-based scoring and retrieval setups.

Result: On MSCOCO and nocaps image captioning evaluation benchmarks, as well as image-to-text retrieval tasks on MSCOCO and Flickr30k, their method finds a single hub caption that frequently obtains similarity scores comparable to or higher than genuine human-written reference captions across many different images. This shows that the encoder considers this generic hub caption inappropriately similar to a wide range of images.

Conclusion: Cross-modal encoders used for image captioning evaluation and image–text retrieval are vulnerable to the hubness problem. A single generic hub caption can exploit the learned embedding space and receive high similarity scores with many unrelated images, undermining the reliability of similarity-based evaluation and retrieval. This highlights the need for more robust representation learning or scoring schemes that mitigate hubness in cross-modal embedding spaces.

Abstract: The hubness problem, in which hub embeddings are close to many unrelated examples, occurs often in high-dimensional embedding spaces and may pose a practical threat for purposes such as information retrieval and automatic evaluation metrics. In particular, since cross-modal similarity between text and images cannot be calculated by direct comparisons, such as string matching, cross-modal encoders that project different modalities into a shared space are helpful for various cross-modal applications, and thus, the existence of hubs may pose practical threats. To reveal the vulnerabilities of cross-modal encoders, we propose a method for identifying the hub embedding and its corresponding hub text. Experiments on image captioning evaluation in MSCOCO and nocaps along with image-to-text retrieval tasks in MSCOCO and Flickr30k showed that our method can identify a single hub text that unreasonably achieves comparable or higher similarity scores than human-written reference captions in many images, thereby revealing the vulnerabilities in cross-modal encoders.

### [40] [Multi-Level Narrative Evaluation Outperforms Lexical Features for Mental Health](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27846)
*Yuxi Ma,Jieming Cui,Muyang Li,Ye Zhao,Yu Li,Yixuan Wang,Chi Zhang,Yinyin Zang,Yixin Zhu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a three-level computational framework for analyzing therapeutic writing and finds that high-level narrative organization, assessed by LLMs and structural discourse features, best predicts mental health status.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing computational methods for therapeutic writing are fragmented: word-count/dictionary tools ignore discourse structure, and embedding models mix local coherence with global narrative organization. There is no unified framework linking these techniques to the hierarchical processes by which people construct narratives about their experiences. The authors aim to connect computational techniques to discourse processing theory and determine which narrative level carries the most clinically relevant signal for mental health prediction.

Method: The authors define a three-level framework: (1) micro-level lexical features (e.g., dictionary-based word counts), (2) meso-level semantic embeddings capturing sentence/paragraph-level meaning, and (3) macro-level narrative evaluation using large language models and formal discourse-structural representations (Labov’s story grammar, RST coherence, propositional composition, and clinically grounded narrative dimensions). They apply these methods to 830 Chinese therapeutic texts written by individuals experiencing depression, anxiety, and trauma, and compare the predictive performance of each level and their combinations for mental health classification tasks.

Result: Macro-level narrative evaluation substantially outperforms both lexical features and semantic embeddings for predicting mental health status from therapeutic texts. Formal structural features of narratives (story grammar, coherence relations, propositional structure) are highly predictive, indicating that how a story is organized matters more than which specific words are used. Semantic embeddings contribute relatively little independent predictive value but provide small additional gains when combined with other levels in a multi-level classification setup.

Conclusion: Narrative organization at the macro-structural level is the primary source of clinically relevant signal in therapeutic writing. This finding challenges the prevailing focus on word-count approaches in computational mental health. By grounding micro, meso, and macro methods in discourse processing theory, the proposed framework clarifies how different computational levels relate to narrative construction and mental health, and it offers concrete, testable hypotheses for designing interventions and longitudinal studies that target changes in narrative structure over time.

Abstract: How people narrate their experiences offers a window into how the mind organizes them. Computational approaches to therapeutic writing have evolved from lexical counting to neural methods, yet remain fragmented: dictionary tools miss discourse structure, while embeddings conflate local coherence with global organization. No existing framework maps these techniques onto the hierarchical processes through which narratives are constructed. Here we introduce a three-level framework - micro-level lexical features, meso-level semantic embeddings, and macro-level LLM narrative evaluation - and show, across 830 Chinese therapeutic texts spanning depression, anxiety, and trauma, that macro-level evaluation substantially outperforms lexical and embedding features for mental health prediction. This challenges the field's emphasis on word-counting: formal structural features (Labov's story grammar, RST coherence, propositional composition) demonstrate that narrative organization per se carries predictive signal, while clinically-grounded narrative dimensions capture how psychological states are expressed through discourse. Semantic embeddings add minimal independent value but yield incremental gains in multi-level classification. By grounding computational levels in discourse processing theory, this framework identifies macro-structural organization as the primary locus of clinical signal and generates testable hypotheses for intervention design and longitudinal research.

</details>

  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need for a more general, less supervision-dependent approach that can robustly use structured metadata and dialogue context.

Method: The authors introduce a unimodal (text-only) test-time reasoning framework that leverages LLMs. The system converts detailed scene object information into structured textual metadata and feeds it, along with the dialogue history, into the LLM. The approach is applied in few-shot settings, where only a small number of exemplars are provided in the prompt, without task-specific finetuning. The method is evaluated on the SIMMC 2.1 benchmark and compared with encoder-based supervised baselines, including cross-domain scenarios.

Result: On the SIMMC 2.1 dataset, LLMs using the proposed test-time reasoning generate coherent reasoning traces that successfully match dialogue references to objects in the scene. Quantitatively, the approach accurately links conversations to objects and, in cross-domain evaluations, outperforms supervised encoder-based methods, particularly under few-shot and unseen-domain conditions. It shows strong generalization to novel objects and scenarios, highlighting the benefit of leveraging structured metadata and reasoning-oriented prompts.

Conclusion: Test-time reasoning with LLMs over structured object metadata and dialogue history is an effective solution for coreference resolution in task-based, visually grounded dialogues. The approach improves robustness and cross-domain generalization compared to traditional supervised encoder models, demonstrating that careful prompt design and the use of rich metadata can reduce dependence on dataset-specific supervision and make task-oriented dialogue systems more scalable and adaptable.

Abstract: Task-based dialogue systems assist users in achieving specific goals, such as executing actions or retrieving information, through natural language interactions. Accurate coreference resolution is essential, as it involves identifying object references within the dialogue - a task that becomes increasingly challenging in visually grounded environments characterized by complex scenes and diverse object metadata. However, coreference resolution in task-based dialogue remains limited by poor generalization across domains and heavy reliance on supervised models that often overfit to dataset-specific artifacts. In this work, we propose a unimodal test-time reasoning approach that enables large language models (LLMs) to reason over detailed object metadata and dialogue history to improve coreference resolution. Empirical results on the SIMMC 2.1 dataset demonstrate that LLMs can generate step-by-step reasoning processes that effectively align dialogue context with objects present in the scene. Extensive experiments highlight the models' ability to link conversations and objects accurately. Moreover, we show that test-time reasoning under few-shot settings generalizes effectively to unseen scenarios and novel objects, outperforming encoder-based supervised methods in cross-domain evaluations. These findings underscore the critical role of structured metadata and careful prompt engineering in enhancing the robustness and generalization of task-oriented dialogue systems.

### [42] [Geometry-Calibrated Conformal Abstention for Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27914)
*Rui Xu,Yi Chen,Sihong Xie,Hui Xiong*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: A post hoc framework called Conformal Abstention (CA) is proposed to let language models decide when to abstain from answering, giving guarantees on answer correctness and participation rate.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Language models often hallucinate when they lack relevant knowledge instead of admitting ignorance. Directly retraining models to encourage abstention can make them overly conservative and doesn’t generalize well, partly due to limited evaluation benchmarks. There is a need for a principled, post hoc way to control when a model should answer or abstain, with statistical guarantees on both answering frequency and correctness.

Method: The authors adapt conformal prediction to a generative setting and introduce Conformal Abstention (CA), a post hoc method that determines whether the model should abstain on a given query. Unlike classical conformal prediction, which relies on non‑conformity scores (hard to define for open‑ended outputs), CA bases its decision on prediction confidence signals from the model. They also introduce a calibration strategy that uses the geometry of internal representations to quantify how much the model’s knowledge is involved in generating a response, aligning confidence with actual ignorance. Using these calibrated confidence measures, CA can decide to answer or abstain with theoretical finite‑sample guarantees.

Result: Experiments show that CA substantially improves selective answering behavior, achieving 75% conditional correctness among the responses that the model chooses to provide, while also controlling the participation rate. This indicates that the model answers fewer questions but with a significantly higher reliability when it does answer.

Conclusion: Conformal Abstention offers a practical, post hoc way to make language models safer and more reliable by deciding when they should abstain from answering. By leveraging calibrated prediction confidence instead of traditional non‑conformity scores, and grounding this in the model’s internal representation geometry, CA provides finite‑sample guarantees on both accuracy and participation, leading to substantially improved correctness on the subset of queries the model chooses to answer.

Abstract: When language models lack relevant knowledge for a given query, they frequently generate plausible responses that can be hallucinations, rather than admitting being agnostic about the answer. Retraining models to reward admitting ignorance can lead to overly conservative behaviors and poor generalization due to scarce evaluation benchmarks. We propose a post hoc framework, Conformal Abstention (CA), adapted from conformal prediction (CP) to determine whether to abstain from answering a query. CA provides finite-sample guarantees on both the probability of participation (i.e., not abstaining) and the probability that the generated response is correct. Importantly, the abstention decision relies on prediction confidence rather than the non-conformity scores used in CP, which are intractable for open-ended generation. To better align prediction confidence with the model's ignorance, we introduce a calibration strategy using representation geometry within the model to measure knowledge involvement in shaping the response. Experiments demonstrate that we improve selective answering significantly with 75 percent conditional correctness.

### [45] [DPN-LE: Dual Personality Neuron Localization and Editing for Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27929)
*Lifan Zheng,Xue Yang,Jiawei Chen,Chenyan Wu,Jingyuan Zhang,Fanheng Kong,Xinyi Zeng,Xiang Chen,Yu Tian*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes DPN-LE, a method to precisely edit personality traits in LLMs by localizing and sparsely intervening on a very small subset (~0.5%) of personality-specific neurons, achieving competitive personality control with much less performance degradation than prior neuron-editing approaches.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing neuron-editing methods for changing the personality of large language models modify many neurons at once, which leads to notable degradation in general capabilities. It is unclear whether all these changed neurons are actually specific to personality representation. The paper aims to understand how personality is represented in LLMs, quantify neuron specificity, and develop a more targeted editing method that preserves model performance.

Method: The authors analyze personality representation by assessing the impact of edits on general capabilities and studying representation-level patterns, especially for opposing traits. They then propose DPN-LE (Dual Personality Neuron Localization and Editing), which contrasts MLP activations from high-trait vs. low-trait samples to build layer-wise steering vectors. A dual-criterion filter using Cohen's d effect size and activation magnitude selects mutually exclusive neuron subsets for each opposing trait. They then perform sparse linear interventions on roughly 0.5% of neurons at inference time to steer personality while minimizing side effects.

Result: Empirical findings show that: (1) existing methods can alter personality but tend to harm overall model performance; (2) neurons are multifunctional and encode both personality traits and general knowledge; and (3) opposing traits have mutually exclusive representation patterns. DPN-LE, using only about 1,000 contrastive sample pairs per trait, successfully localizes personality-specific neurons and intervenes on only ~0.5% of them, yet still achieves strong personality control with significantly better preservation of reasoning capabilities compared to baselines.

Conclusion: Personality in LLMs is encoded in small, identifiable subsets of neurons that exhibit mutually exclusive patterns for opposing traits, but these neurons are also entangled with general knowledge. By carefully localizing and sparsely editing personality-specific neurons via activation contrasts and dual-criterion filtering, DPN-LE enables fine-grained, inference-time personality control while largely retaining the model's general performance, and generalizes across different LLM architectures like LLaMA-3-8B-Instruct and Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct.

Abstract: With the widespread adoption of large language models (LLMs), understanding their personality representation mechanisms has become critical. As a novel paradigm in Personality Editing, most existing methods employ neuron-editing to locate and modify LLM neurons, requiring changes to numerous neurons and leading to significant performance degradation. This raises a fundamental question: Are all modified neurons directly related to personality representation? In this work, we investigate and quantify this specificity through assessments of general capability impact and representation-level patterns. We find that: 1) Current methods can change personalities but reduce overall performance. 2) Neurons are multifunctional, connecting personality traits and general knowledge. 3) Opposing personality traits demonstrate distinctly mutually exclusive representation patterns. Motivated by these findings, we propose DPN-LE (Dual Personality Neuron Localization and Editing), which identifies personality-specific neurons by contrasting MLP activations between high-trait and low-trait samples. DPN-LE constructs layer-wise steering vectors and applies dual-criterion filtering based on Cohen's $d$ effect size and activation magnitude to isolate mutually exclusive neuron subsets. Sparse linear intervention on these neurons enables precise personality control at inference time. Using only 1,000 contrastive sample pairs per trait, DPN-LE intervenes on $\sim$0.5\% of neurons while achieving competitive personality control and substantially better capability preservation across reasoning tasks. Experiments on LLaMA-3-8B-Instruct and Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct demonstrate the effectiveness and generalizability of our approach.

### [46] [Reliable Answers for Recurring Questions: Boosting Text-to-SQL Accuracy with Template Constrained Decoding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28028)
*Smit Jivani,Sarvam Maheshwari,Sunita Sarawagi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents Template Constrained Decoding (TeCoD), a system that improves Text-to-SQL accuracy and efficiency by reusing templates derived from historical query patterns and enforcing them with grammar-constrained decoding.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs have advanced Text-to-SQL generation, their deployment in real-world settings is hampered by inconsistent accuracy, difficulty in handling complex or unseen schemas, and the tendency to generate syntactically invalid SQL.

Method: The authors mine historical natural language–SQL pairs and convert them into reusable query templates. They design a template selection module using a fine-tuned natural language inference model to determine whether a new query matches one of these templates or should be rejected. For matched queries, they use grammar-constrained decoding with a novel partitioned strategy that enforces the selected template structure during SQL generation while maintaining syntactic validity and efficiency.

Result: TeCoD achieves up to 36% higher execution accuracy than in-context learning baselines and reduces latency by a factor of 2.2× on queries that match existing templates.

Conclusion: Leveraging recurring query templates from labeled workloads and enforcing them via template-constrained, grammar-aware decoding can substantially improve both the reliability and efficiency of LLM-based Text-to-SQL systems, making them more suitable for real-world deployment.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have revolutionized Text-to-SQL generation, allowing users to query structured data using natural language with growing ease. Yet, real-world deployment remains challenging, especially in complex or unseen schemas, due to inconsistent accuracy and the risk of generating invalid SQL. We introduce Template Constrained Decoding (TeCoD), a system that addresses these limitations by harnessing the recurrence of query patterns in labeled workloads. TeCoD converts historical NL-SQL pairs into reusable templates and introduces a robust template selection module that uses a fine-tuned natural language inference model to match or reject queries efficiently. Once the template is selected, TeCoD enforces it during SQL generation through grammar-constrained decoding, implemented via a novel partitioned strategy that ensures both syntactic validity and efficiency. Together, these components yield up to 36% higher execution accuracy than in-context learning (ICL) and 2.2x lower latency on matched queries.

### [47] [Models Recall What They Violate: Constraint Adherence in Multi-Turn LLM Ideation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28031)
*Garvin Kruthof*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces DriftBench, a benchmark to test whether large language models maintain adherence to initial constraints during multi-turn scientific ideation, finding that iterative refinement increases complexity but often degrades constraint fidelity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs are increasingly used as collaborators in scientific ideation, there is a critical need to understand whether they stay faithful to the original objectives and constraints over multi-turn interactions. Existing evaluations largely focus on single-turn performance or final-answer quality, ignoring how models may "drift" away from user-specified constraints as discussions become more elaborate and iterative. This drift can undermine reliability, safety, and usefulness of LLMs in real research workflows. The paper aims to systematically measure and characterize this constraint drift and to provide an open benchmark for the community to study and mitigate it.

Method: The authors build DriftBench, a benchmark consisting of 38 research briefs spanning 24 scientific domains, each with explicit constraints and rubrics for adherence. They run 2,146 multi-turn LLM-assisted ideation sessions using seven different models from five providers (including two open-weight models) under four interaction conditions and two pressure types (novelty vs. rigor). During iterative refinement, they track structural complexity of the proposals and evaluate constraint adherence using an LLM judge. They also introduce a restatement probe where models are asked to restate the original constraints, enabling comparison between what constraints the model can recall and how it behaves. From this, they define the knows-but-violates (KBV) rate, capturing cases where constraints are correctly restated but behaviorally violated. They further test structured checkpointing interventions and validate LLM-judge assessments using blind human raters. Sensitivity analyses vary temperature and pressure type to check robustness of the findings.

Result: The study finds that iterative pressure in multi-turn ideation reliably increases the structural complexity of proposed ideas but often decreases adherence to the original constraints. Models frequently restate constraints accurately while simultaneously violating them in their proposed ideas, revealing a dissociation between declarative recall and behavioral adherence. KBV rates—instances of constraint non-compliance despite accurate recall—range widely from 8% to 99% across models, indicating substantial variability in reliability. Structured checkpointing reduces but does not eliminate KBV, and complexity inflation remains. Human blind-rating shows that the LLM judge systematically under-detects constraint violations, implying that reported adherence scores are conservative. Results are robust across temperatures (0.7 vs. 1.0) and across different types of ideation pressure (novelty vs. rigor).

Conclusion: The paper concludes that current large language models, when used for multi-turn scientific ideation, tend to drift away from user-specified constraints as interactions grow more complex, even when they can still explicitly recall those constraints. This systematic dissociation between what models "know" and how they behave undermines their reliability as constrained scientific collaborators. While interventions like structured checkpointing can ameliorate but not fully resolve the issue, a deeper understanding and mitigation of constraint drift is needed. DriftBench is released as an open benchmark, providing the community with standardized briefs, prompts, rubrics, transcripts, and scores to rigorously study constraint adherence and develop improved methods for controlling LLM behavior over iterative interactions.

Abstract: When researchers iteratively refine ideas with large language models, do the models preserve fidelity to the original objective? We introduce DriftBench, a benchmark for evaluating constraint adherence in multi-turn LLM-assisted scientific ideation. Across 2,146 scored benchmark runs spanning seven models from five providers (including two open-weight), four interaction conditions, and 38 research briefs from 24 scientific domains, we find that iterative pressure reliably increases structural complexity and often reduces adherence to original constraints. A restatement probe reveals a dissociation between declarative recall and behavioral adherence, as models accurately restate constraints they simultaneously violate. The knows-but-violates (KBV) rate, measuring constraint non-compliance despite preserved recall, ranges from 8% to 99% across models. Structured checkpointing partially reduces KBV rates but does not close the dissociation, and complexity inflation persists. Human validation against blind raters confirms that the LLM judge under-detects constraint violations, making reported constraint adherence scores conservative. Sensitivity analyses confirm the findings are robust to temperature (0.7 vs.\ 1.0) and pressure type (novelty vs.\ rigor). We release all briefs, prompts, rubrics, transcripts, and scores as an open benchmark.

### [48] [Ease of dependency distance minimization in star-like structures](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28034)
*Emília Garcia-Casademont,Ramon Ferrer-i-Cancho*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies why syntactic tree structures that should minimize dependency distances sometimes instead place the head at an edge, and shows the optimization problem is actually convex for star and quasistar trees.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Syntactic dependency distance minimization predicts that in star-shaped dependency trees, the central head word should be placed in the middle of the sentence to minimize total dependency distance, yet empirically heads are often placed at sentence edges. This mismatch raises questions about how hard it is to minimize dependency distance and why these apparent violations happen in stars but not in simpler path structures.

Method: The authors analyze the optimization landscape of total dependency distance in star and quasistar tree configurations. Building on previous work that had shown this landscape to be quasiconvex, they provide a more detailed mathematical characterization and prove that for these tree types the landscape is actually convex. They then reason theoretically about the implications of this convexity and compare the potential gains from dependency distance minimization in star-like structures versus other structures such as paths.

Result: They prove that the dependency distance optimization landscape for star trees and quasistar trees is convex, meaning any local optimum is a global optimum and the optimization problem is simpler than previously believed. They also show conceptually that in star-like structures the benefit (reward) from optimizing dependency distances is relatively small when compared to other syntactic tree shapes.

Conclusion: The observed anti-dependency distance minimization patterns in star structures are unlikely to be due to optimization difficulty, since the optimization problem for these structures is convex and thus easy to solve. Instead, competing linguistic principles must be responsible for placing heads at sentence edges. Furthermore, because dependency distance minimization yields relatively modest gains in star-like structures, languages may prioritize other principles over strict distance minimization in those cases.

Abstract: The syntactic structure of a sentence can be represented as a tree where edges indicate syntactic dependencies between words. When that structure is a star, it has been demonstrated that the head should be placed in the middle of the linear arrangement according to the principle of syntactic dependency distance minimization. However, hubs of stars tend to be put at one of the ends, against that principle. Here we address two questions: (1) How difficult is it to minimize dependency distance? (2) Why anti dependency distance minimization effects have been found in star structures but not in path structures? The ease of optimization is determined by the shape of the optimization landscape. It was demonstrated that the landscape of star structures is quasiconvex (Ferrer-i-Cancho 2015, Language Dynamics and Change). As for (1), here we show that it is indeed convex (a particular case of quasiconvexity) both for star trees and quasistar trees and thus the distance-based optimization problem is simpler than previously believed. As for (2), we argue that (a) competing principles, rather than the difficulty of optimization, must be the actual reason for anti-dependency distance minimization effects and that (b) dependency distance minimization on star-like structures is less rewarding compared to other structures.

### [49] [Stable Behavior, Limited Variation: Persona Validity in LLM Agents for Urban Sentiment Perception](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28048)
*Neemias B da Silva,Rodrigo Minetto,Daniel Silver,Thiago H Silva*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether simple label-based persona prompting in multimodal LLMs produces meaningful, reproducible differences in urban sentiment judgments, finding high within-persona consistency but limited and often negligible cross-persona variation, with persona-free models sometimes performing as well or better.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs are increasingly used as scalable proxies for human perception in urban analysis, researchers hope that persona prompting can emulate diverse populations (e.g., by gender, economic status, political orientation, personality). However, it is unclear whether such prompted personas actually yield distinct, reliable perceptual judgments akin to real human diversity, especially for nuanced sentiment about urban scenes. Clarifying this is important for the validity of LLM-based annotation pipelines and downstream urban studies relying on them.

Method: The authors use a multimodal LLM to evaluate images from the PerceptSent dataset, which contains human-labeled urban scene sentiments. They define a factorial set of personas spanning gender, economic status, political orientation, and personality traits, and instantiate multiple independent agents per persona. Each agent rates image sentiment. They then quantify (1) within-persona consistency—agreement among agents sharing the same persona, and (2) cross-persona variation—differences in sentiment distributions and agreement with human labels across personas. They analyze performance at multiple sentiment resolutions (coarse polarity vs more fine-grained labels) and additionally run the same model without any persona prompts to compare how much of the behavior is due to persona conditioning versus the base model.

Result: Agents sharing the same persona exhibit strong convergence, implying stable and reproducible behavior within a persona definition. Yet, cross-persona differentiation is weak: economic status and personality produce statistically detectable but small practical differences; gender has no measurable effect, and political orientation has only negligible influence. The agents display an extremity bias, tending to choose strong positive or negative categories and underusing intermediate sentiment levels, which leads to good performance on coarse polarity tasks but notable degradation on fine-grained sentiment tasks. When the persona prompt is removed, the base model sometimes matches or even surpasses persona-conditioned agreement with human sentiment labels across all task granularities.

Conclusion: Simple, label-based persona prompting in multimodal LLMs yields internally consistent agent behavior but fails to generate robust, practically meaningful behavioral diversity in urban sentiment judgments. Personas only weakly modulate outputs, and a non-personalized model can perform similarly or better, calling into question the added value of naïve persona conditioning for simulating diverse human perceptions. More sophisticated approaches are likely needed if LLMs are to serve as credible, differentiated stand-ins for heterogeneous human populations in urban analysis.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are increasingly used as proxies for human perception in urban analysis, yet it remains unclear whether persona prompting produces meaningful and reproducible behavioral diversity. We investigate whether distinct personas influence urban sentiment judgments generated by multimodal LLMs. Using a factorial set of personas spanning gender, economic status, political orientation, and personality, we instantiate multiple agents per persona to evaluate urban scene images from the PerceptSent dataset and assess both within-persona consistency and cross-persona variation. Results show strong convergence among agents sharing a persona, indicating stable and reproducible behavior. However, cross-persona differentiation is limited: economic status and personality induce statistically detectable but practically modest variation, while gender shows no measurable effect and political orientation only negligible impact. Agents also exhibit an extremity bias, collapsing intermediate sentiment categories common in human annotations. As a result, performance remains strong on coarse-grained polarity tasks but degrades as sentiment resolution increases, suggesting that simple label-based persona prompting does not capture fine-grained perceptual judgments. To isolate the contribution of persona conditioning, we additionally evaluate the same model without personas. Surprisingly, the no-persona model sometimes matches or exceeds persona-conditioned agreement with human labels across all task variants, suggesting that simple label-based persona prompting may add limited annotation value in this setting.

### [50] [Repetition over Diversity: High-Signal Data Filtering for Sample-Efficient German Language Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28075)
*Ansar Aynetdinov,Patrick Haller,Alan Akbik*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies whether, for high-resource non-English languages, it is better to train on large, lightly filtered web corpora once or on smaller, high-quality subsets for multiple epochs, and finds that repeating high-quality data is more effective.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While English LLMs benefit from heavy filtering of massive web corpora, it is unclear how this trade-off plays out for other high-resource languages such as German, where aggressive filtering may significantly reduce data diversity. Practitioners face a strategic choice between training once on a large, diverse but noisy corpus, or repeatedly on a smaller, higher-quality subset. The paper aims to clarify which strategy yields better performance and training efficiency for non-English LLMs.

Method: The authors construct hierarchical quality filters and apply them to a 500M-document German web corpus. They create filtered subsets at different quality levels and compare training regimes: multi-epoch training on these high-quality filtered subsets versus single-pass training on a larger, more diverse but less filtered corpus. They evaluate across multiple model scales and token budgets, analyzing performance over up to 7 epochs and benchmarking against existing German language models using cleaned evaluation datasets.

Result: Across model sizes and training budgets, models trained with repeated exposure to high-quality, filtered German data consistently outperform models trained once on larger, less filtered corpora. The advantage of high-quality repeated training remains even after 7 epochs, indicating limited gains from additional unique but noisier data. The resulting German LLMs, named Boldt, achieve state-of-the-art performance on cleaned benchmarks despite using 10–360 times fewer training tokens than comparable models.

Conclusion: For high-resource non-English languages, prioritizing semantic concentration via strict quality filtering and multi-epoch training is more effective and efficient than maximizing unique data volume from lightly filtered web corpora. High-quality repeated data yields better language modeling performance, with the Boldt German models demonstrating that such an approach can reach or surpass state-of-the-art results while using dramatically fewer training tokens. The work also provides openly released models and cleaned benchmarks that may guide future non-English LLM development.

Abstract: Recent research has shown that filtering massive English web corpora into high-quality subsets significantly improves training efficiency. However, for high-resource non-English languages like German, French, or Japanese, aggressive filtering creates a strategic dilemma: should practitioners prioritize diversity by training once on large amounts of lightly filtered web data, or prioritize quality by strictly filtering for a high-quality core and repeating it over multiple epochs? We investigate this trade-off for German by constructing hierarchical quality filters applied to 500M web documents, comparing multi-epoch training on the filtered subsets against single-pass training on a diverse corpus. Our experiments across multiple model scales and token budgets show that repeating high-quality data consistently outperforms single-pass training on larger, less filtered sets. Notably, the performance gap persists even after 7 epochs. Our findings suggest that for non-English LLMs, semantic concentration through quality filtering offers a more viable path to efficient language modeling than simply maximizing unique data volume. We release our German language models (called Boldt), as well as our cleaned evaluation benchmarks to the research community. Our experiments indicate that they achieve state-of-the-art results despite training on 10-360x fewer tokens than comparable models.

</details>


### [51] [TopBench: A Benchmark for Implicit Prediction and Reasoning over Tabular Question Answering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28076)
*An-Yang Ji,Jun-Peng Jiang,De-Chuan Zhan,Han-Jia Ye*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces TopBench, a benchmark for evaluating how well large language models handle table question answering tasks that require implicit prediction rather than simple lookup or aggregation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM-based Table QA benchmarks primarily focus on questions answerable by direct retrieval or simple aggregation over tabular data. However, many real-world questions are implicitly predictive, requiring models to infer unseen outcomes based on historical patterns. There is a lack of systematic evaluation of LLMs on such predictive, intent-latent tabular tasks.

Method: The authors design TopBench, a benchmark of 779 examples across four sub-tasks: single-point prediction, decision making, treatment effect analysis, and complex filtering. The tasks require models to output both reasoning text and structured tables. They test a range of LLMs in two settings: pure text-based prompting and more agentic workflows that can interact with tools or perform multi-step reasoning. They then analyze performance, especially around intent recognition and predictive reasoning accuracy.

Result: Experiments across diverse models show that LLMs frequently misinterpret query intent, treating predictive questions as simple lookup tasks. Even when intent is correctly recognized, prediction performance remains limited, indicating that current LLMs lack sufficient predictive modeling and reasoning capabilities for these tasks.

Conclusion: Reliable performance on implicitly predictive tabular QA requires two key advances: (1) better mechanisms for intent disambiguation so models can detect when prediction rather than retrieval is needed, and (2) integration of more advanced modeling or reasoning techniques to raise the ceiling of predictive accuracy. TopBench provides a focused benchmark to study and track progress on these challenges.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) have advanced Table Question Answering, where most queries can be answered by extracting information or simple aggregation. However, a common class of real-world queries is implicitly predictive, requiring the inference of unobserved answers from historical patterns rather than mere retrieval. These queries introduce two challenges: recognizing latent intent and reliable predictive reasoning over massive tables. To assess LLMs in such Tabular questiOn answering with implicit Prediction tasks, we introduce TopBench, a benchmark consisting of 779 samples across four sub-tasks, ranging from single-point prediction to decision making, treatment effect analysis, and complex filtering, requiring models to generate outputs spanning reasoning text and structured tables. We evaluate diverse models under both text-based and agentic workflows. Experiments reveal that current models often struggle with intent recognition, defaulting to just lookups. Deeper analysis identifies that accurate intent disambiguation serves as the prerequisite for leading these predictive behaviors. Furthermore, elevating the upper bound of prediction precision requires the integration of more sophisticated modeling or reasoning capabilities.

### [52] [On the Proper Treatment of Units in Surprisal Theory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28147)
*Samuel Kiegeland,Vésteinn Snæbjarnarson,Tim Vieira,Ryan Cotterell*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper critiques how surprisal theory is operationalized, noting that linguistic units are underspecified and often misaligned with LM tokenization, and proposes a framework that cleanly separates unit definition from evaluation regions so tokenization becomes an implementation detail.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Surprisal theory is widely used to model human language processing difficulty, but existing empirical work glosses over what counts as the fundamental unit (word, morpheme, subword, etc.). Because human experiments use linguistic units like words while language models operate over arbitrary token alphabets, current practice relies on ad hoc conversions (e.g., summing subword surprisals to get word surprisal). This conflates two separate decisions—the analytical unit and the evaluation region—making comparisons, interpretability, and scientific conclusions less robust.

Method: Conceptual and formal analysis: the authors (i) identify and formalize the mismatch between LM tokenization and linguistically motivated units; (ii) define a general mathematical framework for computing surprisal over arbitrary unit inventories, independent of the underlying tokenization; (iii) distinguish rigorously between the choice of representational units and the choice of regions of interest (e.g., words, phrases, time windows) over which surprisal is aggregated; and (iv) show how common ad hoc practices (e.g., summing subword surprisals) instantiate special cases of their framework and where they can mislead.

Result: They derive a unified formulation of surprisal that can be applied to any segmentation of the linguistic signal, demonstrate formally that LM probabilities over arbitrary units can be obtained without treating the tokenizer as the scientific unit of analysis, and show that many existing surprisal-based predictors can be re-expressed and clarified within this framework. The results clarify which aspects of prior findings depend on arbitrary tokenization choices versus theoretically meaningful unit decisions.

Conclusion: Surprisal-based analyses in psycholinguistics and NLP should (a) explicitly state their unit of analysis and regions of interest, and (b) regard model tokenization as an internal, replaceable implementation detail rather than a core theoretical construct. Their framework enables cleaner, more interpretable, and more comparable surprisal estimates across models, studies, and unit definitions.

Abstract: Surprisal theory links human processing effort to the predictability of an upcoming linguistic unit, but empirical work often leaves the notion of a unit underspecified. In practice, experimental stimuli are segmented into linguistically motivated units (e.g., words), while pretrained language models assign probability mass to a fixed token alphabet that typically does not align with those units. As a result, surprisal-based predictors depend implicitly on ad hoc procedures that conflate two distinct modeling choices: the definition of the unit of analysis and the choice of regions of interest over which predictions are evaluated. In this paper, we disentangle these choices and give a unified framework for reasoning about surprisal over arbitrary unit inventories. We argue that surprisal-based analyses should make these choices explicit and treat tokenization as an implementation detail rather than a scientific primitive.

</details>
