### [53] [Compositional Meta-Learning for Mitigating Task Heterogeneity in Physics-Informed Neural Networks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.26999)
*Beomchul Park,Minsu Koh,Heejo Kong,Seong-Whan Lee*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper introduces LAM-PINN, a modular, meta-learning extension of physics-informed neural networks that clusters parameterized PDE tasks and reuses specialized subnetworks to greatly improve accuracy and efficiency on unseen tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard physics-informed neural networks must be retrained from scratch for each new PDE configuration (different parameters or boundary/initial conditions), which is computationally costly. Existing meta-learning approaches try to share knowledge via a single global initialization, but this can cause negative transfer when tasks are heterogeneous, especially when only spatial/temporal coordinates are available as inputs and the number of training tasks is small. There is a need for a principled way to exploit structure across related PDE tasks that accounts for task similarity and allows selective parameter sharing to improve generalization and reduce training cost on unseen configurations.

Method: The authors propose LAM-PINN, a compositional, modular architecture for PINNs. For each PDE task (defined by specific parameters or conditions), they first perform brief transfer sessions to obtain learning-affinity metrics that reflect how well knowledge transfers between tasks. These metrics, combined with the PDE parameters, form a task representation. Using these representations, tasks are clustered even when the primary inputs to the PINN are just coordinates. The network is decomposed into a set of cluster-specialized subnetworks plus a shared meta-network. A routing mechanism learns weights that determine, for each task, how to combine outputs from the shared and specialized modules, instead of relying on a single global initialization. Meta-learning is used to train both the modules and routing such that adaptation to new tasks is efficient and accurate.

Result: On three benchmark families of parameterized PDEs, LAM-PINN significantly outperforms conventional PINNs when generalizing to unseen tasks. Specifically, it achieves on average a 19.7× reduction in mean squared error on new configurations while using only 10% of the training iterations that standard PINNs require. This demonstrates both improved data efficiency (fewer iterations) and higher solution accuracy under the same or lower computational budget. The benchmarks indicate robustness across different PDE types and parameter variations.

Conclusion: LAM-PINN effectively addresses the limitations of traditional and existing meta-learning PINNs by introducing task-aware modularity and routing based on learned task affinities. By clustering tasks and decomposing the model into specialized and shared components, it mitigates negative transfer and exploits structure within parameterized PDE families, even when only coordinate inputs are available. The strong improvements in accuracy and training efficiency on unseen PDE configurations suggest that LAM-PINN is well-suited for resource-constrained engineering applications where rapid and reliable solution of many related PDE instances is required within a bounded design space.

Abstract: Physics-informed neural networks (PINNs) approximate solutions of partial differential equations (PDEs) by embedding physical laws into the loss function. In parameterized PDE families, variations in coefficients or boundary/initial conditions define distinct tasks. This makes training individual PINNs for each task computationally prohibitive, while cross-task transfer can be sensitive to task heterogeneity. While meta-learning can reduce retraining cost, existing methods often rely on a single global initialization and may suffer from negative transfer, particularly under feature-scarce coordinate inputs and limited training-task availability. We propose the Learning-Affinity Adaptive Modular Physics-Informed Neural Network (LAM-PINN), a compositional framework that leverages task-specific learning dynamics. LAM-PINN combines PDE parameters with learning-affinity metrics from brief transfer sessions to construct a task representation and cluster tasks even with coordinate-only inputs. It decomposes the model into cluster-specialized subnetworks and a shared meta network, and learns routing weights to selectively reuse modules instead of relying on a single global initialization. Across three PDE benchmarks, LAM-PINN achieves an average 19.7-fold reduction in mean squared error (MSE) on unseen tasks using only 10% of the training iterations required by conventional PINNs. These results indicate its effectiveness for generalization to unseen configurations within bounded design spaces of parameterized PDE families in resource-constrained engineering settings.

</details>


### [54] [When Your LLM Reaches End-of-Life: A Framework for Confident Model Migration in Production Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27082)
*Emma Casey,David Roberts,David Sim,Ian Beaver*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Framework to safely migrate production LLM systems by statistically aligning automatic metrics with human judgments for robust model comparison with limited human evals.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Enterprises running LLM-based products must periodically replace or upgrade models because of end-of-life, cost, policy, or performance changes. Doing this naively risks quality regressions, while doing extensive human evaluations is expensive and slow. There is a need for a principled, efficient, and reproducible way to compare candidate models and choose replacements with confidence.

Method: They propose a Bayesian statistical calibration framework that links automated evaluation metrics (e.g., automatic scorers, rubric-based signals) to human judgment data. With a limited set of human-labeled examples, they estimate the relationship between automatic scores and true quality, then use Bayesian inference to quantify uncertainty and compare models. They apply this to a real production QA system spanning six global regions, evaluating multiple dimensions: answer correctness, refusal behavior, and stylistic adherence.

Result: On a commercial QA system handling 5.3M monthly interactions, the framework successfully identifies suitable replacement LLM models while using only limited human evaluation. The calibrated automated metrics align well with human preferences, enabling confident decisions about which candidate models meet or exceed current system performance across multiple quality dimensions and regions.

Conclusion: The proposed Bayesian calibration framework offers a broadly applicable methodology for LLM model migration in enterprise settings. It makes evaluation more efficient by leveraging a small amount of human data to calibrate automated metrics, supports multi-dimensional quality assessment, and yields reproducible, statistically grounded comparisons among models—an increasingly important capability as organizations manage diverse, rapidly changing portfolios of LLM-powered services.

Abstract: We present a framework for migrating production Large Language Model (LLM) based systems when the underlying model reaches end-of-life or requires replacement. The key contribution is a Bayesian statistical approach that calibrates automated evaluation metrics against human judgments, enabling confident model comparison even with limited manual evaluation data. We demonstrate this framework on a commercial question-answering system serving 5.3M monthly interactions across six global regions; evaluating correctness, refusal behavior, and stylistic adherence to successfully identify suitable replacement models. The framework is broadly applicable to any enterprise deploying LLM-based products, providing a principled, reproducible methodology for model migration that balances quality assurance with evaluation efficiency. This is a capability increasingly essential as the LLM ecosystem continues to evolve rapidly and organizations manage portfolios of AI-powered services across multiple models, regions, and use cases.

</details>


### [55] [End-to-end autonomous scientific discovery on a real optical platform](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27092)
*Shuxing Yang,Fujia Chen,Rui Zhao,Junyao Wu,Yize Wang,Haiyao Luo,Ning Han,Qiaolu Chen,Yuze Hu,Wenhao Li,Mingzhu Li,Hongsheng Chen,Yihao Yang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Paper presents Qiushi Discovery Engine, an LLM-based autonomous research agent that performs end-to-end scientific discovery on a real optical platform, discovering a new physical mechanism.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM-based agents can assist with research tasks but have not yet shown fully autonomous, end-to-end scientific discovery in real physical systems with nontrivial, experimentally validated findings. The authors aim to push AI from a tool to an autonomous scientific discoverer and to test whether such a system can make genuinely new, experimentally supported physical discoveries.

Method: They design Qiushi Discovery Engine, an LLM-based agentic system with nonlinear research phases, a Meta-Trace memory system, and a dual-layer architecture. The system interacts with a real optical experimental platform, conducts thousands of iterative reasoning, measurement, and revision steps, and maintains research notes and scripts. They test it by having it reproduce a known transmission-matrix experiment on a different setup, translate an abstract coherence-order theory into measurable quantities, and then run an open-ended optical study where the agent proposes hypotheses, designs experiments, runs tools, and interprets data.

Result: Qiushi Engine autonomously reproduces a published transmission-matrix experiment on a non-original platform and converts abstract coherence-order theory into experimentally measurable observables, leading to what they report as the first observation of that coherence-order structure. In an extended open-ended investigation involving 145.9M tokens, over 3k LLM calls, and extensive tool and script usage, the system proposes and experimentally validates an optical bilinear interaction mechanism that is structurally analogous to the core operation in Transformer attention.

Conclusion: The work demonstrates that an LLM-based agentic system can autonomously carry out end-to-end scientific discovery on a real physical platform and uncover a nontrivial, previously unreported physical mechanism backed by experiments. This marks a claimed milestone for autonomous research agents and suggests potential for AI-driven design of novel, high-speed, energy-efficient optical hardware for pairwise computation.

Abstract: Scientific research has long been human-led, driving new knowledge and transformative technologies through the continual revision of questions, methods and claims as evidence accumulates. Although large language model (LLM)-based agents are beginning to move beyond assisting predefined research workflows, none has yet demonstrated end-to-end autonomous discovery in a real physical system that produces a nontrivial result supported by experimental evidence. Here we introduce Qiushi Discovery Engine, an LLM-based agentic system for end-to-end autonomous scientific discovery on a real optical platform. Qiushi Engine combines nonlinear research phases, Meta-Trace memory and a dual-layer architecture to maintain adaptive and stable research trajectories across long-horizon investigations involving thousands of LLM-mediated reasoning, measurement and revision actions. It autonomously reproduces a published transmission-matrix experiment on a non-original platform and converts an abstract coherence-order theory into experimental observables, providing, to our knowledge, the first observation of this class of coherence-order structure. More importantly, in an open-ended study involving 145.9 million tokens, 3,242 LLM calls, 1,242 tool calls, 163 research notes and 44 scripts, Qiushi Engine proposes and experimentally validates optical bilinear interaction, a physical mechanism structurally analogous to a core operation in Transformer attention. This AI-discovered mechanism suggests a route towards high-speed, energy-efficient optical hardware for pairwise computation. To our knowledge, this is the first demonstration of an AI agentic system autonomously identifying and experimentally validating a nontrivial, previously unreported physical mechanism, marking a milestone for research-level autonomous agents.

</details>

  <summary>Details</summary>

This demonstrates the value of tightly integrating intelligent components for automated ML pipeline generation, improving success rate, robustness, and development efficiency.

Abstract: The purpose of our paper is to develop a unified multi-agent architecture that automates end-to-end machine learning (ML) pipeline generation from datasets and natural-language (NL) goals, improving efficiency, robustness and explainability. A five-agent system is proposed to handle profiling, intent parsing, microservice recommendation, Directed Acyclic Graph (DAG) construction and execution. The approach is evaluated on 150 ML tasks across diverse scenarios. The system achieves an 84.7% end-to-end pipeline success rate, outperforming baseline methods. It demonstrates improved robustness through self-healing and reduces workflow development time compared to manual construction. The study introduces a novel integration of code-grounded RAG, explainable recommendation, self-healing execution and adaptive learning within a single architecture, showing that tightly coupled intelligent components can outperform isolated solutions.

### [57] [Unsupervised Electrofacies Classification and Porosity Characterization in the Offshore Keta Basin Using Wireline Logs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27126)
*Hamdiya Adams,Theophilus Ansah-Narh,Daniel Kwadwo Asiedu,Bruce Kofi Banoeng-Yakubo,Marcellin Atemkeng,Thomas Armah,Richmond Opoku-Sarkodie,Rebecca Davis,Ezekiel Nii Noye Nortey*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Unsupervised k-means clustering of wireline logs is used to define electrofacies in a data‑poor offshore basin, providing a reproducible log-only workflow for early-stage reservoir characterization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In the offshore Keta Basin, Ghana, core and detailed geological data are scarce, making conventional, supervised electrofacies analysis difficult. There is a need for objective, reproducible methods that can extract geological facies information from commonly available wireline logs alone, to reduce interpretational bias and support early-stage exploration decisions in frontier basins.

Method: The authors analyse six standard wireline logs from a single well (Well C), covering about 11,195 depth samples. They work in multivariate log space and apply k-means clustering to partition the data into electrofacies. They evaluate different cluster numbers using inertia (within-cluster variance) and silhouette scores to select an appropriate model. Once four clusters are chosen, they examine the depth distribution and log-response characteristics of each cluster to interpret their geological meaning in terms of clay content, porosity, and rock framework properties.

Result: Four electrofacies clusters are identified as the optimal solution, with an average silhouette coefficient around 0.50, indicating moderate but meaningful cluster separation. These electrofacies show coherent, continuous trends with depth and can be related systematically to a continuum from shale-rich intervals to cleaner, sandier units, reflecting variations in clay content, porosity, and rock framework properties.

Conclusion: The study shows that a log-only, unsupervised k-means clustering approach, guided by quantitative quality metrics, can yield geologically meaningful electrofacies even where core data are limited. The workflow is robust, reproducible, and well suited for early-stage formation evaluation in frontier offshore basins, and it can serve as a basis for more integrated, data-rich studies in the future.

Abstract: This study presents an unsupervised machine learning workflow for electrofacies analysis in the offshore Keta Basin, Ghana, where core data are scarce. Six standard wireline logs from Well~C were analysed over a depth interval comprising approximately $11{,}195$ samples. K-means clustering was applied in multivariate log space, with the clustering structure evaluated using inertia and silhouette diagnostics. Four clusters were identified, supported by an average silhouette coefficient of approximately $0.50$, indicating moderate but meaningful separation. The resulting electrofacies exhibit systematic, depth-continuous patterns associated with variations in clay content, porosity, and rock framework properties, forming a geological continuum from shale-dominated to cleaner sandstone-dominated units. The results demonstrate that log-only, unsupervised clustering supported by quantitative metrics provides a robust and reproducible framework for subsurface characterisation. The proposed workflow offers a practical tool for early-stage formation evaluation in frontier offshore basins and a foundation for future integrated studies.

</details>


### [58] [TRUST: A Framework for Decentralized AI Service v.0.1](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27132)
*Yu-Chao Huang,Zhen Tan,Mohan Zhang,Pingzhi Li,Zhuo Zhang,Tianlong Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: TRUST is a decentralized auditing framework that uses hierarchical reasoning graphs, causal interaction modeling, and multi-tier consensus to robustly and transparently verify Large Reasoning Models and multi-agent systems while preserving privacy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Centralized verification of Large Reasoning Models and Multi-Agent Systems in high-stakes domains suffers from robustness issues (single point of failure, bias, attacks), scalability limits (bottlenecks in complex reasoning), opacity (hidden auditing undermines trust), and privacy risks (exposed reasoning traces can leak proprietary models). There is a need for an auditing framework that is transparent, robust to adversaries, scalable, and privacy-preserving, suitable for decentralized environments and on-chain governance.

Method: The paper proposes TRUST, a decentralized framework with three key technical components: (1) Hierarchical Directed Acyclic Graphs (HDAGs) that decompose chain-of-thought reasoning into five abstraction layers, enabling parallel and distributed auditing of reasoning steps; (2) the DAAN protocol, which converts multi-agent interactions into Causal Interaction Graphs (CIGs) to enable deterministic attribution of root causes for system behavior; and (3) a multi-tier consensus mechanism that combines computational checkers, LLM-based evaluators, and human experts in a stake-weighted voting scheme. This consensus mechanism is designed to remain correct even with up to 30% adversarial participants and includes economic incentives encoded via a Safety-Profitability Theorem. All auditing decisions are recorded on-chain, and reasoning is segmented to prevent reconstruction of proprietary logic, providing privacy by design. The framework is instantiated in several applications, including decentralized auditing, tamper-proof leaderboards, trustless data annotation, and governance of autonomous agents.

Result: Empirically, TRUST achieves 72.4% accuracy across multiple LLMs and benchmarks, outperforming baselines by 4–18%, and maintains resilience under 20% corruption (adversarial participation). The DAAN protocol obtains 70% root-cause attribution accuracy, compared with 54–63% for standard methods, while also reducing token usage by 60%. Human evaluation studies confirm the design’s effectiveness, with an F1 score of 0.89 and a Brier score of 0.074, indicating good calibration and reliability. Theoretically, the authors prove a Safety-Profitability Theorem that ensures honest auditors gain profit and malicious actors incur losses under the stake-weighted consensus design, and they show correctness guarantees up to 30% adversarial participants.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that TRUST provides a practical and theoretically grounded approach to decentralized auditing of reasoning-capable AI systems. By combining hierarchical reasoning decomposition, causal modeling of multi-agent interactions, and incentive-aligned multi-tier consensus, it addresses robustness, scalability, transparency, and privacy limitations of centralized approaches. The framework enables new applications such as decentralized auditing, tamper-proof leaderboards, trustless data annotation, and governed autonomous agents, and represents a step toward safe, accountable deployment of Large Reasoning Models and multi-agent systems in high-stakes domains.

Abstract: Large Reasoning Models (LRMs) and Multi-Agent Systems (MAS) in high-stakes domains demand reliable verification, yet centralized approaches suffer four limitations: (1) Robustness, with single points of failure vulnerable to attacks and bias; (2) Scalability, as reasoning complexity creates bottlenecks; (3) Opacity, as hidden auditing erodes trust; and (4) Privacy, as exposed reasoning traces risk model theft. We introduce TRUST (Transparent, Robust, and Unified Services for Trustworthy AI), a decentralized framework with three innovations: (i) Hierarchical Directed Acyclic Graphs (HDAGs) that decompose Chain-of-Thought reasoning into five abstraction levels for parallel distributed auditing; (ii) the DAAN protocol, which projects multi-agent interactions into Causal Interaction Graphs (CIGs) for deterministic root-cause attribution; and (iii) a multi-tier consensus mechanism among computational checkers, LLM evaluators, and human experts with stake-weighted voting that guarantees correctness under 30% adversarial participation. We prove a Safety-Profitability Theorem ensuring honest auditors profit while malicious actors incur losses. All decisions are recorded on-chain, while privacy-by-design segmentation prevents reconstruction of proprietary logic. Across multiple LLMs and benchmarks, TRUST attains 72.4% accuracy (4-18% above baselines) and remains resilient against 20% corruption. DAAN reaches 70% root-cause attribution (vs. 54-63% for standard methods) with 60% token savings. Human studies validate the design (F1 = 0.89, Brier = 0.074). The framework supports (A1) decentralized auditing, (A2) tamper-proof leaderboards, (A3) trustless data annotation, and (A4) governed autonomous agents, pioneering decentralized AI auditing for safe, accountable deployment of reasoning-capable systems.

</details>


### [59] [Unpacking Vibe Coding: Help-Seeking Processes in Student-AI Interactions While Programming](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27134)
*Daiana Rinja,Eduardo Araujo Oliveira,Sonsoles López-Pernas,Mohammed Saqr,Marcus Specht,Kamila Misiejuk*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes how students use generative AI for “vibe coding” in programming courses and finds that high performers use AI for inquiry and exploration, while low performers delegate work, leading to AI mirroring (rather than improving) their learning approaches.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Generative AI is changing how students program, enabling natural language interaction instead of line-by-line coding. Educators lack clarity on whether these new practices support or undermine learning. The authors aim to understand how different patterns of AI help-seeking relate to student performance and what this implies for designing AI that truly supports learning rather than simply doing the work for students.

Method: The authors collected 19,418 interaction turns between generative AI and 110 undergraduate students engaged in vibe coding. They used inductive qualitative coding to categorize forms of help-seeking, and then applied Heterogeneous Transition Network Analysis to examine the sequences and transitions of these interaction types. They compared patterns between top- and low-performing students.

Result: Top-performing students predominantly engaged in instrumental help-seeking behaviors—asking questions, probing, and exploring concepts—which elicited tutor-like, explanatory responses from the AI. Low-performing students tended to exhibit executive help-seeking, delegating tasks and asking the AI for complete solutions, which positioned the AI as an executor rather than a tutor. Overall, generative AI reflected the intent and strategy of the student rather than nudging them toward more productive learning behaviors.

Conclusion: Current generative AI systems in programming education largely mirror student intent, whether productive or passive, instead of actively promoting deeper learning. To shift AI from a passive tool to an active teammate, systems should be designed to detect unproductive delegation and adaptively redirect interactions toward inquiry-driven, cognitively engaging activities. Pedagogically aligned AI design is needed so that student–AI partnerships augment, rather than replace, students’ cognitive effort.

Abstract: Generative AI is reshaping higher education programming through vibe coding, where students collaborate with AI via natural language rather than writing code line-by-line. We conceptualize this practice as help-seeking, analyzing 19,418 interaction turns from 110 undergraduate students. Using inductive coding and Heterogeneous Transition Network Analysis, we examined interaction sequences to compare top- and low-performing students. Results reveal that top performers engaged in instrumental help-seeking -- inquiry and exploration -- eliciting tutor-like AI responses. In contrast, low performers relied on executive help-seeking, frequently delegating tasks and prompting the AI to assume an executor role focused on ready-made solutions. These findings indicate that currently generative AI mirrors student intent (whether productive or passive) rather than optimizing for learning. To evolve from tools to teammates, AI systems must move beyond passive compliance. We argue for pedagogically aligned design that detect unproductive delegation and adaptively steer educational interactions toward inquiry, ensuring student-AI partnerships augment rather than replace cognitive effort.

### [60] [Optimal Stop-Loss and Take-Profit Parameterization for Autonomous Trading Agent Swarm](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27150)
*Nathan Li,Aikins Laryea,Yigit Ihlamur*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how systematically optimizing exit rules—stop-loss, take-profit, and trailing mechanisms—can materially improve the performance of an autonomous crypto trading agent swarm.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most autonomous crypto trading systems focus on entry conditions and treat exits with simple, fixed, and often untested rules. The authors want to investigate whether more thoughtful, data-driven design of exit logic can significantly improve risk-adjusted returns and provide a disciplined framework for doing so.

Method: Using a dataset of over 900 historical trades generated by an existing autonomous trading agent swarm, the authors replay each trade under a wide range of alternative stop-loss, take-profit, and trailing exit configurations. They then compare the resulting performance distributions to the current production exit setup. For evaluation, they first attempt a chronological train-test split but encounter distortions due to an anomalous war-driven market period, and thus additionally use randomized sampling of trades while explicitly noting its limitations.

Result: The experiments show that exit configuration has a substantial impact on performance. Certain exit setups deliver stronger risk-adjusted returns than the production baseline, with patterns generally indicating that tighter stop-losses, earlier profit realization, and closer trailing stops tend to yield better overall outcomes for the tested agent swarm.

Conclusion: Exit logic is a first-class design component in autonomous crypto trading and should be tuned systematically rather than relying on ad-hoc or untested rules. The paper provides a practical, transparent framework for testing and optimizing exit settings, while also highlighting methodological caveats such as the impact of regime-specific episodes and the trade-offs between chronological and randomized evaluation approaches.

Abstract: Autonomous crypto trading systems often spend most of their design effort on finding entries, while exits are left to fixed rules that are rarely tested in a systematic way. This paper examines whether better stop-loss and take-profit settings can improve the performance of an autonomous trading agent swarm. Using more than 900 historical trades, we replay each trade under many alternative exit policies and compare results against the existing production setup. The study finds that exit design matters meaningfully: stronger configurations improve risk-adjusted performance and generally favor tighter loss limits, earlier profit capture, and closer trailing protection. The paper also discusses a key evaluation challenge: a purely chronological split was initially used, but the newest trades fell into an unusual war-driven market period that sharply distorted test results. To reduce the influence of that single episode, the main comparison was run on randomized data, with the drawbacks of doing so acknowledged explicitly. Overall, the paper presents a practical framework for tuning exit logic in a more disciplined and transparent way.

### [61] [Step-level Optimization for Efficient Computer-use Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27151)
*Jinbiao Wei,Kangqi Ni,Yilun Zhao,Guo Gan,Arman Cohan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces an event-driven, step-level cascade framework for computer-use agents that reduces computational cost by using small models by default and escalating to large models only at high-risk steps, detected via learned monitors for being stuck or drifting from the goal.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing computer-use agents rely on large multimodal models at nearly every interaction step with graphical user interfaces, making them slow and expensive. Long-horizon GUI tasks are heterogeneous: most steps are easy and routine, but errors cluster in a few critical points. Uniformly allocating heavy compute to all steps is wasteful and does not explicitly target the main failure modes: progress stalls and silent semantic drift. There is a need for a more efficient, deployment-ready way to selectively apply powerful models only when they are truly needed.

Method: The authors design an event-driven cascade architecture for GUI agents. A small, cheap policy runs by default for each interaction step. Lightweight learned monitors run in parallel to assess risk based on recent trajectory and semantic state. Two key monitors are introduced: (1) a Stuck Monitor that inspects recent reasoning and actions to detect signs of looping, ineffective behavior, or lack of progress, and then triggers a recovery step handled by a stronger model; and (2) a Milestone Monitor that recognizes semantically meaningful checkpoints in the task where verification is especially valuable, and at those points escalates to the stronger model to check for and correct semantic drift. The stronger model is invoked only on-demand at these detected high-risk events. The framework is modular and can wrap around existing agents without changing or retraining the large model.

Result: On computer-use benchmarks, the event-driven cascade substantially reduces calls to frontier-scale models while maintaining or improving task success rates. The monitors effectively detect and concentrate large-model usage on steps with elevated risk of getting stuck or drifting from the goal. This yields better cost-effectiveness and latency compared to baselines that invoke large models at every step or use simple heuristics for escalation.

Conclusion: Adaptive, event-driven compute allocation using learned stuck and milestone monitors can make computer-use agents more efficient and robust for long-horizon GUI tasks. By decoupling the monitoring logic from the core agent and only escalating to powerful models at critical steps, the framework offers a practical, deployment-oriented way to reduce cost and latency without sacrificing performance, and can be integrated with existing computer-use agents as a drop-in wrapper.

Abstract: Computer-use agents provide a promising path toward general software automation because they can interact directly with arbitrary graphical user interfaces instead of relying on brittle, application-specific integrations. Despite recent advances in benchmark performance, strong computer-use agents remain expensive and slow in practice, since most systems invoke large multimodal models at nearly every interaction step. We argue that this uniform allocation of compute is fundamentally inefficient for long-horizon GUI tasks. Such trajectories are highly heterogeneous: many steps are routine and can be handled reliably by smaller, cheaper policies, while errors tend to concentrate at a relatively small number of high-risk moments. Across computer-use benchmarks, these failures repeatedly take two forms: progress stalls, where the agent loops, repeats ineffective actions, or fails to make meaningful progress, and silent semantic drift, where the agent continues taking locally plausible actions after already deviating from the user's true goal. To address this inefficiency, we propose an event-driven, step-level cascade for computer-use agents that runs a small policy by default and escalates to a stronger model only when lightweight learned monitors detect elevated risk. Our framework combines two complementary signals: a Stuck Monitor that detects degraded progress from recent reasoning-action history and triggers recovery, and a Milestone Monitor that identifies semantically meaningful checkpoints where sparse verification is most informative for catching drift. This design turns always-on frontier-model inference into adaptive, on-demand compute allocation over the course of an evolving interaction. The framework is modular and deployment-oriented: it can be layered on top of existing computer-use agents without changing the underlying agent architecture or retraining the large model.

### [62] [Interval Orders, Biorders and Credibility-limited Belief Revision](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27156)
*Richard Booth,Ivan Varzinczak*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper generalizes the standard belief revision framework by replacing total preorders over possible worlds with richer order structures (interval orders and biorders), axiomatically characterizes the resulting revision operators, and analyzes their logical properties, in particular how they handle inconsistency, credibility, and non-prioritized acceptance of new information.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical AGM-style belief revision relies on a total preorder of possible worlds, which is often too restrictive to represent nuanced attitudes like intransitivities, incomparabilities, or cognitive dissonance. There is a need for more expressive order structures that can still support rational belief change, as seen in rational choice theory but under-explored in belief revision. The authors aim to import and adapt such structures (interval orders and biorders) to model agents that can experience instability or hesitation toward new information and that may change their stance when additional justification is provided.

Method: The authors define belief revision operators induced by interval orders and by biorders over possible worlds, interpreting intervals as ranges of plausibility, with biorders allowing negative-length intervals to capture dissonance. They give formal, axiomatic characterizations of the belief revision operators corresponding to these structures and to two intermediate families between standard interval orders and full biorders. They then analyze which AGM-style postulates (especially Success and Consistency) these operators satisfy or fail, and propose a modified, non-prioritized revision scheme that treats certain inputs as "incredible" and discards them to restore consistency. Finally, they relate these constructions to credibility-limited revision by Hansson et al., noting a weaker closure property for credible sentences, and conceptually argue for the suitability of biorder-based models in scenarios involving initial rejection and later acceptance of information.

Result: The paper shows that interval order–based revision operators can be axiomatically characterized within a generalized belief revision framework, and similarly for biorder-based operators and two intermediate classes. It establishes that biorder-based revisions always satisfy the Success postulate but may output inconsistent belief sets, violating Consistency. By adjusting the revision mechanism so that inputs leading to inconsistency are classified as incredible and hence rejected, the authors obtain new families of non-prioritized belief revision operators that satisfy Consistency but no longer guarantee Success. These operators are structurally related to Hansson-style credibility-limited revision, but differ in that the set of credible sentences is not required to be closed under single-sentence revision.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that moving from total preorders to interval orders and biorders yields richer, well-axiomatized families of belief revision operators capable of capturing phenomena such as dissonance, instability, and context-dependent credibility. While pure biorder-based revision sacrifices Consistency, the modified non-prioritized versions restore consistency at the cost of always accepting new information. This trade-off aligns their framework with, but also meaningfully generalizes, credibility-limited revision. Conceptually, the biorder-based approach is particularly suitable for modeling agents who may first reject new information as incredible yet later accept it when provided with further explanatory support.

Abstract: Rational belief revision is commonly viewed as being based on a preference order between possible worlds, with the resulting new belief set being those sentences true in all the most preferred models of the incoming new information. Usually, such a preference order is taken to be a total preorder. Nevertheless, there are other, more general classes of ordering that can also be employed. In this paper, we explore two such classes that have been studied within the theory of rational choice but have seen limited or no application in belief revision. We begin with interval orders, introduced by Fishburn in the '80s, which associate with each possible world a nonnegative `interval' of plausibility. We then move on to biorders, studied by Aleskerov, Bouyssou, and Monjardet, which generalise interval orders by allowing the intervals to have negative lengths, a feature that can be used to capture a notion of dissonance or instability. We provide axiomatic characterisations of these two resulting families of belief revision operators, as well as of two further families of interest that lie between interval orders and biorders. We show that while biorder-based revisions satisfy the Success postulate, they do not always yield consistent outputs. By modifying their definition to discard inputs that lead to inconsistency as `incredible', we derive new families of so-called non-prioritised revision that satisfy the Consistency postulate, but not the Success one. These families are linked to credibility-limited revision operators of Hansson et al., but for which the set of credible sentences does not satisfy the single-sentence closure condition. We argue that the biorder-based approach is well-suited for scenarios where an agent might initially reject new information, but may accept it when presented with additional explanation.

Model performance is compared primarily using Area Under the ROC Curve (AUC). Special attention is given to low-data regimes (e.g., N=50) to assess robustness when training data are limited.

Result: Across training set sizes, TabPFN achieves among the highest predictive performance, with a reported AUC of 0.892, exceeding LightGBM’s AUC of 0.860 and outperforming other traditional models. In the low-data setting of N=50 training examples, TabPFN maintains relatively strong AUC, whereas the conventional machine learning models show substantial performance degradation. This indicates TabPFN’s superior ability to learn effectively from limited labeled data in this domain.

Conclusion: The study concludes that TabPFN, as a pre-trained foundation model for tabular data, is highly effective for predicting MCI-to-AD conversion, particularly when training data are scarce. Its performance advantage over established machine learning models suggests that foundation models are a promising direction for disease risk prediction in data-limited medical scenarios such as Alzheimer’s disease, potentially enabling earlier and more reliable identification of patients at risk of progression.

Abstract: Accurate prediction of conversion from Mild Cognitive Impairment (MCI) to Alzheimers Diseases (AD) is essential for early intervention, however, developing reliable conversion predictive models is difficult to develop due to limited longitudinal data availability We evaluate TabPFN (Tabular Pre-Trained Foundation Network) against traditional machine learning methods for predicting 3 year MCI to AD conversion using the TADPOLE dataset derived from ADNI. Using multimodal biomarker features extracted from demographics, APOE4, MRI volumes, CSF markers, and PET imaging, we conducted an experimental comparison across varying training set sizes (N=50 to 1000) and models including XGBoost, Random Forest, LightGBM, and Logistic Regression. TabPFN achieved one the highest performance (AUC=0.892), outperforming LightGBM (AUC=0.860) and demonstrating advantages in low data settings. At N=50 training samples, TabPFN maintained strong AUC while the traditional machine learning models struggles at small training samples. These findings demonstrate that foundation models are promising for disease prediction in data limited scenarios, such as Alzheimers diseases.

There is a need for personalized, data-driven models that can integrate heterogeneous clinical, biomarker, and imaging data, handle sparse and irregular measurements, and provide uncertainty estimates to support robust clinical decision-making and in silico experimentation.

Method: The PCD-DT framework integrates three main components: (1) latent state-space models that capture individualized temporal disease dynamics from irregular longitudinal data; (2) multimodal fusion techniques that jointly model clinical scores, biomarkers, and MRI-derived imaging features, including a Bayesian tensor model for high-dimensional imaging; and (3) uncertainty-aware validation and adaptive updating strategies to keep the digital twin calibrated and robust over time. It further proposes using conditional generative models for data augmentation and stress testing underrepresented progression patterns. The Bayesian tensor modeling component is conceptually described as a way to fuse high-dimensional imaging, though detailed empirical results are limited in the abstract.

While early results on TADPOLE are promising and support the value of multimodal fusion, the authors emphasize remaining challenges, particularly improving uncertainty calibration and evaluating longer-horizon predictions. They position PCD-DT as a foundational step toward clinically deployable, patient-specific digital twin systems for neurodegeneration.

Abstract: Cognitive decline is highly heterogeneous across individuals, which complicates prognosis, trial design, and treatment planning. We present the Personalized Cognitive Decline Assessment Digital Twin (PCD-DT), a multimodal and uncertainty-aware framework for modeling patient-specific disease trajectories from sparse, noisy, and irregular longitudinal data. The framework combines three methodological components: (1) latent state-space models for individualized temporal dynamics, (2) multimodal fusion for clinical, biomarker, and imaging features, and (3) uncertainty-aware validation and adaptive updating for robust digital twin operation. We also outline how conditional generative models can support data augmentation and stress testing for underrepresented progression patterns. As a preliminary feasibility study, we analyze longitudinal TADPOLE trajectories and show clear separation between cognitively normal and Alzheimer's disease cohorts in ADAS13, ventricle volume, and hippocampal volume over five years. A Bayesian tensor modeling component for high-dimensional imaging fusion is also discussed. These results support the feasibility of the proposed architecture while also highlighting the need for stronger uncertainty calibration and longer-horizon predictive evaluation. The PCD-DT framework provides a principled starting point for personalized in silico modeling in neurodegenerative disease. This work positions PCD-DT as a foundational step toward clinically deployable, uncertainty-aware digital twin systems.

### [65] [Web2BigTable: A Bi-Level Multi-Agent LLM System for Internet-Scale Information Search and Extraction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27221)
*Yuxuan Huang,Yihang Chen,Zhiyuan He,Yuxiang Chen,Ka Yiu Lee,Huichi Zhou,Weilin Luo,Meng Fang,Jun Wang*

TL;DR: Web2BigTable is a multi-agent, web-to-table search framework that uses bi-level orchestration, shared memory, and self-improvement loops to excel at both broad, schema-aligned web aggregation and deep, long-horizon reasoning, achieving state-of-the-art results on WideSearch and strong performance on XBench-DeepSearch.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing agentic web search systems struggle with two key demands: (1) breadth-oriented tasks that require collecting information about many entities from heterogeneous sources while maintaining schema alignment, coverage, and cross-entity consistency; and (2) depth-oriented tasks that require coherent multi-step reasoning over long, branching search trajectories for a single target. There is a need for a unified framework that can effectively handle both regimes and improve over time.

Method: The paper proposes Web2BigTable, a bi-level multi-agent framework for web-to-table search. An upper-level orchestrator decomposes user tasks into sub-problems, while lower-level worker agents solve these sub-problems in parallel. The system employs a closed-loop run–verify–reflect process with persistent, human-readable external memory that lets agents refine both task decomposition and execution over time. Workers share a common workspace where partial findings are visible, enabling coordination to reduce redundant exploration, resolve conflicting evidence, and dynamically fill coverage gaps. The framework also incorporates self-evolving updates to each single agent based on past runs.

Result: On the WideSearch benchmark, Web2BigTable achieves state-of-the-art performance, with an Avg@4 Success Rate of 38.50 (7.5× higher than the second best method at 5.10), Row F1 of 63.53 (+25.03 over the second best), and Item F1 of 80.12 (+14.42 over the second best). It generalizes beyond breadth-oriented search, attaining 73.0 accuracy on the depth-oriented XBench-DeepSearch benchmark, indicating robust performance across both wide and deep search tasks.

Conclusion: Web2BigTable effectively unifies breadth- and depth-oriented agentic web search through a bi-level, multi-agent architecture with shared workspaces and a run–verify–reflect improvement loop. Its strong empirical results on WideSearch and XBench-DeepSearch demonstrate that structured multi-agent coordination and persistent external memory can substantially improve both wide, schema-aligned web aggregation and deep, long-horizon reasoning, establishing a new state of the art for web-to-table search tasks.

Abstract: Agentic web search increasingly faces two distinct demands: deep reasoning over a single target, and structured aggregation across many entities and heterogeneous sources. Current systems struggle on both fronts. Breadth-oriented tasks demand schema-aligned outputs with wide coverage and cross-entity consistency, while depth-oriented tasks require coherent reasoning over long, branching search trajectories. We introduce \textbf{Web2BigTable}, a multi-agent framework for web-to-table search that supports both regimes. Web2BigTable adopts a bi-level architecture in which an upper-level orchestrator decomposes the task into sub-problems and lower-level worker agents solve them in parallel. Through a closed-loop run--verify--reflect process, the framework jointly improves decomposition and execution over time via persistent, human-readable external memory, with self-evolving updates to each single-agent. During execution, workers coordinate through a shared workspace that makes partial findings visible, allowing them to reduce redundant exploration, reconcile conflicting evidence, and adapt to emerging coverage gaps. Web2BigTable sets a new state of the art on WideSearch, reaching an Avg@4 Success Rate of \textbf{38.50} ($7.5\times$ the second best at 5.10), Row F1 of \textbf{63.53} (+25.03 over the second best), and Item F1 of \textbf{80.12} (+14.42 over the second best). It also generalises to depth-oriented search on XBench-DeepSearch, achieving 73.0 accuracy. Code is available at https://github.com/web2bigtable/web2bigtable.

### [66] [When Roles Fail: Epistemic Constraints on Advocate Role Fidelity in LLM-Based Political Statement Analysis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27228)
*Juergen Dietrich*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper empirically tests whether multi-agent LLM systems used for political discourse actually maintain their assigned adversarial roles, introducing quantitative metrics of role fidelity and finding systematic failures driven by epistemic constraints of the models and fact-checking sources.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-agent LLM pipelines for democratic and political discourse often rely on multiple models taking distinct, adversarial roles (e.g., supporter vs. critic) to generate diverse assessments. These systems implicitly assume models will reliably stick to their assigned roles, but this assumption has not been rigorously tested. If role fidelity fails, such systems may give a misleading impression of epistemic diversity and balance, which is especially problematic for democratic deliberation and evaluation of political statements.

Method: The authors use the TRUST pipeline as a testbed and develop an epistemic stance classifier that can infer whether a model is taking an advocate or opposing role from its reasoning text, without depending on obvious surface-level lexical cues. They evaluate model outputs on 60 political statements (30 in English, 30 in German). Role fidelity is quantified via four proposed metrics: Role Drift Index (RDI), Expected Drift Distance (EDD), Directional Drift Index (DDI), and Entropy-based Role Stability (ERS). They then compare different LLMs (Mistral Large vs. Claude Sonnet) and different fact-check providers (including Perplexity) to see how these choices affect role fidelity and failure modes.

Result: The analysis identifies two closely related failure modes: (1) an Epistemic Floor Effect, where fact-check results impose an absolute lower bound on how strongly a model can maintain a legitimizing role for false or debunked statements; and (2) Role-Prior Conflict, where the model’s pretraining knowledge overrides role instructions when the factual status of a statement is clear. The authors argue both are manifestations of a broader mechanism they term Epistemic Role Override (ERO), in which the model’s epistemic commitments trump role adherence. Empirically, Mistral Large achieves substantially higher role fidelity than Claude Sonnet (67% vs. 39%, a 28 percentage point difference) and displays different failure behavior: Mistral tends to drop the role without reversing stance, whereas Claude actively flips to the opposite position. Role fidelity appears robust to language (similar behavior for English and German). Fact-check provider choice is not neutral: for Claude Sonnet, using Perplexity as a fact-check source significantly lowers role fidelity on German statements (−15 percentage points, p = 0.007), while Mistral’s fidelity is unaffected by that choice.

Conclusion: Multi-agent LLM systems that rely on adversarial role assignments cannot be assumed to faithfully maintain those roles, especially in politically charged or factually unambiguous contexts. The newly introduced role fidelity metrics reveal systematic deviations driven by Epistemic Role Override, where the model’s internal epistemic state and external fact checks override role instructions. Model selection and fact-check provider selection materially affect fidelity and failure modes, while language appears less critical. Therefore, validation of multi-agent LLM pipelines must explicitly measure role fidelity; otherwise, such systems may falsely appear to offer rich epistemic diversity while in fact collapsing to the model’s priors or to fact-check constraints.

Abstract: Democratic discourse analysis systems increasingly rely on multi-agent LLM pipelines in which distinct evaluator models are assigned adversarial roles to generate structured, multi-perspective assessments of political statements. A core assumption is that models will reliably maintain their assigned roles. This paper provides the first systematic empirical test of that assumption using the TRUST pipeline. We develop an epistemic stance classifier that identifies advocate roles from reasoning text without relying on surface vocabulary, and measure role fidelity across 60 political statements (30 English, 30 German) using four metrics: Role Drift Index (RDI), Expected Drift Distance (EDD), Directional Drift Index (DDI), and Entropy-based Role Stability (ERS). We identify two failure modes - the Epistemic Floor Effect (fact-check results create an absolute lower bound below which the legitimizing role cannot be maintained) and Role-Prior Conflict (training-time knowledge overrides role instructions for factually unambiguous statements) - as manifestations of a single mechanism: Epistemic Role Override (ERO). Model choice significantly affects role fidelity: Mistral Large outperforms Claude Sonnet by 28pp (67% vs. 39%) and exhibits a qualitatively different failure mode - role abandonment without polarity reversal - compared to Claude's active switch to the opposing stance. Role fidelity is language-robust. Fact-check provider choice is not universally neutral: Perplexity significantly reduces Claude's role fidelity on German statements (Delta = -15pp, p = 0.007) while leaving Mistral unaffected. These findings have direct implications for multi-agent LLM validation: a system validated without role fidelity measurement may systematically misrepresent the epistemic diversity it was designed to provide.

### [67] [Reinforced Agent: Inference-Time Feedback for Tool-Calling Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27233)
*Anh Ta,Junjie Zhu,Shahin Shayandeh*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes putting an automatic reviewer agent in the inference-time execution loop for tool-calling LLM agents, to proactively check and correct tool calls before execution, and introduces metrics to quantify when this reviewer is actually helpful versus harmful.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of tool-calling LLM agents are post-hoc: they analyze completed trajectories for tool choice, parameter accuracy, and scope recognition, but they cannot intervene during execution. Errors revealed this way are typically handled via prompt tuning or retraining and cannot prevent mistakes in real time. The authors are motivated to build a mechanism that can evaluate and correct an agent’s tool calls during inference, and to rigorously measure the tradeoff between the corrections a reviewer introduces and the new errors it may cause.

Method: The authors embed a dedicated reviewer agent inside the inference-time execution loop. The primary execution agent proposes provisional tool calls; before these are executed, the reviewer evaluates them and can adjust or reject them. This creates a separation of concerns: the base agent focuses on task execution, while the reviewer focuses on quality control. To study this systematically, they define Helpfulness-Harmfulness metrics, where helpfulness is the fraction of original agent errors that reviewer feedback corrects, and harmfulness is the fraction of correct responses that the reviewer degrades. They then run experiments on BFCL (single-turn tool use) and Tau2-Bench (multi-turn stateful tasks), and also explore reviewer model choice (o3-mini vs GPT-4o) and prompt design, including automated prompt optimization with GEPA.

Result: On BFCL and Tau2-Bench, integrating the reviewer into the loop improves performance, with +5.5% gain on irrelevance detection and +7.1% on multi-turn tasks. The Helpfulness-Harmfulness metrics show that reviewer choice matters: the o3-mini reasoning model achieves about a 3:1 ratio of corrections to degradations, while GPT-4o achieves roughly 2.1:1, indicating o3-mini is a safer and more beneficial reviewer. Applying automated prompt optimization via GEPA further boosts performance by about +1.5–2.8%.

Conclusion: Placing a reviewer agent in the execution loop enables proactive detection and mitigation of tool-calling errors, rather than relying only on post-hoc evaluations. The separation of execution and review allows independent improvement of the reviewer via model selection and prompt optimization, without retraining the base agent. The proposed Helpfulness-Harmfulness metrics give a principled way to assess whether a reviewer configuration is net beneficial, and experiments on standard benchmarks show measurable gains, especially when using a strong reasoning model and optimized prompts as the reviewer.

Abstract: Tool-calling agents are evaluated on tool selection, parameter accuracy, and scope recognition, yet LLM trajectory assessments remain inherently post-hoc. Disconnected from the active execution loop, such assessments identify errors that are usually addressed through prompt-tuning or retraining, and fundamentally cannot course-correct the agent in real time. To close this gap, we move evaluation into the execution loop at inference time: a specialized reviewer agent evaluates provisional tool calls prior to execution, shifting the paradigm from post-hoc recovery to proactive evaluation and error mitigation.
  In practice, this architecture establishes a clear separation of concerns between the primary execution agent and a secondary review agent. As with any multi-agent system, the reviewer can introduce new errors while correcting others, yet no prior work to our knowledge has systematically measured this tradeoff. To quantify this tradeoff, we introduce Helpfulness-Harmfulness metrics: helpfulness measures the percentage of base agent errors that feedback corrects; harmfulness measures the percentage of correct responses that feedback degrades. These metrics directly inform reviewer design by revealing whether a given model or prompt provides net positive value.
  We evaluate our approach on BFCL (single-turn) and Tau2-Bench (multi-turn stateful scenarios), achieving +5.5% on irrelevance detection and +7.1% on multi-turn tasks. Our metrics reveal that reviewer model choice is critical: the reasoning model o3-mini achieves a 3:1 benefit-to-risk ratio versus 2.1:1 for GPT-4o. Automated prompt optimization via GEPA provides an additional +1.5-2.8%. Together, these results demonstrate a core advantage of separating execution and review: the reviewer can be systematically improved through model selection and prompt optimization, without retraining the base agent.

### [68] [AutoSurfer -- Teaching Web Agents through Comprehensive Surfing, Learning, and Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27253)
*Fazle Elahi Faisal,Qianhui Wu,Baolin Peng,Jianfeng Gao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AutoSurfer is a system that systematically explores websites to generate high-quality, diverse web interaction trajectories for training multimodal LLM web agents, significantly improving task completion accuracy over prior methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multimodal LLM web agents can automate complex online tasks but are limited by scarce, low-quality web trajectory training data. Existing automatic data generators either explore only shallowly from the homepage or via random walks, leading to poor website coverage and hallucinated or ambiguous tasks. A better, systematic way to explore sites and ground tasks in real interaction paths is needed to train reliable, site-specific web agents.

Method: AutoSurfer introduces three main components: (1) a breadth-first, systematic exploration strategy that maintains a queue of discovered pages and actions, propagates knowledge to avoid re-exploring similar elements, and recursively expands multi-level GUI elements to mimic how humans learn a new site; (2) a task synthesis module that uses the collected exploration trajectories as structural context, grounding complex tasks in actual navigation paths rather than solely on isolated pages or random actions, which reduces hallucinations and ambiguity; and (3) a trajectory refinement mechanism where the same exploration trajectories serve as hints to guide a downstream web agent to produce more accurate, reliable execution traces of tasks. These components together generate comprehensive, high-quality web trajectories for each website.

Result: On the WebArena benchmark, AutoSurfer-generated data is used to fine-tune Qwen2.5-VL-7B-Instruct. The resulting web agent surpasses state-of-the-art automatic trajectory generation methods (Explorer, OS-Genesis, SynthAgent), achieving up to 24.23% overall task completion accuracy versus 19.59% for the best prior method. Additionally, analysis of the synthesized tasks reveals a more diverse distribution of task types and goals compared to baselines.

Conclusion: Systematic, trajectory-guided exploration and task synthesis can substantially improve the coverage, realism, and diversity of web trajectory datasets for training web agents. AutoSurfer’s breadth-first exploration, trajectory-grounded task generation, and trajectory-guided refinement together yield better-performing, more reliable website-specific LLM agents than previous automatic data generation approaches, as evidenced by improved task completion accuracy and task diversity on WebArena.

Abstract: Recent advances in multimodal large language models (LLMs) have revolutionized web agents that can automate complex tasks on websites. However, their accuracy remains limited by the scarcity of high-quality web trajectory training data. Existing automatic trajectory generation methods suffer from incomplete website coverage due to homepage-based task proposals or random-walk exploration. Such methods often result in hallucinated or ambiguous task synthesis that lead to incomplete and unreliable trajectory generation. Here, we present AutoSurfer, a comprehensive web trajectory generator that addresses these limitations through three key innovations. First, AutoSurfer employs a systematic breadth-first exploration strategy that maintains a queue of discovered pages and action traces, propagates knowledge across pages to avoid redundant exploration, and recursively expands multi-level graphical user interface elements - closely resembling how a human would learn a new website. Second, AutoSurfer leverages the exploration trajectory to guide task synthesis, reducing hallucinations by grounding complex tasks in actual navigation paths rather than isolated actions or page content alone. Third, AutoSurfer uses the same exploration trajectory as hints to steer a web agent toward more accurate and reliable trajectory refinement. Together, these innovations enable AutoSurfer to comprehensively cover a website's action space and generate data suitable for training website-specific LLMs. We evaluate AutoSurfer on the WebArena benchmark by fine-tuning Qwen2.5-VL-7B-Instruct and demonstrate that it outperforms state-of-the-art methods - Explorer, OS-Genesis, and SynthAgent - achieving up to 24.23% overall task completion accuracy compared to 19.59% for the best prior method. Further, task diversity analysis demonstrates that AutoSurfer yields a more diverse distribution of synthesized tasks.

### [69] [OptimusKG: Unifying biomedical knowledge in a modern multimodal graph](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27269)
*Lucas Vittor,Ayush Noori,Iñaki Arango,Joaquín Polonuer,Sam Rodriques,Andrew White,David A. Clifton,Marinka Zitnik*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: OptimusKG is a large, multimodal biomedical knowledge graph with a strict schema and rich properties, built from structured resources and validated against the literature for downstream ML and LLM applications.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing biomedical knowledge graphs often lack schema-level constraints when built from text, or are difficult to harmonize when assembled from heterogeneous structured sources. There is a need for a unified, schema-consistent, and richly annotated graph that integrates molecular, anatomical, clinical, and environmental data and is directly usable for machine learning and LLM-based reasoning.

Method: The authors construct a labeled property graph (LPG) called OptimusKG from 18 structured and semi-structured biomedical ontologies and controlled vocabularies. They define a top-level schema for node and edge types, retain detailed type-specific properties, cross-references, and provenance, and store the graph in Apache Parquet format. They then evaluate edge validity by using a multimodal literature-retrieval agent (PaperQA3) to check whether sampled edges are supported by scientific publications, and compare support for true versus synthetically generated false edges.

Result: OptimusKG comprises 190,531 nodes across 10 entity types, 21,813,816 edges across 26 relation types, and 67,249,863 property instances containing over 110 million values across 150 property keys. PaperQA3 found supporting literature for 70.0% of sampled real edges, while 83.4% of sampled false edges had no supporting evidence. Unsupported edges were enriched in associations from experimental and functional genomics resources, indicating that some encoded knowledge may not yet be synthesized in the literature.

Conclusion: OptimusKG provides a large, schema-enforced, multimodal biomedical knowledge graph with rich metadata and provenance, suitable as a standardized resource for graph-based machine learning, knowledge-grounded LLM retrieval, and biomedical discovery tasks such as hypothesis generation. Its partial literature validation suggests good precision of encoded relations while also capturing cutting-edge experimental knowledge that is not yet well represented in publications.

Abstract: Biomedical knowledge graphs (KGs) are widely used in the life sciences, yet many are derived from unstructured documents and therefore lack schema-level constrains, whereas graphs assembled from structured resources are difficult to harmonize into a unified representation. We present OptimusKG, a multimodal biomedical labeled property graph (LPG) built from structured and semi-structured resources to preserve factual, type-specific metadata across molecular, anatomical, clinical, and environmental domains. OptimusKG contains 190,531 nodes across 10 entity types, 21,813,816 edges across 26 relation types, and 67,249,863 property instances encoding 110,276,843 values across 150 distinct property keys, derived from 18 ontologies and controlled vocabularies. The graph enforces a top-level schema for nodes and edges and retains granular, type-specific properties, cross-references, and provenance across molecular, anatomical, clinical, and environmental domains. We assessed the validity of OptimusKG by evaluating whether graph relationships are supported by evidence from the scientific literature using a multimodal agent, PaperQA3. PaperQA3 identified supporting evidence for 70.0% of sampled edges, whereas 83.4% of sampled false edges received no supporting evidence. Edges without literature support were concentrated in associations derived from experimental and functional genomics resources, suggesting that OptimusKG captures biomedical knowledge that may precede synthesis in the scientific literature. OptimusKG is distributed as Apache Parquet files, providing a standardized resource for graph-based machine learning, knowledge-grounded retrieval with large language models, and biomedical discovery use cases such as hypothesis generation.

### [70] [The Inverse-Wisdom Law: Architectural Tribalism and the Consensus Paradox in Agentic Swarms](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27274)
*Dahlia Shehata,Ming Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that in current multi-agent AI systems, adding more logically strong agents can actually make collective errors more stable, because agents prioritize agreement over truth.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most multi-agent AI research assumes that having many agents collaborate will approximate a “wisdom of the crowd” effect, improving accuracy and robustness. The authors suspect this assumption is flawed: when agents share similar architectures and training, they may amplify each other’s biases and sycophancy, converging on confident but wrong answers. They want to understand and formalize why and how this happens, and what design principles are needed to avoid it.

Method: The authors define and formalize several phenomena: the Consensus Paradox (agreement over truth), the Inverse-Wisdom Law (more logical agents can strengthen errors), Logic Saturation (full internal agreement despite being factually wrong), Architectural Tribalism Asymmetry, Tribalism Coefficient, and Sycophantic Weight. They conduct 36 experiments, generating 12,804 multi-agent trajectories across three benchmarks (GAIA, Multi-Challenge, SWE-bench). They systematically vary swarm composition—numbers and types of “logical” agents and synthesizers—using three SOTA LLMs (Gemini 3.1 Pro, Claude Sonnet 4.6, GPT-5.4), then measure how often swarms converge on correct vs incorrect outcomes and how stable those outcomes are under further audits.

Result: Empirically, they find that in “kinship-dominant” swarms (agents with similar architectures or behavior), adding more logically capable agents tends to increase the stability of wrong answers rather than the probability of reaching truth. Extra logical audits drive systems into Logic Saturation: the swarm becomes fully consistent internally (zero entropy of opinions) while still being factually wrong with high probability. Interactions among the three SOTA models reveal an asymmetry driven by their architectural traits; one model acting as synthesizer can dominate and gate the final outcome regardless of the others’ quality.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that multi-agent “wisdom of the crowd” intuition breaks down for homogeneous, kinship-dominant LLM swarms. Swarm reliability is controlled more by the synthesizer’s receptive logic and its tendencies toward tribalism and sycophancy than by the average agent quality. They propose the Heterogeneity Mandate: robust and safe multi-agent AI systems must intentionally incorporate architectural and behavioral diversity to avoid consensus on confident errors, and designers should monitor and control metrics like the Tribalism Coefficient and Sycophantic Weight as core safety levers.

Abstract: As AI transitions toward multi-agent systems (MAS) to solve complex workflows, research paradigms operate on the axiomatic assumption that agent collaboration mirrors the "Wisdom of the Crowd". We challenge this assumption by formalizing the Consensus Paradox: a phenomenon where agentic swarms prioritize internal architectural agreement over external logical truth. Through a 36 experiments encompassing 12,804 trajectories across three state-of-the-art (SOTA) benchmarks (GAIA, Multi-Challenge, and SWE-bench), we prove the Inverse-Wisdom Law: in kinship-dominant swarms, adding logical agents increases the stability of erroneous trajectories rather than the probability of truth. The introduction of additional logical audits converges the system toward a Logic Saturation where internal entropy hits zero while factual error hits unity. By evaluating the interaction between the 3 preeminent SOTA models (Gemini 3.1 Pro, Claude Sonnet 4.6, and GPT-5.4), we establish the Architectural Tribalism Asymmetry as a mechanistic law of transformer weights. We demonstrate that terminal swarm integrity is strictly gated by the synthesizer's receptive logic, rather than aggregate agent quality. We define the Tribalism Coefficient and the Sycophantic Weight as the primary mechanistic determinants of swarm failure. Finally, we establish the Heterogeneity Mandate as a foundational safety requirement for resilient agentic architectures.

### [71] [Mechanized Foundations of Structural Governance: Machine-Checked Proofs for Governed Intelligence](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27289)
*Alan L. McCann*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Paper develops and mechanizes a theory of structural governance for cognitive workflow systems with several core theorems and a verified interpreter specification.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To formalize and prove properties about governance, safety, and expressive completeness in cognitive workflow systems, and to connect these abstract proofs to a real-world runtime implementation.

Method: Uses Coq 8.19 with Interaction Trees and parameterized coinduction to mechanize key theorems, and traditional paper proofs with explicit reductions and rewriting systems for others; also provides a formal specification of the BEAM runtime and validates it via property-based testing.

Result: Introduces a coinductive safety predicate for governed execution, proves governance invariance across meta-levels, shows expressive completeness of four atomic primitives via Kleisli closure, defines an alternating normal form with a confluent rewrite system, establishes the necessity of an opaque reasoning primitive via reduction to Rice’s theorem, and validates a Coq specification of the BEAM runtime against 70,000 random tests with no mismatches.

Conclusion: Structural governance of cognitive workflow systems can be captured with precise coinductive and categorical machinery, a minimal set of primitives is both sufficient and partly necessary for semantic tasks, and these ideas can be linked to and validated against a concrete production runtime through a substantial mechanized development.

Abstract: We present five results in the theory of structural governance for cognitive workflow systems. Three are mechanized in Coq 8.19 using the Interaction Trees library with parameterized coinduction; two are proved on paper with explicit reductions. The Coinductive Safety Predicate (gov_safe) is a coinductive property that captures governance safety for infinite program behaviors, indexed by a boolean permission flag that is provably false for ungoverned I/O and true for governed interpretations (mechanized). The Governance Invariance Theorem establishes that governance is uniform across the meta-recursive tower: governance at level n+1 reduces to governance at level n by definitional equality of the type (mechanized). The Sufficiency Theorem proves that four atomic primitives (code, reason, memory, call) are expressively complete for any discrete intelligent system, formalized as compositional closure of a Kleisli category (mechanized). The Alternating Normal Form provides a canonical decomposition of any machine into alternating code and effect layers, with a confluent rewriting system (paper proof). The Necessity Theorem proves via explicit reduction to Rice's theorem that an architecturally opaque component (the reason primitive) is mathematically necessary for problems requiring semantic judgment (paper proof). A sixth contribution connects the abstract model to the deployed runtime: the Verified Interpreter Specification formalizes the BEAM runtime's trust, capability, and hash chain logic in Coq, then tests the running system against this specification using property-based testing with over 70,000 randomly generated directive sequences and zero disagreements. The mechanization comprises approximately 12,000 lines across 36 modules with 454 theorems and zero admitted lemmas.
