### [72] [The Two Boundaries: Why Behavioral AI Governance Fails Structurally](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27292)
*Alan L. McCann*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that for AI systems that produce real-world effects, governance must be built into the architecture so that what the system can do and what is governed are exactly the same set of actions; otherwise, ungoverned risk and empty governance are structurally unavoidable.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Deployed AI systems that can act in the world (e.g., via tools, APIs, databases) typically have their technical capabilities and governance policies specified separately. This creates three zones: actions that are both possible and governed (useful), actions that are possible but not governed (dangerous risk), and policies that cover actions the system cannot actually perform (governance theater). Because this misalignment is widespread and consequential, the authors want a rigorous, formal way to characterize and ultimately eliminate this structural gap between capability and governance for effectful AI systems.

Method: The authors focus on governance of effects (actions in the world) rather than governance of model outputs. Using formal methods and computability theory, they analyze architectures where governance tries to constrain effects by inspecting or interpreting behavior. Leveraging Rice's theorem, they prove that for any Turing-complete system in which governance is an external behavioral layer, it is undecidable in general to determine if a program’s effects comply with a given policy. They then define the notion of coterminous governance—where the boundary of what the system can express (its effectful capabilities) is exactly the same as the boundary of what governance can regulate. Achieving this requires an architectural separation of computation from effect, so that effectful operations pass through a governance-enforced interface. They formalize and mechanize these ideas in Coq, encoding the architecture, properties, and proofs across numerous theorems and modules.

Result: The paper establishes that when effectful AI systems are built as Turing-complete architectures with an external behavioral governance layer, governance cannot, in general, decide whether effects comply with policy—creating inevitable zones of ungoverned risk and governance theater. It introduces and characterizes coterminous governance as a system property that eliminates this structural gap by construction: the set of possible effects is exactly the set that governance can address. Under architectures that separate computation from effect, governance checks become intrinsic to the execution pipeline instead of a bolt-on layer. The authors provide fully mechanized proofs in Coq (454 theorems across 36 modules with no unproven lemmas), formally demonstrating the undecidability result and the conditions under which coterminous governance can be achieved.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that any AI governance scheme for systems with real-world effects must satisfy coterminous governance—alignment of capability and governance boundaries—or else it will unavoidably produce both ungoverned risky behavior and governance theater. This is not a matter of better policies or more monitoring but of system architecture: computation must be separated from effect so that all effectful actions are mediated by governance logic embedded in the execution path. When this structural condition holds, separate governance infrastructure becomes redundant, as governance is effectively baked into how effects occur. The authors propose coterminous governance as a concrete, testable criterion for evaluating AI governance designs and support their claims with fully mechanized formal proofs.

Abstract: Every system that performs effects has two boundaries: what it can do (expressiveness) and what governance covers (governance). In nearly all deployed AI systems, these boundaries are defined independently, creating three regions: governed capabilities (the only useful region), ungoverned capabilities (risk), and governance policies that address non-existent capabilities (theater). Two of the three regions are failure modes. We focus on the governance of effects: actions that AI systems perform in the world (API calls, database writes, tool invocations). This is distinct from the governance of model outputs (content quality, bias, fairness), which operates at a different level and requires different mechanisms. We present a formal framework for analyzing this structural gap. Rice's theorem (1953) proves the gap is undecidable in the general case for any Turing-complete architecture that attempts to govern effects behaviorally: no algorithm can decide non-trivial semantic properties of arbitrary programs, including the property "this program's effects comply with the governance policy." We define coterminous governance: a system property where the expressivenessboundary equals the governance boundary. We show that coterminous governance requires an architectural decision (separatingcomputation from effect) rather than a governance layer added after the fact. We show that structural governance under this separation subsumes separate governance infrastructure: governance checks become part of the execution pipeline rather than a second system running alongside it. We propose coterminous governance as the testable criterion for any AI governance system: either the two boundaries are provably identical, or risk and theater are structurally inevitable. Proofs are mechanized in Coq (454 theorems, 36 modules, 0 admitted).

</details>


### [73] [Learning Rate Engineering: From Coarse Single Parameter to Layered Evolution](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27295)
*Ming-Hong Yao,Di Wang,Jian Cui,Jin-Yan Chen,Zi-Hao Cui,Fa Wang,Chen Wei,Qiu-Ye Yu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a taxonomy of five generations of learning rate strategies and proposes a new unified optimizer, DALS, that performs competitively across diverse regimes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing learning rate strategies have evolved in a fragmented way from simple global rates to complex layer-wise methods, and no single method works robustly across both from-scratch training and fine-tuning. The authors want to systematically organize this evolution, understand why each transition happened, and design a unified strategy that reconciles conflicting needs of different layers and training regimes, particularly the ‘impossible trinity’ in transfer learning where lower layers must remain stable while higher layers must adapt strongly.

Method: 1) Define a five-generation taxonomy of learning rate scheduling methods: global fixed rates, global scheduling, parameter-wise adaptation, layer-wise differentiation, and joint layer-time scheduling. 2) Analyze the core motivation behind moving from simpler to more tailored schemes, especially in the context of transfer learning. 3) Propose Discriminative Adaptive Layer Scaling (DALS), which combines phase-adaptive cosine scheduling, depth-aware Grokfast gradient filtering, and LARS-style trust ratio scaling into a unified optimizer. 4) Implement and benchmark 18 different strategies, including three DALS variants, across all five generations on five datasets (synthetic, CIFAR-10 from scratch, RTE, TREC-6, IMDb) covering both from-scratch and fine-tuning scenarios.

Result: On the synthetic dataset, DALS achieves the highest accuracy at 98.0%, and a faster DALS variant (DALS-Fast) reaches 90% accuracy in just 3 epochs, indicating strong optimization efficiency. The large-scale comparison across five datasets shows clear regime-dependent behavior: no single learning rate strategy dominates in all settings. Notably, STLR+Discriminative, previously strong in ULMFiT-style fine-tuning, performs extremely poorly on from-scratch tasks (e.g., 43.6% vs. 96.8% with RAdam on TREC-6), demonstrating that its directional decay assumptions are harmful without pretrained features. DALS, in contrast, provides top performance on synthetic tasks and remains competitive in fine-tuning scenarios, suggesting better robustness across regimes.

Conclusion: Learning rate strategies can be coherently organized into five generations that reflect increasing granularity and adaptivity in time and space (layers/parameters). The needs of lower and higher layers, especially in transfer learning, create an ‘impossible trinity’ that naive global or purely discriminative schedules cannot resolve. Techniques like STLR+Discriminative are heavily regime-dependent and can fail catastrophically when their assumptions (e.g., presence of pretrained features) are violated. The proposed DALS framework, which unifies phase-aware scheduling, depth-aware filtering, and trust ratio scaling, offers a more balanced and robust optimizer that performs strongly on synthetic and remains competitive on real fine-tuning tasks, though results confirm there is still no universal best strategy across all training regimes.

Abstract: Learning rate scheduling has evolved from the single global fixed rate of early SGD to sophisticated layer-wise adaptive strategies. We systematize this evolution into five generations: (Gen1) global fixed learning rates, (Gen2) global scheduling, (Gen3) parameter-level adaptation, (Gen4) layer-level differentiation, and (Gen5) joint layer-time scheduling. We trace the fundamental motivation behind each transition, showing how the shift from one-size-fits-all to tailoring by layer and time addresses the impossible trinity of transfer learning: lower layers require small updates to preserve general knowledge while higher layers need large updates to adapt to new tasks. Building on this taxonomy, we propose Discriminative Adaptive Layer Scaling (DALS), a unified framework that integrates phase-adaptive cosine scheduling, depth-aware Grokfast gradient filtering, and LARS-style trust ratios into a single coherent optimizer. We benchmark 18 strategies including three DALS variants across all five generations on five datasets: synthetic, CIFAR-10 (from scratch), RTE, TREC-6, and IMDb (fine-tuning). On synthetic, DALS achieves the best accuracy at 98.0%, while DALS-Fast reaches 90% in just 3 epochs. The cross-dataset analysis reveals striking regime-dependent patterns -- no single strategy wins across all regimes. Critically, STLR+Discriminative, the ULMFiT champion, catastrophically fails on from-scratch tasks (43.6% on TREC-6 from scratch vs. 96.8% with RAdam), confirming that directional decay biases are harmful without pretrained features. DALS avoids either extreme, achieving the best synthetic result while maintaining competitive fine-tuning performance.

*Gyoung S. Na,Chanyoung Park*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a new AI paradigm, machine collective intelligence, that autonomously discovers compact, interpretable governing equations from data, outperforming deep neural networks in extrapolation and parsimony.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical scientific practice seeks explicit governing equations from empirical observations, but automatically deriving such equations remains difficult. Modern AI, especially deep learning, excels at approximating functions yet typically produces black-box models that lack interpretability and extrapolation ability. This gap limits AI’s role in genuine scientific discovery, where concise, explainable, and generalizable equations are essential. The authors are motivated to overcome this limitation by creating an AI framework that can autonomously infer governing equations, without heavy domain-specific handcrafting, across a variety of dynamical systems.

Method: The authors propose machine collective intelligence, a unified framework that combines symbolic AI (for representing and manipulating explicit equations) with metaheuristics (for global search and optimization). They deploy multiple reasoning agents that each maintain symbolic hypotheses about the governing equations. These agents iteratively generate candidate equations, evaluate them against data, critique each other’s proposals, and consolidate promising structures. Through this coordinated, evolutionary process, the collective refines its symbolic models, effectively performing autonomous symbolic regression and model discovery beyond what a single agent could achieve. The framework is applied to systems with deterministic, stochastic, and previously unknown dynamics, without relying on handcrafted domain knowledge.

Result: Across diverse test systems, the framework autonomously recovered the true or equivalent governing equations directly from empirical data. It did so even for stochastic and previously uncharacterized systems. Compared with deep neural networks trained on the same data, the discovered symbolic equations achieved dramatically superior extrapolation performance, reducing extrapolation error by up to six orders of magnitude. Additionally, the method compressed complex models with 0.5–1 million parameters into compact equations with only 5–40 interpretable parameters, while preserving or improving predictive accuracy.

Conclusion: Machine collective intelligence demonstrates that integrating symbolic reasoning with metaheuristic search and multi-agent coordination enables autonomous discovery of governing equations from data. The approach overcomes key limitations of black-box AI models in interpretability and extrapolation, producing concise, human-understandable equations that generalize far beyond the training regime. The authors argue that this represents a significant shift toward AI systems that can participate in principled scientific discovery, potentially transforming how scientists model complex natural and engineered systems.

Abstract: Deriving governing equations from empirical observations is a longstanding challenge in science. Although artificial intelligence (AI) has demonstrated substantial capabilities in function approximation, the discovery of explainable and extrapolatable equations remains a fundamental limitation of modern AI, posing a central bottleneck for AI-driven scientific discovery. Here, we present machine collective intelligence, a unified paradigm that integrates two fundamental yet distinct traditions in computational intelligence--symbolism and metaheuristics--to enable autonomous and evolutionary discovery of governing equations. It orchestrates multiple reasoning agents to evolve their symbolic hypotheses through coordinated generation, evaluation, critique, and consolidation, enabling scientific discovery beyond single-agent inference. Across scientific systems governed by deterministic, stochastic, or previously uncharacterized dynamics, machine collective intelligence autonomously recovered the underlying governing equations without relying on hand-crafted domain knowledge. Furthermore, the resulting equations reduced extrapolation error by up to six orders of magnitude relative to deep neural networks, while condensing 0.5-1 million model parameters into just 5-40 interpretable parameters. This study marks an important shift in AI toward the autonomous discovery of principled scientific equations.

### [75] [METASYMBO: Multi-Agent Language-Guided Metamaterial Discovery via Symbolic Latent Evolution](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27300)
*Jianpeng Chen,Wangzhi Zhan,Dongqi Fu,Junkai Zhang,Zian Jia,Ling Li,Wei Wang,Dawei Zhou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: MetaSymbO is a multi-agent, language-guided framework that uses symbolic operations in a latent space to generate and refine mechanical metamaterials from free-form natural language design intents, achieving higher structural validity, better language alignment, and strong novelty compared to baselines, with successful real-world case studies.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Metamaterial discovery often starts from vague, qualitative design goals stated in natural language (e.g., “auxetic but stiff in one direction”), not from precise numerical targets. Existing inverse-design pipelines need explicit property targets and are mainly optimized to reproduce or interpolate known designs, making them poorly suited for early-stage exploration and for systematically discovering novel structures that still satisfy structural and physical constraints. Large language models, on the other hand, can understand human intents but are not natively aware of geometry and mechanical validity. The paper is motivated by the need to bridge this gap: to create a system that can interpret free-form language intents, propose valid and periodic metamaterial microstructures, and iteratively refine them using fast property feedback, while going beyond the limitations of purely data-driven reproduction of existing samples.

Method: The authors introduce MetaSymbO, a three-agent multi-component system: (1) a Designer agent that parses free-form natural language design intents and retrieves an initial semantically consistent structural scaffold, linking linguistic concepts to structures; (2) a Generator agent that operates in a disentangled latent space to synthesize candidate microstructures, where different latent dimensions correspond to interpretable geometric or functional factors; and (3) a Supervisor agent that supplies fast, property-aware feedback, enabling iterative refinement loops without full high-fidelity simulations at each step. On top of this architecture, they propose symbolic-driven latent evolution: programmable operators acting on the disentangled latent codes (e.g., composing, editing, or combining latent factors) that allow one to modify or recombine structures at inference time following symbolic or logical rules derived from the design intent. The system is trained and evaluated against baselines, with metrics on structural validity (symmetry, periodicity), language-guidance alignment, structure novelty, and downstream mechanical performance.

Result: Empirically, MetaSymbO significantly improves structural validity compared with state-of-the-art baselines, achieving up to 34% improvement in symmetry and nearly 98% improvement in periodicity. It also obtains 6–7% higher language-guidance scores, indicating better alignment between generated structures and the input natural language descriptions, while preserving or improving structure novelty relative to advanced reasoning LLM-based baselines. Qualitative studies show that the symbolic logic operators in the latent space enable controlled, programmable semantic changes to geometry (e.g., systematically refining or composing patterns consistent with intent). Real-world case studies, including the design of auxetic and high-stiffness metamaterials, demonstrate that the generated structures are not only valid and interpretable but also practically effective in achieving desired mechanical behaviors.

Conclusion: MetaSymbO demonstrates that coupling language understanding with a disentangled, symbolically controllable latent space can enable efficient, early-stage metamaterial design from qualitative, free-form intents. Its multi-agent architecture, along with symbolic-driven latent evolution, produces geometrically valid, periodic, and novel microstructures that remain strongly aligned with natural language goals and show promising real-world mechanical performance. The work suggests a path toward more interactive, programmable, and generalizable design tools where researchers can explore the metamaterial design space via high-level language and symbolic operations, rather than relying solely on explicit numerical targets or purely data-driven replication of known structures.

Abstract: Metamaterial discovery seeks microstructured materials whose geometry induces targeted mechanical behavior. Existing inverse-design methods can efficiently generate candidates, but they typically require explicit numerical property targets and are less suitable for early-stage exploration, where researchers often begin with incomplete constraints and qualitative intents expressed in natural language. Large language models can interpret such intents, but they lack geometric awareness and physical property validity. To address this gap, we propose MetaSymbO, a multi-agent framework for language-guided Metamaterial discovery via Symbolic-driven latent evOlution. Specifically, MetaSymbO contains three agents: a Designer that interprets free-form design intents and retrieves a semantically consistent scaffold, a Generator that synthesizes candidate microstructures in a disentangled latent space, and a Supervisor that provides fast property-aware feedback for iterative refinement. To move beyond the limitations of reproducing known samples from literature and training data, we further introduce symbolic-driven latent evolution, which applies programmable operators over disentangled latent factors to compose, modify, and refine structures at inference time. Extensive experiments demonstrate that (i) MetaSymbO improves structural validity by up to 34% in symmetry and nearly 98% in periodicity compared to state-of-the-art baselines; (ii) MetaSymbO achieves about 6-7% higher language-guidance scores while maintaining superior structure novelty compared to advanced reasoning LLMs; (iii) qualitative analyses confirm the effectiveness of symbolic logic operators in enabling programmable semantic alignment; and (iv) realworld case studies on auxetic, high-stiffness metamaterial design further validate its practical capability.

### [76] [End-to-End Evaluation and Governance of an EHR-Embedded AI Agent for Clinicians](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27309)
*Aaryan Shah,Andrew Hines,Alexia Downs,Denis Bajet,Paulius Mui,Fabiano Araujo,Laura Offutt,Aida Rutledge,Elizabeth Jimenez*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes and demonstrates a continuous governance framework for clinical AI systems, showing it is practical and improves system performance over time.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most clinical AI evaluations are done at a single point in time, which misses performance drift, real-world failures, and operational issues that arise after deployment. There is a need for a practical, end-to-end governance framework that enables ongoing oversight, improvement, and safety monitoring of AI used in clinical workflows.

Method: The authors design and deploy an end-to-end governance framework around Hyperscribe, an EHR-integrated AI agent that converts ambient clinical audio into structured chart updates. The framework includes: (1) rubric-based validation of AI outputs by clinicians, (2) controlled experiments to gate model/version changes before deployment, (3) live deployment feedback capture from users, (4) technical performance monitoring (latency, completion rates, reliability), and (5) cost tracking. Twenty clinicians created and applied detailed evaluation rubrics to 823 cases, and seven Hyperscribe versions were evaluated with this process during real-world use.

Result: Across seven Hyperscribe versions, median rubric scores improved from 84% to 95%. Over three months, 107 live feedback entries showed a shift from predominantly error reports (79%) and limited positive observations (14%) to fewer errors (30%) and more positive observations (45%), suggesting that engineering interventions guided by feedback addressed key failures. Operational metrics showed a median processing time of 8.1 seconds per audio segment and a 99.6% effective completion rate after implementing retry mechanisms to mitigate transient model errors.

Conclusion: A structured, continuous, multi-channel governance framework for clinical AI—combining rubric-based evaluation, controlled experimentation, real-time user feedback, technical monitoring, and cost tracking—can be feasibly implemented in practice and leads to measurable improvements in AI performance, reliability, and user perception over time.

Abstract: Clinical AI systems require not just point-in-time evaluation but continuous governance: the ongoing practice of monitoring, evaluating, iterating, and re-evaluating performance throughout deployment. We present an end-to-end framework of governance that integrates rubric validation, live deployment feedback, technical performance monitoring, and cost tracking, with controlled experimentation gating system changes before deployment. Applied to Hyperscribe, an EHR-embedded agent that converts ambient audio into structured chart updates, twenty clinicians authored 1,646 validated rubrics across 823 cases. Seven Hyperscribe versions were evaluated through controlled experiments, with median scores improving from 84% to 95%. Analysis of 107 live feedback entries over three months showed feedback composition shifting from 79% error reports and 14% positive observations to 30% errors and 45% positive observations as engineering interventions resolved failures. Median processing time per audio segment was 8.1 seconds with a 99.6% effective completion rate after retry mechanisms absorbed transient model errors. These results demonstrate that continuous, multi-channel governance of deployed clinical AI is both achievable and effective.

### [77] [Investigating More Explainable and Partition-Free Compositionality Estimation for LLMs: A Rule-Generation Perspective](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27340)
*Ziyao Xu,Cong Wang,Houfeng Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a new way to measure how compositional large language models (LLMs) are, by having them generate rule-like programs and then evaluating the complexity of these rules.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing compositional generalization tests only evaluate models by checking whether they can give correct outputs on novel combinations, which (a) ignores whether the model actually understands the underlying compositional structure and (b) depends heavily on dataset splits that may leak training combinations into the test set. The authors want a more explainable, robust, and principled way to estimate compositionality in LLMs.

Method: They introduce a rule-generation framework where an LLM is asked to generate an explicit program (set of rules) that maps inputs to outputs in a task. Using complexity-based theoretical tools, they then quantify how compositional the generated rules are, treating the program as an explicit hypothesis about the task structure rather than just testing outputs. They apply this framework to a string-to-grid task to probe the internal compositional behavior of several advanced LLMs.

Result: On the string-to-grid task, advanced LLMs can generate rule programs that reveal different patterns of compositionality and also clear deficiencies. The analysis shows that models vary in how systematically they compose rules and where they fail, yielding richer diagnostic information than standard compositional generalization benchmarks.

Conclusion: The rule-generation perspective provides a new avenue for estimating and analyzing compositionality in LLMs that overcomes key weaknesses of previous benchmarks—improving explainability by exposing explicit learned rules and avoiding reliance on fragile dataset partitions. This framework uncovers nuanced compositionality profiles and shortcomings of current LLMs, and can serve as a basis for more principled future evaluations.

Abstract: Compositional generalization tests are often used to estimate the compositionality of LLMs. However, such tests have the following limitations: (1) they only focus on the output results without considering LLMs' understanding of sample compositionality, resulting in explainability defects; (2) they rely on dataset partition to form the test set with combinations unseen in the training set, suffering from combination leakage issues. In this work, we propose a novel rule-generation perspective for compositionality estimation for LLMs. It requires LLMs to generate a program as rules for dataset mapping and provides estimates of the compositionality of LLMs using complexity-based theory. The perspective addresses the limitations of compositional generalization tests and provides a new way to analyze the compositionality characterization of LLMs. We conduct experiments and analysis of existing advanced LLMs based on this perspective on a string-to-grid task, and find various compositionality characterizations and compositionality deficiencies exhibited by LLMs.

The work suggests that future agentic frameworks should systematically integrate specialized predictive models rather than trying to solve everything via natural-language-centric pipelines.

Abstract: Agentic large language model systems have demonstrated strong capabilities. However, their reliance on language as the universal interface fundamentally limits their applicability to many real-world problems, especially in scientific domains where domain-specific foundation models have been developed to address specialized tasks beyond natural language. In this work, we introduce Eywa, a heterogeneous agentic framework designed to extend language-centric systems to a broader class of scientific foundation models. The key idea of Eywa is to augment domain-specific foundation models with a language-model-based reasoning interface, enabling language models to guide inference over non-linguistic data modalities. This design allows predictive foundation models, which are typically optimized for specialized data and tasks, to participate in higher-level reasoning and decision-making processes within agentic systems. Eywa can serve as a drop-in replacement for a single-agent pipeline (EywaAgent) or be integrated into existing multi-agent systems by replacing traditional agents with specialized agents (EywaMAS). We further investigate a planning-based orchestration framework in which a planner dynamically coordinates traditional agents and Eywa agents to solve complex tasks across heterogeneous data modalities (EywaOrchestra). We evaluate Eywa across a diverse set of scientific domains spanning physical, life, and social sciences. Experimental results demonstrate that Eywa improves performance on tasks involving structured and domain-specific data, while reducing reliance on language-based reasoning through effective collaboration with specialized foundation models.

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how people actually reason with common XAI explanations on tabular data, builds cognitive models of these reasoning strategies, and shows these models explain human decisions better than standard ML proxies, helping identify effective vs. ineffective explanation-use strategies.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although XAI is meant to help users understand and better use AI systems, user studies show that explanations often fail to improve decisions. Method: The authors focus on a forward-simulation task (predicting what an AI will decide) with tabular data and three explanation conditions: no explanation, feature importance, and feature attribution. (1) In a formative study, they elicit and categorize users’ reasoning strategies when using these different forms of XAI. (2) In a larger summative user study, they collect behavioral data (participants’ decisions) under these conditions. (3) They build cognitive models that explicitly implement the hypothesized reasoning strategies for each explanation type and fit these models to the human decision data. (4) They compare the fit of these cognitive models to simpler machine learning proxy models. Finally, they use the fitted cognitive models to simulate human-like behavior and explore hypotheses and research questions that would be expensive to test with real participants.

Result: The cognitive models that encode specific reasoning strategies align better with actual human decision patterns than baseline ML proxies. This indicates that the identified strategies capture key aspects of how humans use (or misuse) XAI explanations. The model comparisons reveal which strategies lead to more accurate anticipation of AI decisions and which are inefficient or systematically biased. The fitted models also serve as surrogates for human participants, enabling low-cost exploration of design and research questions about explanation usability.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that understanding and formally modeling human reasoning is critical for making XAI genuinely usable. Cognitive models of explanation use can "debug" users’ mental models of AI, highlight ineffective reasoning paths, and guide the design of explanations that better support real-world decision-making. Moreover, once validated, such models can stand in for users in early-stage research, allowing rapid hypothesis testing about explanation designs before running costly human studies.

Abstract: Explainable AI (XAI) aims to improve user understanding and decisions when using AI models. However, despite innovations in XAI, recent user evaluations reveal that this goal remains elusive. Understanding human cognition can help explain why users struggle to effectively use AI explanations. Focusing on reasoning on structured (tabular) data, we examined various reasoning strategies for different XAI methods (none, feature importance, feature attribution) in the decision task of anticipating AI decisions (i.e., forward simulation). We i) elicited reasoning strategies from a formative user study, and ii) collected decisions from a summative user study. Using cognitive modeling, we implemented the processes underlying each reasoning strategy and evaluated their alignment with human decision-making. We found that our models better fit human decisions than baseline machine learning proxies, providing insights into which reasoning strategies are (in)effective. We then demonstrate how the fitted model can be used to form hypotheses and investigate research questions that are costly to study with real human participants. This work contributes to debugging human understanding of XAI, informing the future development of more usable and interpretable AI explanations.

### [80] [Safe Bilevel Delegation (SBD): A Formal Framework for Runtime Delegation Safety in Multi-Agent Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27358)
*Yuan Sun*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Safe Bilevel Delegation (SBD), a formal framework for dynamically and safely delegating tasks to LLM sub-agents via bilevel optimization, with theoretical guarantees but no completed experiments yet.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLM agents are increasingly used in high-stakes domains, systems often rely on hierarchies of specialized sub-agents. Current approaches either fix multi-agent architectures at design time or offer only coarse guidelines, lacking a runtime mechanism to continually balance safety against efficiency as task context shifts. The authors aim to create a principled, adaptive way to control how much autonomy is given to sub-agents while guaranteeing probabilistic safety, and to clarify how accountability should be distributed along delegation chains.

Method: They formulate delegation as a bilevel optimization problem. The outer problem is a meta-weight network φ that maps context state s to a safety-efficiency trade-off weight λ(s) ∈ [0,1]. The inner problem optimizes a delegation policy π under a probabilistic safety constraint P(safe) ≥ 1−δ, effectively solving for actions and delegation structures that respect a required safety level. A continuous delegation degree α ∈ [0,1] encodes how much control is ceded to sub-agents versus retained by humans, enabling smooth interpolation between human-in-the-loop and full autonomy. They analyze the optimization using projected gradient descent on the inner problem and introduce an analytical framework for accountability propagation across multi-hop delegation in the hierarchy. They also outline instantiations of SBD in medical AI, financial risk control, and educational supervision, including how to define safety constraints and evaluation protocols, but these remain future empirical work.

Result: They derive three main theoretical results: (1) Safety Monotonicity: increasing the safety weight from the outer meta-network cannot make the inner policy less safe, i.e., it induces a weakly safer policy; (2) Inner Policy Convergence: under standard smoothness assumptions, projected gradient descent applied to the inner constrained optimization converges linearly to a solution; (3) Accountability Propagation bound: in multi-hop delegation chains, they provide a formal upper bound on how much responsibility can be assigned to each agent, ensuring a provable ceiling on per-agent accountability. They also specify how SBD would be operationalized in three domains (medical, financial, educational), but do not yet present empirical metrics or numerical outcomes.

Conclusion: Safe Bilevel Delegation offers a principled, runtime-controllable mechanism for balancing safety and efficiency in hierarchical LLM-based systems, with formal guarantees on safety monotonicity, optimization convergence, and bounded accountability allocation. The framework can adjust delegation levels continuously between human control and full autonomy using a learned meta-weight network. While the paper currently contributes theory and task-specific protocols, validating SBD empirically in real high-stakes domains remains future work, to be reported in subsequent versions.

Abstract: As large language model (LLM) agents are deployed in high-stakes environments, the question of how safely to delegate subtasks to specialized sub-agents becomes critical. Existing work addresses multi-agent architecture selection at design time or provides broad empirical guidelines, but neither provides a runtime mechanism that dynamically adjusts the safety-efficiency trade-off as task context changes during execution.
  We propose Safe Bilevel Delegation (SBD), a formal framework for runtime delegation safety in hierarchical multi-agent systems. SBD formulates task delegation as a bilevel optimization problem: an outer meta-weight network phi learns context-dependent safety-efficiency weights lambda(s) in [0,1]; an inner loop optimizes the delegation policy pi subject to a probabilistic safety constraint P(safe) >= 1-delta. The continuous delegation degree alpha in [0, 1] controls how much decision authority is transferred to each sub-agent, interpolating smoothly between full human override (alpha=0) and fully autonomous execution (alpha=1).
  We establish three theoretical results: (1) Safety Monotonicity--higher outer safety weight produces a weakly safer inner policy; (2) Inner Policy Convergence--projected gradient descent on the inner problem converges linearly under standard smoothness assumptions; (3) an Accountability Propagation bound that distributes responsibility across multi-hop delegation chains with a provable per-agent ceiling. We instantiate SBD in three high-stakes domains--medical AI (MIMIC-III), financial risk control (S and P 500), and educational agent supervision (ASSISTments)--specifying datasets, safety constraint sets, baselines, and evaluation protocols. This manuscript presents the formal framework and theoretical results in full; empirical validation following the protocols described herein is planned and will be reported in a forthcoming revision.

</details>

*Jean Martins,Leonid Mokrushin,Marin Orlic*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They design and release a SHACL-based validation framework (tio-shacl) that formally validates intents expressed with the TM Forum Intent Ontology, achieving complete coverage, cross-engine compatibility, and high validation accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

This missing step can lead to invalid, inconsistent, or ambiguous intents entering operational systems, risking misconfigurations and undermining trust in intent-based network management.

Method: They build a SHACL validation framework tailored to the TMF Intent Ontology, defining 56 node shapes and 69 property shapes that span all 15 ontology modules. They also design a reusable library of 25 parameterized SPARQL-based constraint components to express complex constraints. The framework introduces new validation patterns to handle recursive logical operators, quantity-based constraints, and dependencies between different expectations in an intent. They target complete coverage of the ontology vocabulary and test compatibility with three major SHACL engines, as well as validate against a curated corpus of 133 test cases.

Result: The resulting framework, tio-shacl, covers 100% of the TMF Intent Ontology v3.6.0 vocabulary (87 classes, 109 properties, and 72 functions). Conclusion: tio-shacl fills a critical gap in intent-based networking by offering the first comprehensive, formal SHACL-based validation framework for the TMF Intent Ontology. It enables automated, standards-aligned checking of network intents before admission, improving correctness and reliability of intent-driven network management, and it is made publicly available under an open-source MIT license for adoption and further extension by the community.

Abstract: Intent-based networking promises to revolutionize telecommunications network management by enabling operators to specify high-level goals rather than low-level configurations. The TM Forum Intent Ontology (tio) provides a standardized vocabulary for expressing network intents, yet lacks formal validation mechanisms to ensure intent correctness before its admission. We present tio-shacl, the first comprehensive SHACL (Shapes Constraint Language) validation framework for the TMF Intent Ontology. Our contribution includes 56 node shapes and 69 property shapes across all 15 tio v3.6.0 ontology modules, a reusable constraint library with 25 parameterized SPARQL-based constraint components, and novel validation patterns for recursive logical operators, quantity-based constraints, and cross-expectation relationships. We pursued 100% vocabulary coverage (87 classes, 109 properties, 72 functions), cross-implementation compatibility across three major SHACL engines, and validation accuracy on a corpus of 133 test cases. tio-shacl is publicly available under MIT license at https://github.com/EricssonResearch/tio-shacl and enables automated syntactic and semantic validation of network intents, addressing a critical gap in the field.

### [82] [Measurement Risk in Supervised Financial NLP: Rubric and Metric Sensitivity on JF-ICR](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27374)
*Sidi Chang,Peiying Zhu,Yuxiao Chen,Rongdong Chai*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Paper examines how sensitive financial NLP benchmarks are to rubric wording, metric choice, and ranking methods using a Japanese financial implicit-commitment recognition task.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Financial institutions increasingly rely on LLMs to interpret earnings calls and disclosures. Benchmark scores guide model selection, but are treated as objective because of fixed gold labels. The authors argue that this ignores measurement risk: evaluation outcomes can change substantially with small variations in rubrics, metrics, or aggregation rules, threatening the reliability of benchmark-based decisions.

Method: They use the Japanese Financial Implicit-Commitment Recognition (JF-ICR) benchmark: 253 test items evaluated with 4 frontier LLMs, 5 rubric variants, 3 temperature settings, and 5 ordinal metrics. They quantify how model-assigned labels change across rubrics, analyze which metrics are informative given the dataset’s skewed class distribution, and test how different ranking/aggregation procedures (Bradley–Terry, Borda, Ranked Pairs) affect model ordering, focusing on metrics that pass a metric-identifiability audit.

Result: 1) Rubric wording significantly affects model label assignments: agreement between rubrics R2 and R3 ranges from 70.0% to 83.4%, with most disagreements around the boundary between +1 and 0 (implicit commitment vs. neutral). 2) Some metrics become uninformative under the JF-ICR class distribution: within-one accuracy is overly lenient and dominated by the majority class, while worst-class accuracy is unstable due to only two items in the rarest class. Exact accuracy, macro-F1, and weighted kappa remain usable (“identifiable”) metrics. 3) After restricting to identifiable metrics, different ranking schemes (Bradley–Terry, Borda, Ranked Pairs) largely agree on model orderings, whereas using all five metrics produces ranking disagreements for closely performing models.

Conclusion: Gold labels alone do not guarantee objective or stable benchmark-based decisions in financial NLP. Evaluation outcomes depend materially on rubric wording, metric selection, and ranking/aggregation rules. The authors advocate a governance and reporting discipline: explicitly stress-test rubrics, audit which metrics are actually informative under the data distribution, and base leaderboard or deployment decisions only on metrics and ranking procedures that remain consistent under such audits, especially in high-stakes financial applications.

Abstract: As LLMs become credible readers of earnings calls, investor-relations Q\&A, guidance, and disclosure language, supervised financial NLP benchmarks increasingly function as decision evidence for model selection and deployment. A hidden assumption is that gold labels make such evidence objective. This assumption breaks down when the benchmark ruler itself is sensitive to rubric wording, metric choice, or aggregation policy. We study this measurement risk on Japanese Financial Implicit-Commitment Recognition (JF-ICR; a pinned 253-item test split x 4 frontier LLMs x 5 rubrics x 3 temperatures x 5 ordinal metrics). Three findings follow. First, rubric wording materially changes model-assigned labels: R2--R3 agreement ranges from 70.0% to 83.4%, with the dominant movement near the +1 / 0 implicit-commitment boundary. This pattern is consistent with a pragmatic-boundary interpretation, but is not a validated linguistic-causality claim because the present rubric variants confound semantics, examples, and verbosity. Second, not every metric remains informative under the JF-ICR class distribution. Within-one accuracy is too easy because near misses receive credit and the majority class dominates; worst-class accuracy is too noisy because the rarest class has only two examples. Exact accuracy, macro-F1, and weighted \k{appa} are therefore the identifiable metrics under our operational rule. Third, ranking claims become more defensible only after this metric-identifiability audit: Bradley--Terry, Borda, and Ranked Pairs agree on the identifiable metric subset, while the full five-metric sweep produces disagreement on the closest pair. The contribution is not a new leaderboard, but a reporting discipline for supervised financial benchmarks whose gold labels exist and whose evaluation ruler still requires governance.

### [83] [Robust Learning on Heterogeneous Graphs with Heterophily: A Graph Structure Learning Approach](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27387)
*Yihan Zhang,Ercan E. Kuruoglu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose HGUL, a unified framework for robust representation learning on heterogeneous, heterophilic graphs with structural noise, combining kNN-based graph construction, adaptive structure learning, and heterogeneous affinity learning to outperform existing methods and remain robust under noisy edges.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Heterogeneous graphs with heterophily better capture many real-world systems, but their learning performance suffers when graph connectivity is noisy or misleading. Existing works have mostly focused on either heterophily or denoising/robustness, not both together, leaving a gap in robust representation learning that can simultaneously handle heterophily and structural noise.

Method: They design HGUL, a unified framework with three modules: (1) a kNN-based graph construction module that rebuilds more reliable local neighborhoods from features; (2) a graph structure learning module that refines the adjacency matrix by adaptively down-weighting or removing noisy edges; and (3) a heterogeneous affinity learning module that constructs an extended affinity matrix at the class level using a polynomial graph kernel, capturing richer relationships across heterogeneous node types and labels.

Result: On multiple benchmark datasets, HGUL surpasses state-of-the-art methods when the input graphs are clean, and it preserves high performance even as artificial structural noise is added. Its accuracy degrades more slowly than baselines with increasing noise levels, showing improved robustness to noisy edges in heterogeneous, heterophilic graphs.

Conclusion: Jointly addressing heterophily and structural noise is crucial for learning on heterogeneous graphs. HGUL’s combination of kNN-based reconstruction, adaptive structure learning, and heterogeneous affinity modeling yields more robust node and class representations, leading to consistent performance gains and resilience to corrupted graph structures.

Abstract: Heterogeneous graphs with heterophily have emerged as a powerful abstraction for modeling complex real-world systems, where nodes of different types and labels interact in diverse and often non-homophilous ways. Despite recent advances, robust representation learning for such graphs remains largely unexplored, particularly in the presence of noisy or misleading connectivity. In this work, we investigate this problem and identify structural noise as a critical challenge that significantly degrades model performance. To address this issue, we propose a unified framework, Heterogeneous Graph Unified Learning (HGUL), which jointly handles heterophily and noisy graph structures. The framework consists of three complementary modules: a kNN-based graph construction module that recovers reliable local neighborhoods, a graph structure learning module that adaptively refines the adjacency by filtering noisy edges, and a heterogeneous affinity learning module that captures class-level relationships via an extended affinity matrix derived from a polynomial graph kernel. Extensive experiments on multiple datasets demonstrate that HGUL consistently outperforms existing methods on clean graphs and maintains strong robustness under varying levels of structural noise. The results further underscore the importance of jointly modeling heterophily and noise in heterogeneous graph learning.

The paper is motivated by the need to give strategic leaders and AI system designers a clear way to perceive and reason about these hybrid decision configurations.

Method: The paper develops a conceptual framework rather than an empirical model. It defines a leadership-facing spectrum of five archetypal configurations—Pure Human, Centaur (human-dominant with AI in the loop), Co-equal, Minotaur (AI-dominant with humans in the loop), and Pure AI—based on where leadership work occurs: problem framing, redirection, and answerability. It also introduces the concept of co-adaptability and situates the spectrum within the broader idea of heterogeneous teaming across varied human and non-human participants.

Result: The result is a structured spectrum that leaders can use to identify how a particular decision is actually being shaped, to notice when the configuration drifts (e.g., from Centaur to Minotaur), and to evaluate whether that configuration is appropriate for the decision’s stakes and context. The framework highlights specific risks such as misrecognition of where authority lies, ceremonial oversight, and counterproductive human involvement, and it links these to the distribution of power, responsibility, and trust in organizations.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that organizational futures and the governability of AI-infused decision-making will depend on leaders’ ability to detect and reason about the real configuration of human–AI collaboration in consequential decisions. By using the proposed spectrum and the notion of co-adaptability, leaders can better align decision configurations with the nature of the task, maintain meaningful accountability, and design AI deployments that produce trustworthy and inhabitably governed futures.

Abstract: What shapes a consequential decision when human and artificial intelligence work on it together? The answer is becoming harder to see. A decision may look human-led after AI has set the frame, or appear automated while human judgment still carries decisive force. This paper offers a leadership-facing spectrum to see those relationships within a bounded mandate: Pure Human, Centaur (human-dominant, with AI in the loop), Co-equal, Minotaur (AI-dominant, with humans in the loop), and Pure AI.
  The spectrum asks where leadership work occurs: who frames the problem, who redirects the work, and who can answer for what follows. The five positions are landmarks that help leaders recognize configurations as they layer, drift, or change in a single decision. The central risk is misrecognition: leaders may keep a human-centered story in place after decision-shaping authority has shifted elsewhere. They may believe oversight remains meaningful when it has become ceremonial, or keep humans in the loop when their involvement could make the decision worse. The framework introduces co-adaptability, the capacity of a configuration to improve as human and non-human participants adjust together, and places it within heterogeneous teaming, where participants may vary by number, substrate, model architecture, capability, speed, memory, and form of participation.
  The aim is practical: to help strategic leaders and those designing or deploying AI systems recognize the configuration at work, notice when it shifts, and judge whether it fits the decision before them. These configurations will shape how power, responsibility, and trust are distributed in organizational life. Whether the futures they help create remain governable and worth inhabiting will depend on leaders who can see, early enough, where and how consequential decisions are actually being shaped.

### [86] [PRTS: A Primitive Reasoning and Tasking System via Contrastive Representations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27472)
*Yang Zhang,Jiangyuan Zhao,Chenyou Fan,Fangzheng Yan,Tian Li,Haitong Tang,Sen Fu,Xuan'er Wu,Qizhen Weng,Weinan Zhang,Xiu Li,Chi Zhang,Chenjia Bai,Xuelong Li*

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing VLA models mainly use supervised behavior cloning, which treats learning as imitating demonstrations from static language-visual pairs and ignores that robot learning is inherently a goal-reaching, temporally extended process. This leads to weak understanding of temporal task progress and goal feasibility, especially for long-horizon and contact-rich tasks. The paper aims to inject explicit goal-reachability awareness into VLA pretraining so that the model can reason about whether a language-specified goal is physically attainable from a given state-action sequence, improving planning and control.

Method: The authors propose PRTS (Primitive Reasoning and Tasking System), a Vision-Language-Action foundation model trained via Goal-Conditioned Reinforcement Learning on offline data. Language instructions are treated as goals, and a contrastive RL objective is used so that in a learned embedding space, the inner product between state-action embeddings and goal embeddings approximates the log-discounted goal occupancy, i.e., the probability of eventually reaching the goal from the current state-action. This supervision is extracted directly from offline trajectories without explicit reward labels. A role-aware causal mask integrates this goal-reachability learning into a vision-language backbone with minimal additional cost beyond standard behavior cloning.

Result: PRTS is pretrained on 167B tokens covering diverse manipulation and embodied reasoning data. It achieves state-of-the-art performance on several benchmarks: LIBERO, LIBERO-Pro, LIBERO-Plus, SimplerEnv, and a real-world suite of 14 complex manipulation tasks. The gains are particularly large for long-horizon tasks, contact-rich interactions, and zero-shot novel-instruction scenarios, demonstrating strong generalization and planning capability.

Conclusion: Recasting VLA pretraining as goal-conditioned reinforcement learning with contrastive supervision enables the model to encode goal reachability directly in its representations. This bridges semantic understanding of language instructions with temporal task progress, significantly improving execution success and long-horizon planning for general-purpose robotic policies while incurring little extra computational overhead compared to vanilla behavior cloning.

Abstract: Vision-Language-Action (VLA) models advance robotic control via strong visual-linguistic priors. However, existing VLAs predominantly frame pretraining as supervised behavior cloning, overlooking the fundamental nature of robot learning as a goal-reaching process that requires understanding temporal task progress. We present \textbf{PRTS} (\textbf{P}rimitive \textbf{R}easoning and \textbf{T}asking \textbf{S}ystem), a VLA foundation model that reformulates pretraining through Goal-Conditioned Reinforcement Learning. By treating language instructions as goals and employing contrastive reinforcement learning, PRTS learns a unified embedding space where the inner product of state-action and goal embeddings approximates the log-discounted goal occupancy, the probability of reaching the language-specified goal from the current state-action, quantitatively assessing physical feasibility beyond static semantic matching. PRTS draws this dense goal-reachability supervision directly from offline trajectories without reward annotations, and folds it into the VLM backbone via a role-aware causal mask, incurring negligible overhead over vanilla behavior cloning. This paradigm endows the high-level reasoning system with intrinsic goal reachability awareness, bridging semantic reasoning and temporal task progress, and further benefits goal-conditioned action prediction. Pretrained on 167B tokens of diverse manipulation and embodied-reasoning data, PRTS reaches state-of-the-art performance on LIBERO, LIBERO-Pro, LIBERO-Plus, SimplerEnv, and a real-world suite of 14 complex tasks, with particularly substantial gains on long-horizon, contact-rich, and zero-shot novel-instruction settings, confirming that injecting goal-reachability awareness significantly improves both execution success and long-horizon planning of general-purpose robotic foundation policies.

</details>


### [87] [Belief-Guided Inference Control for Large Language Model Services via Verifiable Observations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27536)
*Wenhao Yuan,Chenchen Lin,Jian Chen,Jinfeng Xu,Shuo Yang,Edith Cheuk Han Ngai*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They introduce Veroic, a POMDP-based framework that uses lightweight verifiable signals about an LLM’s response to adaptively decide whether to accept the cheap default answer or pay for more expensive computation, improving the trade-off between quality and cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Black-box LLM APIs often provide only limited observability of answer quality at inference time, and stronger checks or more powerful inference routes (e.g., more calls, ensembles, verifiers) are expensive. Systems need a principled way to decide, per request, whether the default low-cost response is reliable enough or if they should spend more computation to reduce risk, under an overall budget. Existing heuristics don’t fully model partial observability, uncertainty, and sequential budget constraints.

Method: They formulate request-time control as a partially observable Markov decision process (POMDP) that captures hidden response reliability and budget coupling over time. From each input-output pair, they construct a lightweight “verifiable observation channel” that aggregates heterogeneous quality signals (e.g., self-consistency, verifier scores, simple checks) into a belief state over latent reliability. A budget-aware control policy then uses this belief state to choose between returning the default low-cost output or invoking a higher-cost inference pathway for that request.

Result: On multiple and diverse tasks, Veroic yields better quality-vs-cost trade-offs than comparative methods, more accurate and calibrated estimation of response risk, and more stable performance for long-horizon settings where many sequential decisions and budgets interact.

Conclusion: Modeling adaptive inference as a POMDP over latent reliability, and using lightweight verifiable observations to maintain a belief state, allows Veroic to control when to allocate extra computation more effectively than heuristic baselines, leading to improved reliability, calibration, and robustness under computational budgets in black-box LLM services.

Abstract: In black-box large language model (LLM) services, response reliability is often only partially observable at decision time, while stronger inference pathways incur substantial computational cost, inducing a budgeted sequential decision problem: for each request, the system should decide whether the default low-cost response is sufficiently reliable or whether additional computation should be allocated to improve response quality. In this paper, we propose \textbf{Ver}ifiable \textbf{O}bservations for Risk-aware \textbf{I}nference \textbf{C}ontrol (\textsc{Veroic}), a framework for adaptive inference control in black-box LLM settings, which formulates request-time control as a \textit{partially observable Markov decision process} to capture partial observability and sequential budget coupling. It constructs a lightweight verifiable observation channel from the input-output pair by aggregating heterogeneous quality signals into a belief state over latent response reliability, which is then used by a budget-aware policy to decide whether to return the default output or trigger a higher-cost inference pathway. Experiments on diverse tasks show that \textsc{Veroic} achieves improved quality-cost trade-offs, stronger risk estimation and calibration, and more robust long-horizon inference control than competitive baselines.

### [88] [In-Context Examples Suppress Scientific Knowledge Recall in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27540)
*Chaemin Jang,Woojin Park,Hyeok Yun,Dongman Lee,Jihee Kim*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how in-context examples affect large language models’ ability to recover hidden scientific structure and finds that examples often push models from knowledge-based reasoning to mere pattern fitting.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Scientific reasoning typically requires inferring latent structure, such as hidden parameters or laws, rather than just matching observed patterns. Although LLMs can use scientific formulas they have learned during pretraining, it is unclear how providing in-context examples changes the balance between using that internal knowledge versus just fitting patterns in the given examples.

Method: The authors construct 60 tasks involving latent structure recovery across five scientific domains (e.g., chemistry, economics). They evaluate four different LLMs over 6,000 trials. For each task, they compare model behavior with and without in-context examples, where the examples themselves are generated from the correct underlying formulas. They then measure how the presence of examples alters whether the model uses knowledge-driven derivations or falls back on empirical pattern matching.

Result: They find that adding in-context examples systematically displaces the models’ reliance on their pretrained scientific knowledge. Even when examples are perfectly consistent with the correct formulas, the models pay less attention to those formulas and more to surface-level patterns. This displacement effect is robust across all tested domains, models, and tasks, though its impact on task accuracy varies: it can hurt, not change, or even superficially improve performance depending on how good the pattern-fitting strategy is relative to knowledge-based reasoning for that specific task.

Conclusion: In-context examples do not reliably reinforce LLMs’ internal scientific knowledge and can instead push models away from knowledge-driven reasoning toward empirical curve fitting. As a result, practitioners using LLMs for scientific applications should be cautious: adding worked examples or demonstrations might unintentionally undermine the very kind of structured, theory-based reasoning they aim to elicit.

Abstract: Scientific reasoning rarely stops at what is directly observable; it often requires uncovering hidden structure from data. From estimating reaction constants in chemistry to inferring demand elasticities in economics, this latent structure recovery is what distinguishes scientific reasoning from curve fitting. Large language models (LLMs) can often recall and apply relevant scientific formulas, but we show that this ability is surprisingly easy to suppress. We show that adding in-context examples makes models rely less on pretrained domain knowledge, even when those examples are generated by the very same formula. Rather than reinforcing knowledge-driven derivation, examples shift computation toward empirical pattern fitting. We document this knowledge displacement on 60 latent structure recovery tasks across five scientific domains, 6,000 trials, and four models. This displacement is consistent across domains, but its accuracy consequences depend on how the displaced strategy compares to the one that replaces it: the same shift can lower accuracy, leave it unchanged, or appear to improve it. In all cases, however, the model shifts away from knowledge-driven reasoning. For practitioners deploying LLMs on scientific tasks, the message is cautionary: in-context examples may displace, rather than reinforce, the knowledge they are intended to support.

Abstract: Automatically generating interactive 3D indoor scenes from natural language is crucial for virtual reality, gaming, and embodied AI. However, existing LLM-based approaches often suffer from spatial errors and collisions, in part because common scene representations-raw coordinates or verbose code-are difficult for models to reason about 3D spatial relationships and physical constraints. We propose SpatialGrammar, a domain-specific language that represents gravity-aligned indoor layouts as BEV grid placements with deterministic compilation to valid 3D geometry, enabling verifiable constraint checking. Building on this representation, we develop (1) SG-Agent, a closed-loop system that uses compiler feedback to iteratively refine scenes and enforce collision constraints, and (2) SG-Mini, a 104M-parameter model trained entirely on compiler-validated synthetic data. Across 159 test scenes spanning five scenarios of different complexity, SG-Agent improves spatial fidelity and physical plausibility over prior methods, while SG-Mini performs competitively against larger LLM-based baselines on single-shot generation scenarios.

### [90] [Trace-Level Analysis of Information Contamination in Multi-Agent Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27586)

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper investigates how injected uncertainty in representations of heterogeneous artifacts affects the behavior, reliability, and costs of structured LLM agent workflows, and proposes a taxonomy and measurement framework for such “contamination.”


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLM agents increasingly operate over complex workflows and heterogeneous artifacts, errors and uncertainty can propagate in non-obvious ways: small representational issues can trigger large changes in planning, tool use, and intermediate state, without being caught by standard verification methods. There is a need to systematically understand and measure how such contamination manifests, how it alters execution traces, and why conventional guardrails often miss it, in order to design more robust and cost-effective agent systems.

Method: The authors model uncertainty as controllable perturbations applied to artifact-derived representations (from PDFs, spreadsheets, slides, etc.). They then run fixed, instrumented agent workflows on GAIA tasks under both clean and perturbed conditions, logging detailed traces of plans, tool calls, and intermediate state for 614 paired runs across three language models. By comparing trace divergence between clean and perturbed runs, they quantify and categorize different contamination patterns and analyze associated operational metrics (e.g., path length, rerouting, early stopping). They also test existing verification and guardrail mechanisms to assess whether they detect or block contaminated trajectories.

Result: Experiments on 32 GAIA tasks and 614 paired runs reveal that workflow structure and answer correctness are often decoupled: workflows can diverge significantly yet still yield correct answers, or they can appear structurally similar while producing incorrect outputs. The study identifies three main contamination manifestations—silent semantic corruption, behavioral detours with eventual recovery, and combined structural disruption—and links them to specific control-flow signatures such as rerouting between tools, extended or looping execution, and premature termination. The results further show that widely used verification and guardrail strategies frequently fail to detect these forms of contamination, despite noticeable trace changes and cost impacts.

Conclusion: Uncertainty in artifact representations can subtly or dramatically contaminate structured LLM workflows, affecting control flow, intermediate reasoning, and costs in ways that are not reliably captured by correctness checks or standard guardrails. The paper proposes a formal taxonomy of contamination types and a trace-based framework for detecting and localizing them, demonstrating that structural trace analysis yields insights beyond final-answer evaluation. These findings suggest that robust agent design should incorporate targeted verification focused on workflow traces, defensive patterns that anticipate specific contamination types, and cost-aware monitoring of anomalous execution behavior.

Abstract: Reasoning over heterogeneous artifacts (PDFs, spreadsheets, slide decks, etc.) increasingly occurs within structured agent workflows that iteratively extract, transform, and reference external information. In these workflows, uncertainty is not merely an input-quality issue: it can redirect decomposition and routing decisions, reshape intermediate state, and produce qualitatively different execution trajectories. We study this phenomenon by treating uncertainty as a controlled variable: we inject structured perturbations into artifact-derived representations, execute fixed workflows under comprehensive logging, and quantify contamination via trace divergence in plans, tool invocations, and intermediate state. Across 614 paired runs on 32 GAIA tasks with three different language models, we find a decoupling: workflows may diverge substantially yet recover correct answers, or remain structurally similar while producing incorrect outputs. We characterize three manifestation types: silent semantic corruption, behavioral detours with recovery, and combined structural disruption and their control-flow signatures (rerouting, extended execution, early termination). We measure operational costs and characterize why commonly used verification guardrails fail to intercept contamination. We contribute (i) a formal taxonomy of contamination manifestations in structured workflows, (ii) a trace-based measurement framework for detecting and localizing contamination across agent interactions, and (iii) empirical evidence with implications for targeted verification, defensive design, and cost control.

MEDS involves 28,000 personas from 14 LLMs (from families like Mistral, Qwen, DeepSeek, Granite, Phi and Grok) shadowing either humans or AI assistants. Each record/shadow includes a set of prompts along with psychological/sociodemographic persona metadata and four types of math tasks: (i) open math interview, (ii) three psychometric tests about math perceptions with explanations, (iii) cognitive networks capturing math attitudes, and (iv) 18 high-school math test questions together with their reasoning and confidence scores. MEDS differs from traditional score-only math benchmarks because it integrates concepts of self-efficacy, math anxiety, and cognitive network science besides math proficiency scores. ### [92] [WaferSAGE: Large Language Model-Powered Wafer Defect Analysis via Synthetic Data Generation and Rubric-Guided Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27629)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Semiconductor manufacturing suffers from data scarcity and label noise in wafer defect datasets, yet accurate and explainable defect analysis is crucial for yield improvement. Existing large proprietary models are hard to deploy on-premise due to privacy, cost, and infrastructure constraints, and they are not tailored to specialized industrial visual understanding tasks like wafer defect VQA. The paper aims to build a domain-specialized, compact model that can be deployed privately while matching or surpassing large models on wafer defect understanding.

Method: 1) Data preparation: Start from limited labeled wafer maps, apply clustering-based cleaning to reduce label noise. 2) Synthetic supervision: Use vision-language models to generate rich defect descriptions, convert them into structured evaluation rubrics (criteria covering defect types, spatial distribution, morphology, and root causes). Use these rubrics to synthesize diverse VQA pairs that probe the different aspects of defects. 3) Evaluation framework: Define a dual assessment system combining rule-based metrics with LLM-Judge scores, and align them using Bayesian optimization for stable automatic evaluation. 4) Model training: Train a 4B-parameter Qwen3-VL model with curriculum-based reinforcement learning using Group Sequence Policy Optimization (GSPO), where rewards are aligned with the structured rubrics so the model learns to answer wafer-related questions in a graded, curriculum fashion.

Result: The resulting 4B-parameter Qwen3-VL model trained within the WaferSAGE framework attains an LLM-Judge score of 6.493 on wafer defect VQA, which is close to the 7.149 score of the much larger proprietary Gemini-3-Flash model. This demonstrates strong performance despite much smaller scale and fully on-premise deployment feasibility. The experiments show that incorporating rubric-driven synthetic data and optimized evaluation significantly boosts performance on domain-specific industrial visual understanding tasks.

Conclusion: WaferSAGE shows that small, domain-specialized vision-language models can approach and sometimes surpass large proprietary models on narrow industrial tasks such as wafer defect VQA. By combining clustering-based data cleaning, rubric-guided synthetic VQA generation, a dual assessment framework, and curriculum RL via GSPO, the approach yields a practical, privacy-preserving, and cost-effective solution suitable for on-premise deployment in semiconductor manufacturing, offering a template for similar applications in other data-scarce, high-stakes industrial domains.

Abstract: We present WaferSAGE, a framework for wafer defect visual question answering using small vision-language models. To address data scarcity in semiconductor manufacturing, we propose a three-stage synthesis pipeline incorporating structured rubric generation for precise evaluation. Starting from limited labeled wafer maps, we employ clustering-based cleaning to filter label noise, then generate comprehensive defect descriptions using vision-language models, which are converted into structured evaluation rubrics criteria. These rubrics guide the synthesis of VQA pairs, ensuring coverage across defect type identification, spatial distribution, morphology, and root cause analysis.
  Our dual assessment framework aligns rule-based metrics with LLM-Judge scores via Bayesian optimization, enabling reliable automated evaluation. Through curriculum-based reinforcement learning with Group Sequence Policy Optimization (GSPO) and rubric-aligned rewards, our 4B-parameter Qwen3-VL model achieves a 6.493 LLM-Judge score, closely approaching Gemini-3-Flash (7.149) while enabling complete on-premise deployment. We demonstrate that small models with domain-specific training can surpass proprietary large models in specialized industrial visual understanding, offering a viable path for privacy-preserving, cost-effective deployment in semiconductor manufacturing.
