### [93] [Political Bias Audits of LLMs Capture Sycophancy to the Inferred Auditor](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27633)
*Petter Törnberg,Michelle Schimmel*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that standard tests of political bias in LLMs partially measure sycophancy: models adjust their political answers based on who they think is asking, so bias can’t be treated as a fixed ideology.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing audits say frontier LLMs are left-leaning, but separate work shows LLMs are sycophantic and adapt to user cues. It is unclear whether measured political bias reflects fixed ideological leanings, user accommodation, or both. The authors want to disentangle these effects and understand political bias as an interaction between model and inferred interlocutor.

Method: They run a large factorial experiment on six frontier LLMs using three major political-audit instruments (Political Compass Test, Pew Political Typology, and 1,540 partisan-benchmarked Pew American Trends Panel items). The only experimental manipulation is the stated identity of the question-asker (e.g., baseline/neutral, conservative Republican, progressive Democrat). They collect 30,990 model responses, then code each answer as closer to Democrats or Republicans and compare shifts in response distributions across asker identities. They also directly ask models who they think the default asker is and what answers that person would expect.

Result: In a neutral baseline, all six models appear left-leaning. When the asker is described as a conservative Republican, models’ responses shift markedly to the right: the share of Democrat-aligned responses drops by 28–62 percentage points, and all models end up right of center. A symmetric progressive-Democrat cue produces only small changes; the rightward shift under conservative cues is about 8 times larger than the leftward shift under progressive cues. When queried about the default user, models describe an auditor/researcher/academic and predict that this person expects the Democrat-coded answer about 75% of the time, similar to behavior under an explicit progressive cue.

Conclusion: Measured political bias in LLMs is not simply a fixed ideological orientation. Standard single-prompt audits are picking up an interaction between model behavior and its (often implicit) assumptions about who is asking and what they expect. Political bias is thus a context-dependent response profile that must be characterized across realistic user identities rather than as a single point on an ideological spectrum.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are commonly evaluated for political bias based on their responses to fixed questionnaires, which typically place frontier models on the political left. A parallel literature shows that LLMs are sycophantic: they adapt their answers to the views, identities, and expectations of the user. We show that these findings are linked: standard political-bias audits partly capture sycophantic accommodation to the inferred auditor. We employ a factorial experiment across three major audit instruments--the Political Compass Test, the Pew Political Typology, and 1,540 partisan-benchmarked Pew American Trends Panel items--administered to six frontier LLMs while varying only the asker's stated identity (N = 30,990 responses). At baseline, all six models lean left. When the asker identifies as a conservative Republican, responses shift sharply: the share of items closer to Democrats falls by 28-62 percentage points, and all six models move right of center. A mirror-image progressive-Democrat cue produces little change; rightward accommodation is 8.0$\times$ larger than leftward. When asked who the default asker is, models identify an auditor, researcher, or academic; when asked what answer that asker expects, they select the Democrat-coded option 75% of the time, nearly the rate under an explicit progressive cue. These patterns are inconsistent with a purely fixed model ideology and indicate that single-prompt audits capture an interaction between model and inferred interlocutor. Political bias in LLMs is therefore not a fixed point on an ideological scale but a response profile that must be mapped across realistic interlocutors.

### [94] [Generative structure search for efficient and diverse discovery of molecular and crystal structures](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27636)

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents Generative Structure Search (GSS), a framework that unifies diffusion-based generative modeling with random structure search to efficiently discover stable and metastable molecular and crystalline structures, significantly reducing sampling cost and extending to out-of-distribution compositions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Discovering stable and metastable structures is crucial in molecular and materials science, but traditional search over high-dimensional energy landscapes is computationally expensive. Deep generative models can sample structures efficiently but are biased by training data and often miss rare yet physically important minima. There is a need for a method that combines data-driven priors with explicit physical guidance to explore energy landscapes more effectively and uncover a broader set of relevant structures.

Method: The authors propose Generative Structure Search (GSS), a unified sampling framework that views diffusion-based generation and random structure search (RSS) as two extremes of a single process. GSS uses learned score fields from diffusion-like models as data-driven priors and combines them with physical forces derived from energy evaluations (e.g., from quantum chemistry or interatomic potentials). By jointly driving the sampling with both the learned scores and physical energy gradients, GSS balances prior-guided proposals with energy-guided refinement to explore local minima more efficiently. The framework is applied to both molecular and crystalline systems and evaluated on and beyond the training distribution.

Result: GSS efficiently recovers a diverse set of metastable structures for molecular and crystalline systems. Compared to standard random structure search, GSS achieves similar or better coverage of relevant minima with more than an order-of-magnitude (over tenfold) reduction in sampling cost. It also maintains effectiveness for compositions not seen during training, indicating robustness and some degree of generalization beyond the training data distribution.

Conclusion: GSS provides a physically grounded generative search strategy that effectively integrates data-driven priors from deep generative models with explicit energy-based exploration. This unified framework bridges diffusion generation and random structure search, yielding significantly more sample-efficient discovery of stable and metastable structures. The approach overcomes limitations of purely data-driven generative models, enabling discovery of structures that lie beyond the support of the training data and offering a promising direction for accelerated molecular and materials design.

Abstract: Predicting stable and metastable structures is central to molecular and materials discovery, but remains limited by the cost of searching high-dimensional energy landscapes. Deep generative models offer efficient structure sampling, yet their outputs remain shaped by training data and can underexplore minima that are rare but physically relevant. We introduce generative structure search (GSS), a unified framework that formulates diffusion-based generation and random structure search (RSS) as limiting regimes of a common sampling process driven by learned score fields and physical forces. Coupling these drivers lets GSS use data priors to accelerate sampling while retaining energy-guided exploration of local minima. Across molecular and crystalline systems, GSS recovers diverse metastable structures with more than tenfold lower sampling cost than RSS for broad coverage and remains effective for compositions outside the training distribution. The results establish a physically grounded generative search strategy for discovering structures beyond the reach of data-driven sampling alone.

### [96] [From Context to Skills: Can Language Models Learn from Context Skillfully?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27660)
*Shuzheng Si,Haozhe Zhao,Yu Lei,Qingyi Wang,Dingwei Chen,Zhitong Wang,Zhenhailong Wang,Kangyang Luo,Zheng Wang,Gang Chen,Fanchao Qi,Minjia Zhang,Maosong Sun*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Ctx2Skill, a self-evolving multi-agent framework that automatically extracts and refines natural-language skills from context to improve language models’ context learning, without human labels or external feedback.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing language models often fail when tasks require reasoning over long, complex contexts that go beyond their internal (parametric) knowledge. A promising solution is to let models learn directly from a given context by extracting reusable rules or procedures as natural-language "skills." However, this is hard in practice because (1) manually annotating such skills for long, technical contexts is extremely expensive, and (2) there is no obvious automatic signal that tells us whether an automatically constructed skill is actually helpful for solving tasks. The paper is motivated by the need for an autonomous system that can discover, refine, and validate such context-specific skills without costly human supervision or external feedback.

Method: The paper proposes Ctx2Skill, a self-evolving multi-agent framework. It runs a self-play loop among several agents: (1) a Challenger that generates probing tasks and evaluation rubrics from the context; (2) a Reasoner that tries to solve those tasks using an evolving set of natural-language skills; and (3) a neutral Judge that provides binary feedback (success/failure) on the Reasoner’s solutions. When failures occur, two more agents—Proposer and Generator—analyze these failure cases to synthesize new or refined skills for both the Challenger and Reasoner. This continual loop supports automated skill discovery and refinement based solely on internal interaction outcomes. To avoid adversarial collapse, where the Challenger generates overly extreme tasks and the Reasoner learns over-specialized skills that do not generalize, the framework introduces Cross-time Replay. This mechanism searches across historical skill sets and identifies the one that best balances performance across a representative set of tasks, stabilizing and generalizing skill evolution. The resulting skill set is model-agnostic and can be injected as prompts or tools into arbitrary LMs.

Result: On four context learning tasks from the CL-bench benchmark, plugging the skills produced by Ctx2Skill into various backbone language models leads to consistent improvements in task solving rates. These empirical results indicate that autonomously discovered skills enhance LMs’ ability to perform context learning across different models and task types, without requiring manual annotations or external feedback signals.

Conclusion: Ctx2Skill demonstrates that language models can autonomously evolve a repertoire of context-specific natural-language skills through multi-agent self-play, internal feedback, and cross-time replay. This process yields robust, generalizable skills that, when integrated into diverse LMs, improve their ability to reason over complex contexts beyond their parametric knowledge. The framework offers a scalable, label-free pathway for enhancing context learning and suggests a broader direction for self-improving LM systems driven by internally generated tasks and feedback.

Abstract: Many real-world tasks require language models (LMs) to reason over complex contexts that exceed their parametric knowledge. This calls for context learning, where LMs directly learn relevant knowledge from the given context. An intuitive solution is inference-time skill augmentation: extracting the rules and procedures from context into natural-language skills. However, constructing such skills for context learning scenarios faces two challenges: the prohibitive cost of manual skill annotation for long, technically dense contexts, and the lack of external feedback for automated skill construction, since there is no automatic signal to tell whether a proposed skill is helpful. In this paper, we propose Ctx2Skill, a self-evolving framework that autonomously discovers, refines, and selects context-specific skills without human supervision or external feedback. At its core, a multi-agent self-play loop has a Challenger that generates probing tasks and rubrics, a Reasoner that attempts to solve them guided by an evolving skill set, and a neutral Judge that provides binary feedback. Crucially, both the Challenger and the Reasoner evolve through accumulated skills: dedicated Proposer and Generator agents analyze failure cases and synthesize them into targeted skill updates for both sides, enabling automated skill discovery and refinement. To prevent adversarial collapse caused by increasingly extreme task generation and over-specialized skill accumulation, we further introduce a Cross-time Replay mechanism that identifies the skill set achieving the best balance across representative cases for the Reasoner side, ensuring robust and generalizable skill evolution. The resulting skills can be plugged into any language model to obtain better context learning capability. Evaluated on four context learning tasks from CL-bench, Ctx2Skill consistently improves solving rates across backbone models.

</details>


### [97] [Fairness for distribution network operations and planning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27669)
*Pedro F. C. de Carvalho,Zijie Liu,Md Umar Hashmi,Dirk Van Hertem*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper surveys fairness concepts and metrics in distribution network planning and operation, focusing on how different fairness definitions affect stakeholders, optimization complexity, and the price of fairness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As fairness becomes a central goal in power distribution networks, planners face multiple, sometimes conflicting, fairness notions and metrics. These choices influence social outcomes, stakeholder satisfaction, and the efficiency losses captured by the price of fairness, but the landscape is fragmented and technically complex. A structured overview is needed to guide transparent and consistent decision-making.

Method: The study conducts a conceptual and methodological review of fairness notions (e.g., egalitarian, merit-based) and their associated quantitative metrics used in distribution network planning and operational resource allocation problems. It examines how these metrics are mathematically formulated (from linear to nonlinear programming) and how they integrate into optimization frameworks for DN planning and operation.

Result: The paper maps existing fairness notions and corresponding metrics, classifies them by their mathematical properties and optimization complexity, and analyzes how each affects stakeholders and the efficiency–fairness trade-off, expressed as the price of fairness. It highlights which metrics are more tractable and where implementation challenges arise.

Conclusion: Different fairness definitions lead to different trade-offs among stakeholders and different levels of computational complexity in DN planning and operation. By systematizing fairness notions, metrics, and their optimization implications, the paper provides guidance to enable more consistent, transparent, and practically applicable fairness-aware DN planning and operational decisions.

Abstract: The incorporation of fairness into the distribution network (DN) planning and operation has become a key goal of recent studies. The cost of implementing fairness, denominated the price of fairness (PoF), covers the efficiency that is renounced for attaining social cohesion through fair outcomes. Locational disparity makes fairness schemes emerge to level the consumers playing field. However, fairness encompasses a range of notions. From egalitarian to merit-based criteria, various metrics are implemented as a tool for measuring equitable utility distribution. These have different mathematical complexities, from linear to non-linear programming cases, which affect their overall applicability. Hence, this study compiles the overarching fairness notions and metrics, reviewing how these affect stakeholders and the inherent mathematical optimisation in resource allocation problems. The aim is to support consistent and transparent planning and decision-making within DN operations.

### [98] [The TEA Nets framework combines AI and cognitive network science to model targets, events and actors in text](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27673)
*Sebastiano Franchini,Alexis Carrillo,Edoardo Sebastiano De Duro,Riccardo Improta,Ali Aghazadeh Ardebili,Massimo Stella*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents Target-Event-Agent Networks (TEA Nets), a Python-based framework for extracting and analyzing subject–verb–object structures to study emotions and semantics in text, validated on conspiracy and psychotherapy corpora and LLM outputs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Understanding how agents, actions, and targets interact in narratives is central to analyzing emotions, semantics, and ideology, but existing NLP tools often lack interpretability and cognitive grounding. The authors aim to build a cognitively motivated, transparent framework that can uncover how different texts (e.g., conspiratorial narratives, human vs. LLM therapy sessions) structure agency and emotion, enabling fine-grained comparison across corpora.

Method: They define Target-Event-Agent Networks (TEA Nets), which computationally extract triplets of Agents (subjects), Events (verbs), and Targets (objects) from text and represent them as networks. Implemented as an open-source Python library, TEA Nets are applied in three case studies: (1) the LOCO conspiracy corpus to compare high- vs. low-conspiracy narratives; (2) HOPE psychotherapy transcripts (human therapists/patients); and (3) CounseLLMe transcripts generated by LLMs (Claude 3 Haiku and GPT-3.5). They then quantify link frequencies and emotional properties (e.g., anger, sadness intensity) against random baselines and between groups, using statistical tests (z-scores, U tests).

Result: In the LOCO conspiracy corpus, high-conspiracy narratives showed personal pronouns ("I", "you", "we") being linked to the same actions about twice as often as in low-similarity conspiracy narratives. High-conspiracy texts preferentially connected person-focused elements ("you", "people") via anger-eliciting actions above a random baseline (z = 2.63, p < .05), while low-similarity narratives focused more on scientific actors ("researcher", "scientist"). In the psychotherapy datasets, both humans and LLMs (Claude 3 Haiku, GPT-3.5) used sad words more frequently than expected by chance when expressing feelings, but Claude 3 Haiku expressed sadness with significantly lower emotional intensity than humans (U = 1243.5, p = .036).

Conclusion: TEA Nets can successfully recover interpretable Agent–Event–Target structures and associated emotional and semantic patterns across diverse corpora. The framework reveals systematic differences in how conspiracy vs. non-conspiracy narratives and humans vs. LLMs distribute agency and emotion. These findings suggest TEA Nets are a powerful, cognitively grounded, and transparent tool for narrative, emotion, and semantic-frame analysis, with particular application to studying conspiracy thinking and training psychotherapy using LLM-simulated patients.

Abstract: We introduce Target-Event-Agent Networks (TEA Nets) as a computational framework to extract subjects (``Agents"), verbs (``Events"), and objects (``Targets") from texts. Grounded in cognitive network science and artificial intelligence, TEA Nets are implemented as an open-source Python library. We test TEA Nets in three case studies, demonstrating the framework's ability to perform interpretable emotion detection, semantic frame analyses, and linguistic inquiries across conspiracy texts and textual responses generated by LLMs. In the LOCO conspiracy corpus, TEA Nets revealed that highly conspiratorial narratives (4,227 texts) linked personal pronouns (``I", ``you", ``we") with the same actions twice as frequently as low-similarity conspiracy narratives. High-conspiracy narratives connected person-focused elements (``you", ``people") through actions eliciting anger above the random baseline ($z = 2.63, p < .05$), a trend absent in low-similarity conspiracy narratives, which emphasized scientific actors (``researcher", ``scientist"). In the HOPE and CounseLLMe datasets of 212 (human) and 200 (LLM-based) psychotherapy transcripts, respectively, TEA Nets highlighted emotional differences. When expressing feelings, Claude 3 Haiku, GPT-3.5, and humans used sad words with higher frequency than random expectations but Haiku expressed sadness with lower emotional intensity than humans ($U = 1243.5, p = .036$). We discuss these differences in the context of psychotherapy training on LLM-simulated patients. Our results show that Target-Event-Agent Networks can extract relevant emotional, syntactic, and semantic insights from narratives, opening new avenues for text analysis with cognitive network science.

### [99] [When Agents Evolve, Institutions Follow](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27691)
*Chao Fei,Hongcheng Guo,Yanghua Xiao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper treats political institutions as design blueprints for coordinating large language model (LLM) multi-agent systems, showing that the way agents are organized (who proposes, who reviews, who executes, how to correct) heavily affects performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM work focuses mostly on improving single-agent intelligence, but many real problems require coordinating multiple bounded, fallible agents. Human civilizations have long confronted analogous coordination challenges and developed political institutions to manage them. The authors want to systematically explore whether these institutional structures can inform the design of LLM multi-agent architectures, and to quantify how different governance forms impact collective performance.

Method: The authors conceptualize LLM multi-agent systems using questions central to political institutions: proposal, review, execution, and error correction. They identify seven historical political institutions grouped into four canonical governance patterns, then translate each into a concrete multi-agent architecture (i.e., different topologies of proposing, reviewing, and executing agents). They run these architectures under identical experimental conditions, using three different LLMs and two benchmarks, to compare efficiency, accuracy, and other performance measures. They analyze how governance topology and model capability interact with task characteristics to determine outcomes.

Result: Governance topology substantially affects system performance: for a fixed base model, the performance gap between the best and worst institutional architecture exceeds 57 percentage points. Furthermore, the optimal governance architecture changes systematically with both model capability and the nature of the task, rather than there being one universally superior design. This demonstrates that organizational form is a first-order variable in multi-agent system effectiveness.

Conclusion: Multi-agent LLM systems face the same fundamental coordination problem as human societies, and political institutions offer a useful, structured design space for addressing it. There is no single best organizational form; instead, effective collective intelligence will rely on governance mechanisms that can be dynamically chosen, reselected, and reconfigured as models and tasks evolve. The field is likely to move from optimizing isolated self-evolving agents toward designing self-evolving multi-agent systems, with institutional topology as a key lever. The authors provide open-source code (SocialSystemArena) to support further work in this direction.

Abstract: Across millennia, complex societies have faced the same coordination problem of how to organize collective action among cognitively bounded and informationally incomplete individuals. Different civilizations developed different political institutions to answer the same basic questions of who proposes, who reviews, who executes, and how errors are corrected. We argue that multi-agent systems built on large language models face the same challenge. Their central problem is not only individual intelligence, but collective organization. Historical institutions therefore provide a structured design space for multi-agent architectures, making key trade-offs between efficiency and error correction, centralization and distribution, and specialization and redundancy empirically testable. We translate seven historical political institutions, spanning four canonical governance patterns, into executable multi-agent architectures and evaluate them under identical conditions across three large language models and two benchmarks. We find that governance topology strongly shapes collective performance. Within a single model, the gap between the best and worst institution exceeds 57 percentage points, while the optimal architecture shifts systematically with model capability and task characteristics. These results suggest that collective intelligence will not advance through a single optimal organizational form, but through governance mechanisms that can be reselected and reconfigured as tasks and capabilities evolve. More broadly, this points to a transition from \textbf{self-evolving agents} to the \textbf{self-evolving multi-agent system}. The code is available on \href{https://github.com/cf3i/SocialSystemArena}{GitHub}.

### [100] [Bridging Values and Behavior: A Hierarchical Framework for Proactive Embodied Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27699)
*Chunhui Zhang,Yuxuan Wang,Aoyang Qin,Yi-Long Lu,Kunlun Wu,Yizhou Wang,Wei Wang*

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing embodied agents mostly follow instructions or satisfy immediate needs reactively, lacking a stable, explicit value framework for making consistent long-term decisions and resolving conflicts between competing objectives. This gap prevents agents from exhibiting coherent self-directed behavior and makes it hard to evaluate autonomy beyond simple task success metrics.

Method: Design a hierarchical cognitive architecture called ValuePlanner. A large language model-based cognitive module operates at a high level to reason symbolically about abstract values and their trade-offs, outputting value-informed subgoals. These subgoals are converted into concrete action plans by a classical PDDL planner at the low level. The system runs in a closed feedback loop where outcomes inform subsequent high-level value reasoning. Additionally, the authors design a value-centric evaluation suite that measures cumulative value gain, preference alignment, and behavioral diversity rather than only task completion.

Result: In the TongSim household simulation environment, ValuePlanner is able to arbitrate between competing values and generate coherent, long-horizon, self-directed behavioral trajectories that are not seen in baseline agents which are limited to instruction following or needs-based reactive policies. Quantitatively, it performs better on the proposed value-centric metrics than these baselines.

Conclusion: Separating value-based high-level scheduling from low-level action planning enables embodied agents to more effectively integrate intrinsic values into their behavior, yielding more autonomous and coherent long-term behavior. The proposed architecture and evaluation suite provide a structured path toward building and assessing value-driven autonomous agents beyond traditional task-success metrics.

Abstract: Current embodied agents are often limited to passive instruction-following or reactive need-satisfaction, lacking a stable, high-order value framework essential for long-term, self-directed behavior and resolving motivational conflicts. We introduce \textit{ValuePlanner}, a hierarchical cognitive architecture that decouples high-level value scheduling from low-level action execution. \textit{ValuePlanner} employs an LLM-based cognitive module to generate symbolic subgoals by reasoning through abstract value trade-offs, which are then translated into executable action plans by a classical PDDL planner. This process is refined via a closed-loop feedback mechanism. Evaluating such autonomy requires methods beyond task-success rates, and we therefore propose a value-centric evaluation suite measuring cumulative value gain, preference alignment, and behavioral diversity. Experiments in the TongSim household environment demonstrate that \textit{ValuePlanner} arbitrates competing values to generate coherent, long-horizon, self-directed behavior absent from instruction-following and needs-driven baselines. Our work offers a structured approach to bridging intrinsic values and grounded behavior for autonomous agents.

### [101] [Contextual Agentic Memory is a Memo, Not True Memory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27707)
*Binyan Xu,Xilin Dai,Kehuan Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that current AI 'memory' mechanisms are actually just lookup systems, which fundamentally limits agents’ ability to generalize, learn over the long term, and stay secure, and proposes integrating true weight-based memory inspired by neuroscience.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern agent architectures rely heavily on vector databases, RAG, scratchpads, and clever context management, and these are often marketed or conceptualized as giving agents “memory.” The authors believe this is a conceptual mistake: these systems store and retrieve examples but do not support the kind of rule-based generalization that neural weights provide. This confusion leads to overestimating agent capabilities, misunderstanding their learning dynamics, and underestimating security risks like persistent memory poisoning. The paper is motivated by the need to clarify this distinction, show the concrete theoretical and practical consequences, and guide the design of genuinely learning, robust agentic systems.

Method: The authors conceptually and formally distinguish retrieval-based exemplar storage from weight-based memory. They use arguments grounded in learning theory and compositionally novel task settings to show that exemplar-based retrieval has an intrinsic generalization ceiling that cannot be overcome by enlarging context windows or improving retrieval methods. They map these ideas to Complementary Learning Systems (CLS) from neuroscience, in which the hippocampus supports fast exemplar storage and the neocortex supports slow structural learning through weight consolidation. They then analyze current AI agent designs through this lens, critique four alternative perspectives that might defend retrieval-as-memory, and articulate design implications and a coexistence framework for retrieval and weight-based memory.

Result: The paper shows that: (1) retrieval-based systems generalize only by similarity to stored cases, whereas weight-based memory can apply learned abstractions to truly unseen inputs; (2) agents that rely purely on retrieval tend to accumulate ever-growing note collections without forming compact, transferable expertise; (3) such agents hit a provable ceiling on performance for tasks requiring compositional generalization that no engineering of context size or retrieval quality can break; and (4) their architecture creates a structural vulnerability to memory poisoning, since corrupted content, once stored, can repeatedly resurface across sessions and contaminate future behavior. The authors also demonstrate, via the CLS framework, that biological systems avoid these pathologies by coupling fast retrieval with slower consolidation into weights, and outline how analogous mechanisms are missing in current AI agents.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that current “agentic memory” stacks are fundamentally incomplete: they implement only fast exemplar lookup, not true memory capable of robust, compositional generalization and safe long-term learning. Treating lookup as memory is a category error with practical consequences for capability, learning, and security. They advocate designing agents that pair retrieval systems with mechanisms for gradual weight-based consolidation, inspired by Complementary Learning Systems in the brain. They call on system builders to incorporate such consolidation, on benchmark designers to include tests that expose the generalization limits of pure retrieval, and on the memory research community to develop architectures where lookup and learned structure coexist but are not conflated.

Abstract: Current agentic memory systems (vector stores, retrieval-augmented generation, scratchpads, and context-window management) do not implement memory: they implement lookup. We argue that treating lookup as memory is a category error with provable consequences for agent capability, long-term learning, and security. Retrieval generalizes by similarity to stored cases; weight-based memory generalizes by applying abstract rules to inputs never seen before. Conflating the two produces agents that accumulate notes indefinitely without developing expertise, face a provable generalization ceiling on compositionally novel tasks that no increase in context size or retrieval quality can overcome, and are structurally vulnerable to persistent memory poisoning as injected content propagates across all future sessions. Drawing on Complementary Learning Systems theory from neuroscience, we show that biological intelligence solved this problem by pairing fast hippocampal exemplar storage with slow neocortical weight consolidation, and that current AI agents implement only the first half. We formalize these limitations, address four alternative views, and close with a co-existence proposal and a call to action for system builders, benchmark designers, and the memory community.

</details>

### [103] [Auditing Frontier Vision-Language Models for Trustworthy Medical VQA: Grounding Failures, Format Collapse, and Domain Adaptation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27720)
*Xupeng Chen,Binbin Shi,Chenqian Le,Qifu Yin,Lang Lin,Haowei Ni,Ran Gong,Panfeng Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper audits frontier vision-language models on medical visual question answering, finding poor localization and grounding that undermine trustworthiness, while showing that domain-adapted fine-tuning can close much of the VQA performance gap but leaves perception issues unresolved.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To safely deploy vision-language models in clinical environments, their failures—especially around visual grounding and localization—must be well understood, since errors like incorrect anatomy localization or laterality confusion can be clinically dangerous. Existing work largely reports VQA accuracy without deeply auditing how models behave under realistic, decomposed pipelines or how grounding quality impacts trustworthiness.

Method: The authors evaluate five recent frontier and grounding-aware VLMs (Gemini 2.5 Pro, GPT-5, o3, GLM-4.5V, Qwen 2.5 VL) on medical VQA datasets with bounding-box annotations (e.g., SLAKE, VQA-RAD) along two axes: (1) Perception: they measure localization performance using mean IoU and Acc@0.5, analyze anatomical/pathological target localization, and inspect laterality errors. (2) Pipeline integration: they construct a self-grounding pipeline where each model first predicts bounding boxes and then answers questions based on these predictions, tracking VQA accuracy and format/parse failures under the two-step prompts. They also run a counterfactual experiment where the predicted bounding boxes are replaced by ground truth to isolate the impact of the perception module. Finally, they perform supervised fine-tuning of Qwen 2.5 VL on combined Med-VQA training data and evaluate open-ended recall on SLAKE.

Result: All evaluated models perform poorly at localization, with the best achieving only 0.23 mean IoU and 19.1% Acc@0.5, and they show clinically risky laterality confusion. Integrating models in a self-grounding pipeline consistently reduces VQA accuracy across models, largely because of inaccurate localization and high parse/format failure rates (70–99% for Gemini and GPT-5 on VQA-RAD) under the two-step prompts. When ground-truth boxes are substituted for model-predicted boxes, VQA accuracy is recovered and even improved, indicating that the main failure source is the perception/localization module rather than the decomposition strategy itself. In follow-up fine-tuning, Qwen 2.5 VL achieves 85.5% open-ended recall on SLAKE, the highest reported among comparable methods.

Conclusion: The study identifies poor visual grounding—particularly bounding-box localization quality—as a key trustworthiness bottleneck for frontier VLMs in medical VQA, outweighing issues in the decomposed reasoning pipeline itself. While supervised domain adaptation can substantially improve VQA performance at the answer level, it does not by itself guarantee reliable perception or grounding, which remain open challenges. Future work should focus on improving and auditing the perception components of VLMs, especially for safety-critical clinical deployment.

Abstract: Deploying vision-language models (VLMs) in clinical settings demands auditable behavior under realistic failure conditions, yet the failure landscape of frontier VLMs on specialized medical inputs is poorly characterized. We audit five recent frontier and grounding-aware VLMs (Gemini~2.5~Pro, GPT-5, o3, GLM-4.5V, Qwen~2.5~VL) on Medical VQA along two trust-relevant axes. Perception: all models localize anatomical and pathological targets poorly -- the best model reaches only 0.23 mean IoU and 19.1% Acc@0.5 -- and exhibit clinically dangerous laterality confusion. Pipeline integration: a self-grounding pipeline, where the same model localizes then answers, degrades VQA accuracy for every model -- driven by both inaccurate localization and format-compliance failures under the two-step prompt (parse failure rises to 70%--99% for Gemini and GPT-5 on VQA-RAD). Replacing predicted boxes with ground-truth annotations recovers and improves VQA accuracy, consistent with the failure residing in the perception module rather than in the decomposition itself. These observational findings identify grounding quality as a primary trustworthiness bottleneck in our SLAKE bounding-box setting. As a complementary fine-tuning follow-up, supervised fine-tuning of Qwen~2.5~VL on combined Med-VQA training data attains the highest reported SLAKE open-ended recall (85.5%) among comparable methods, suggesting that the VQA-level gap is tractable with domain adaptation; whether this also closes the perception/trustworthiness bottleneck is left to future work.

### [104] [Iterative Multimodal Retrieval-Augmented Generation for Medical Question Answering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27724)
*Xupeng Chen,Binbin Shi,Chenqian Le,Jiaqi Zhang,Kewen Wang,Ran Gong,Jinhan Zhang,Chihang Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces MED-VRAG, a multimodal medical RAG system that retrieves and reasons directly over page images (with figures, tables, and layouts) instead of plain text, achieving improved accuracy on multiple medical QA benchmarks via iterative evidence gathering and a memory bank.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most medical RAG systems ignore visual information in biomedical papers and rely solely on text chunks, losing tables, figures, and layout cues that are crucial for clinical reasoning and evidence-based answers. There is a need for scalable retrieval over entire document pages as images and for methods that let a vision-language model iteratively refine queries and aggregate multimodal evidence, while remaining computationally efficient at large corpus scale.

Method: The paper proposes MED-VRAG, an iterative multimodal RAG framework that operates on page images from PubMed Central instead of OCR text. Stage-1 uses ColQwen2.5 to compute patch-level page embeddings and builds an offline coarse-to-fine index: each page is represented by 8 centroids, approximate nearest neighbor search is done over centroids, and the top-R pages are re-ranked with exact two-way scoring, keeping retrieval latency under 30 ms for ~350K pages. Stage-2 employs a Qwen2.5-based vision-language model that conducts up to three reasoning rounds; in each round it can reformulate the query, retrieve new page images, and store extracted evidence in a memory bank. This iterative process allows the model to refine its understanding and integrate multimodal cues across pages; runtime is ~15.9 s for one iteration and ~47.8 s for three rounds on 4xA100 GPUs. The authors run controlled comparisons, ablations (image vs text retrieval, number of iterations, use of memory bank), and benchmark evaluations on four medical QA datasets.

Result: On MedQA, MedMCQA, PubMedQA, and MMLU-Med, MED-VRAG attains an average accuracy of 78.6%. Using the same Qwen2.5-VL-32B backbone, adding page-level retrieval yields a +5.8 percentage point improvement over a no-retrieval baseline. In a cross-paper comparison, MED-VRAG slightly outperforms MedRAG combined with GPT-4 by 1.8 points (76.8% → 78.6%), though the authors note this is not a head-to-head evaluation. Ablation studies attribute roughly +1.0 point to using page-image retrieval instead of text-chunk retrieval, +1.5 points to multi-round iterative reasoning, and +1.0 point to the memory bank mechanism.

Conclusion: Retrieving and reasoning directly over biomedical document page images, including figures, tables, and layouts, can improve medical QA performance beyond text-only RAG. MED-VRAG demonstrates that coarse-to-fine multimodal retrieval at corpus scale is feasible within tight latency budgets and that iterative VLM-based reasoning with a memory bank yields measurable gains. The framework suggests that future medical RAG systems should embrace multimodal document representations and multi-step evidence aggregation rather than relying solely on static text chunks and single-pass retrieval.

Abstract: Medical retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) systems typically operate on text chunks extracted from biomedical literature, discarding the rich visual content (tables, figures, structured layouts) of original document pages. We propose MED-VRAG, an iterative multimodal RAG framework that retrieves and reasons over PMC document page images instead of OCR'd text. The system pairs ColQwen2.5 patch-level page embeddings with a sharded MapReduce LLM filter, scaling to ~350K pages while keeping Stage-1 retrieval under 30 ms via an offline coarse-to-fine index (C=8 centroids per page, ANN over centroids, exact two-way scoring on the top-R shortlist). A vision-language model (VLM) then iteratively refines its query and accumulates evidence in a memory bank across up to 3 reasoning rounds, with a single iteration costing ~15.9 s and the full three-round pipeline ~47.8 s on 4xA100. Across four medical QA benchmarks (MedQA, MedMCQA, PubMedQA, MMLU-Med), MEDVRAG reaches 78.6% average accuracy. Under controlled comparison with the same Qwen2.5-VL-32B backbone, retrieval contributes a +5.8 point gain over the no-retrieval baseline; we also note a +1.8 point edge over MedRAG + GPT-4 (76.8%), with the caveat that this is a cross-paper rather than head-to-head comparison. Ablations isolate +1.0 from page-image vs text-chunk retrieval, +1.5 from iteration, and +1.0 from the memory bank.

  <summary>Details</summary>

In the scenario task, the AI-generated summary was strongly preferred for quality, empathy, and overall usefulness, yet identification of the AI summary was near chance. Findings show that consumers judge AI through concrete communication quality and visible human governance, underscoring the need for clinically supervised deployment frameworks beyond technical performance alone.

We present \textsc{Intent2Tx}, a high-fidelity benchmark featuring 29,921 single-step and 1,575 multi-step instances meticulously derived from 300 days of real-world Ethereum mainnet traces. Unlike prior works that rely on synthetic instructions, \textsc{Intent2Tx} grounds natural language intents in real-world protocol interactions across 11 categories, including diverse long-tail Decentralized Finance (DeFi) primitives. To enable rigorous evaluation, we propose an execution-aware framework that transcends surface-level text matching by employing differential state analysis on forked mainnet environments. Our extensive evaluation of 16 state-of-the-art LLMs reveals that while scaling and retrieval-augmentation enhance logical consistency and parameter precision, current models struggle with out-of-distribution generalization and multi-step planning. Crucially, our execution-based analysis demonstrates that syntactically valid outputs often fail to achieve intended state transitions, highlighting a significant gap in current "reasoning-to-execution" capabilities. \textsc{Intent2Tx} serves as a critical foundation for developing autonomous, reliable agents in intent-centric Web3 ecosystems. Conclusion: Current GUI agents that perform well on single-application tasks struggle significantly with realistic, cross-application professional workflows. WindowsWorld exposes deficiencies in multi-application coordination, conditional reasoning, and efficient execution. The benchmark provides a systematic way to measure and drive progress on more realistic, profession-oriented computer-use capabilities in future agent systems.

The resulting benchmark contains 181 tasks with an average of 5.0 sub-goals across 17 common desktop applications, of which 78% are inherently multi-application. Experimental results of leading large models and agents show that: 1) All computer-use agents perform poorly on multi-application tasks (< 21% success rate), far below the performance of simple single-app tasks; 2) They largely fail at tasks requiring conditional judgment and reasoning across $\geq$ 3 applications, stalling at early sub-goals; 3) Low execution efficiency, where tasks often fail despite far exceeding human step limits. Code, benchmark data, and evaluation resources are available at github.com/HITsz-TMG/WindowsWorld.

### [109] [Post-Optimization Adaptive Rank Allocation for LoRA](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27796)
*Vishnuprasadh Kumaravelu,Sunil Gupta,P. K. Srijith*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces PARA, a post-hoc, data-free method to compress LoRA by adaptively assigning non-uniform ranks to layers using SVD-based pruning, achieving large parameter reductions with minimal performance loss.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard LoRA uses a uniform rank across all layers, ignoring that different layers may require different intrinsic dimensionalities. This causes parameter redundancy and inefficiency, especially as foundation models and their LoRA adapters grow in size. There is a need for a simple way to reduce LoRA parameters without re-training or destabilizing training with dynamic architectures.

Method: PARA is a post-optimization, data-free compression technique applied after a LoRA model has been fine-tuned. It performs Singular Value Decomposition on the LoRA matrices in each layer and prunes them using a single global threshold on singular values shared across all layers. Singular values below the threshold are discarded, effectively reducing the rank of each LoRA module. This yields non-uniform, layer-wise rank allocation that reflects spectral (singular-value-based) importance, and it plugs into existing fine-tuning pipelines without modifying the original training process.

Result: Across multiple vision and language benchmarks, PARA achieves 75–90% reduction in the number of LoRA parameters while largely preserving the predictive performance of the original, uncompressed LoRA models. The empirical results show that most of the LoRA capacity is redundant and that adaptive rank reduction can be done without access to training data.

Conclusion: Layer-wise intrinsic dimensionality in LoRA adapters is highly non-uniform, and enforcing a single global rank wastes parameters. By using a post-hoc, SVD-based global-threshold pruning strategy, PARA can drastically compress LoRA adapters while maintaining accuracy, and it can be integrated easily into existing fine-tuning workflows since it does not alter training or require data. This makes PARA a practical tool for deploying parameter-efficient fine-tuned foundation models at scale.

Abstract: Exponential growth in the scale of modern foundation models has led to the widespread adoption of Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA) as a parameter-efficient fine-tuning technique. However, standard LoRA implementations disregard the varying intrinsic dimensionality of model layers and enforce a uniform rank, leading to parameter redundancy. We propose Post-Optimization Adaptive Rank Allocation (PARA), a data-free compression method for LoRA that integrates seamlessly into existing fine-tuning pipelines. PARA leverages Singular Value Decomposition to prune LoRA ranks using a global threshold over singular values across all layers. This results in non-uniform rank allocation based on layer-wise spectral importance. As a post-hoc method, PARA circumvents the training modifications and resulting instabilities that dynamic architectures typically incur. We empirically demonstrate that PARA reduces parameter count by 75-90\% while preserving the predictive performance of the original, uncompressed LoRA across multiple vision and language benchmarks. Code will be published upon acceptance.

</details>


### [110] [From Unstructured Recall to Schema-Grounded Memory: Reliable AI Memory via Iterative, Schema-Aware Extraction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27906)
*Alex Petrov,Alexander Gusak,Denis Mukha,Dima Korolev*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a schema-grounded external memory system for AI agents that treats memory as a structured system of record rather than pure text retrieval, achieving higher accuracy on extraction and end-to-end memory tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prevailing AI memory approaches rely on vector retrieval over past text, which works for thematic recall but fails for production needs like exact factual state, updates, deletions, relations, and explicit unknowns. These require reliable, queryable, and updatable memory similar to a transactional system of record, not fuzzy search. The authors aim to design a memory architecture better aligned with these real-world requirements.

Method: They introduce a schema-grounded memory architecture where schemas define what must be stored, what can be ignored, and which values must never be inferred. Memory ingestion is handled via an iterative, schema-aware write path decomposed into (1) object detection, (2) field detection, and (3) field-value extraction. The pipeline includes validation gates, local retries, and stateful prompt control, shifting most interpretation to the write phase. Read operations then become constrained queries over verified structured records instead of re-interpreting retrieved text. They implement this system, xmemory, and evaluate it on structured extraction and end-to-end memory benchmarks.

Result: On a structured extraction benchmark, the judge-in-the-loop variant of their system achieves 90.42% object-level accuracy and 62.67% output accuracy, outperforming multiple frontier structured-output baselines. On a custom end-to-end memory benchmark, their system (xmemory) attains 97.10% F1, compared with 80.16%–87.24% for third-party baselines. On an application-level task, xmemory achieves 95.2% accuracy, beating specialized memory systems, code-generated Markdown harnesses, and production-grade frontier-model application harnesses.

Conclusion: Schema-grounded, write-intensive memory architectures provide more reliable support for workloads needing stable facts and stateful computation than approaches based primarily on retrieval scale or model strength. By moving interpretation to a schema-aware write path and treating memory as a structured system of record, AI agents can achieve substantially better accuracy and robustness than with traditional vector-based retrieval alone.

Abstract: Persistent AI memory is often reduced to a retrieval problem: store prior interactions as text, embed them, and ask the model to recover relevant context later. This design is useful for thematic recall, but it is mismatched to the kinds of memory that agents need in production: exact facts, current state, updates and deletions, aggregation, relations, negative queries, and explicit unknowns. These operations require memory to behave less like search and more like a system of record.
  This paper argues that reliable external AI memory must be schema-grounded. Schemas define what must be remembered, what may be ignored, and which values must never be inferred. We present an iterative, schema-aware write path that decomposes memory ingestion into object detection, field detection, and field-value extraction, with validation gates, local retries, and stateful prompt control. The result shifts interpretation from the read path to the write path: reads become constrained queries over verified records rather than repeated inference over retrieved prose.
  We evaluate this design on structured extraction and end-to-end memory benchmarks. On the extraction benchmark, the judge-in-the-loop configuration reaches 90.42% object-level accuracy and 62.67% output accuracy, above all tested frontier structured-output baselines. On our end-to-end memory benchmark, xmemory reaches 97.10% F1, compared with 80.16%-87.24% across the third-party baselines. On the application-level task, xmemory reaches 95.2% accuracy, outperforming specialised memory systems, code-generated Markdown harnesses, and customer-facing frontier-model application harnesses. The results show that, for memory workloads requiring stable facts and stateful computation, architecture matters more than retrieval scale or model strength alone.

### [113] [MCPHunt: An Evaluation Framework for Cross-Boundary Data Propagation in Multi-Server MCP Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27819)
*Haonan Li,Tianjun Sun,Yongqing Wang,Qisheng Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: MCPHunt is a benchmark and analysis framework for studying how multi-server MCP agents accidentally propagate credentials across trust boundaries, even without adversarial intent, and evaluates how well prompt-based mitigations can reduce this risk while preserving utility.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-server MCP (Model Context Protocol) agents coordinate tools and services that each have their own read/write permissions. When these are composed, credentials or sensitive tokens can unintentionally flow across trust boundaries, not due to malicious models but due to workflow structure and instructions. Existing evaluations do not cleanly isolate this non-adversarial, verbatim credential propagation or distinguish between necessary credential transfer and avoidable policy violations. A controlled, measurement-focused benchmark is needed to understand where, how often, and via which mechanisms credential propagation occurs, and to test mitigations.

Method: The authors design MCPHunt, a controlled benchmark for credential propagation in multi-server MCP setups. Their methodology has three key elements: (1) canary-based taint tracking by embedding identifiable canary credentials and then detecting propagation via objective string matching; (2) an environment-controlled coverage design that defines risky, benign, and hard-negative scenarios to validate that the pipelines are sound and to ensure that findings are not an artifact of a particular credential format; and (3) CRS (credential requirement stratification) that separates cases where credential transfer is mandated by the task and instructions from cases where the model could have redacted them but did not, thus distinguishing faithful execution from policy-violating behavior. They run 3,615 traces across 5 models, 147 tasks, and 9 mechanism families, analyzing propagation behavior and then conduct a prompt-mitigation study on 3 models to measure how much propagation can be reduced while maintaining task utility.

Result: Empirically, policy-violating credential propagation is common: 11.5–41.3% of main-benchmark traces show propagation across all tested models. Propagation is highly pathway-specific, with a 25x difference across different mechanisms and a strong concentration in browser-mediated data flows. Hard-negative controls show that realistic credential formatting is not required for this behavior—prompt-driven cross-boundary data transfer is sufficient to induce it. Prompt-based mitigations can substantially reduce policy-violating propagation (up to 97% reduction) while retaining most utility (around 80.5%), but the success of these mitigations varies significantly with the model’s instruction-following capability.

Conclusion: MCPHunt demonstrates that non-adversarial, verbatim credential propagation across MCP trust boundaries is a real and measurable risk driven by workflow topology and instructions, not just adversarial prompting or particular credential formats. The risk is mechanism- and pathway-dependent, especially in browser-related flows. While carefully designed prompts can greatly mitigate propagation without fully sacrificing usefulness, their effectiveness is model-dependent, indicating that prompt defenses alone are insufficient for robust protection. The released code, traces, and labeling pipeline provide a reproducible basis for future work on safer multi-server MCP agent design and policy enforcement.

Abstract: Multi-server MCP agents create an information-flow control problem: faithful tool composition can turn individually benign read/write permissions into cross-boundary credential propagation -- a structural side effect of workflow topology, not necessarily malicious model behavior. We present MCPHunt, to our knowledge the first controlled benchmark that isolates non-adversarial, verbatim credential propagation across multi-server MCP trust boundaries, with three methodological contributions: (1) canary-based taint tracking that reduces propagation detection to objective string matching; (2) an environment-controlled coverage design with risky, benign, and hard-negative conditions that validates pipeline soundness and controls for credential-format confounds; (3) CRS stratification that disentangles task-mandated propagation (faithful execution of verbatim-transfer instructions) from policy-violating propagation (credentials included despite the option to redact). Across 3,615 main-benchmark traces from 5 models spanning 147 tasks and 9 mechanism families, policy-violating propagation rates reach 11.5--41.3% across all models. This propagation is pathway-specific (25x cross-mechanism range) and concentrated in browser-mediated data flows; hard-negative controls provide evidence that production-format credentials are not necessary -- prompt-directed cross-boundary data flow is sufficient. A prompt-mitigation study across 3 models reduces policy-violating propagation by up to 97% while preserving 80.5% utility, but effectiveness varies with instruction-following capability -- suggesting that prompt-level defenses alone may not suffice. Code, traces, and labeling pipeline are released under MIT and CC BY 4.0.

</details>


### [114] [Mapping the Methodological Space of Classroom Interaction Research: Scale, Duration, and Modality in an Age of AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28098)

Method: Conceptual/theoretical analysis that defines a three-dimensional methodological space (scale, duration, modality), mapped onto existing classroom interaction research. Result: The framework shows that different configurations of scale, duration, and modality privilege different kinds of insights (e.g., patterns vs. mechanisms vs. lived practice) and systematically obscure others. The analysis identifies ways AI can support new combinations (e.g., long-duration, multi-modal, large-scale analyses) and highlights tensions around operationalization and interpretability.

Conclusion: Positioning studies within the three-dimensional space clarifies their affordances and limitations, making trade-offs explicit and helping researchers choose complementary methods. The framework also serves as a design tool for AI-based classroom analytics, suggesting how to balance scale, duration, and modality to surface mechanisms that matter for teaching practice while remaining interpretable and actionable.

Abstract: Research on classroom interaction has long been divided between large-scale observation and in-depth ethnographic work. We propose a framework mapping this methodological space along three dimensions--scale, duration, and modality--where a study's position shapes what it reveals and obscures. We illustrate it through contrasting studies of dialogic teaching--Howe et al. (2019) and Snell and Lefstein (2018)--and an interview with the lead researchers, organized around three questions: what can be operationalized, what mechanisms become visible, and what translates to practice. We then examine how AI is expanding this space and how the framework can guide research and tool design.

*Mohit Dubey,Open Gigantic*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes OBJECTGRAPH, a new document file format optimized for LLM agents that treats documents as traversable knowledge graphs instead of linear text, cutting token usage drastically while preserving task performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing document formats are built for humans reading text linearly, but autonomous LLM agents access information via retrieval. This mismatch causes inefficiencies: agents must stuff whole documents into context windows, waste tokens on irrelevant content, accumulate unnecessary state in multi-turn workflows, and broadcast all information to all roles. The authors argue that these issues cannot be fully solved by better prompts, retrieval, or compression alone because the root cause is the document format itself.

Method: The authors design OBJECTGRAPH (.og), a document format that is a typed, directed knowledge graph and a strict superset of Markdown. They formalize the Document Consumption Problem and define six structural properties that an agent-friendly document format should satisfy. They prove that OBJECTGRAPH satisfies all six properties. They add native primitives such as the Progressive Disclosure Model for staged information access, the Role-Scoped Access Protocol for role-specific visibility, and Executable Assertion Nodes for embedding verifiable, executable content. They also implement transpilers from existing formats into OBJECTGRAPH and evaluate performance empirically.

Result: Across five document classes and eight agent task types, using OBJECTGRAPH reduces token usage by up to 95.3 percent without statistically significant loss in task accuracy (p > 0.05). Their transpiler achieves 98.7 percent content preservation on a held-out document benchmark, indicating high fidelity in converting existing documents into the new format.

Conclusion: Reframing documents as traversable knowledge graphs rather than linear text resolves key inefficiencies in how LLM agents consume information. OBJECTGRAPH meets formal structural requirements that current formats lack, integrates agent-centric primitives, and empirically delivers large token savings with maintained accuracy. The authors conclude that future agent ecosystems should treat document format design as a first-class concern, and that OBJECTGRAPH offers a practical, backward-compatible path forward.

Abstract: Every document format in existence was designed for a human reader moving linearly through text. Autonomous LLM agents do not read - they retrieve. This fundamental mismatch forces agents to inject entire documents into their context window, wasting tokens on irrelevant content, compounding state across multi-turn loops, and broadcasting information indiscriminately across agent roles. We argue this is not a prompt engineering problem, not a retrieval problem, and not a compression problem: it is a format problem.
  We introduce OBJECTGRAPH (.og), a file format that reconceives the document as a typed, directed knowledge graph to be traversed rather than a string to be injected. OBJECTGRAPH is a strict superset of Markdown - every .md file is a valid .og file - requires no infrastructure beyond a two-primitive query protocol, and is readable by both humans and agents without tooling.
  We formalize the Document Consumption Problem, characterise six structural properties no existing format satisfies simultaneously, and prove OBJECTGRAPH satisfies all six. We further introduce the Progressive Disclosure Model, the Role-Scoped Access Protocol, and Executable Assertion Nodes as native format primitives. Empirical evaluation across five document classes and eight agent task types demonstrates up to 95.3 percent token reduction with no statistically significant degradation in task accuracy (p > 0.05). Transpiler fidelity reaches 98.7 percent content preservation on a held-out document benchmark.

</details>


### [116] [Synthetic Computers at Scale for Long-Horizon Productivity Simulation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28181)
*Tao Ge,Baolin Peng,Hao Cheng,Jianfeng Gao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a scalable way to build synthetic, realistic computer environments and run long-horizon agent simulations on them to generate rich training data for productivity-oriented AI agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Training AI agents for realistic, long-horizon productivity tasks requires environments that reflect actual user computer setups, including complex directory structures, documents, and ongoing projects. Real-world collection at scale is hard due to privacy, diversity, and cost, so there is a need for a scalable synthetic alternative that still preserves realism and task complexity.

Method: The authors introduce “Synthetic Computers at Scale,” a pipeline that (1) procedurally generates realistic, user-specific computer environments with folders and diverse productivity artifacts; (2) instantiates a pair of agents per environment—one that defines multi-week, multi-deliverable objectives tailored to that synthetic user, and another that acts as the user, operating the computer, navigating files, collaborating with simulated colleagues, and producing artifacts; and (3) runs long-horizon simulations spanning thousands of turns and many hours of runtime to collect trajectories and outputs as experiential learning signals.

Result: They build 1,000 synthetic computers and run long-horizon simulations on each, with over 8 hours and 2,000 turns per run on average. The resulting synthetic trajectories and artifacts are used to train or fine-tune agents, which then show significant performance gains on both in-domain (similar to the simulated tasks) and out-of-domain productivity benchmarks, indicating good transfer and usefulness of the synthetic data.

Conclusion: Scalable generation of synthetic, content-rich computer environments combined with long-horizon agent simulations is a promising foundation for self-improving AI agents in productivity settings. With enough compute and personas, this approach could extend to millions or billions of synthetic user worlds, covering a broad variety of professional roles and workflows, and serving as a basis for agentic reinforcement learning for complex, long-term tasks.

Abstract: Realistic long-horizon productivity work is strongly conditioned on user-specific computer environments, where much of the work context is stored and organized through directory structures and content-rich artifacts. To scale synthetic data creation for such productivity scenarios, we introduce Synthetic Computers at Scale, a scalable methodology for creating such environments with realistic folder hierarchies and content-rich artifacts (e.g., documents, spreadsheets, and presentations). Conditioned on each synthetic computer, we run long-horizon simulations: one agent creates productivity objectives that are specific to the computer's user and require multiple professional deliverables and about a month of human work; another agent then acts as that user and keeps working across the computer -- for example, navigating the filesystem for grounding, coordinating with simulated collaborators, and producing professional artifacts -- until these objectives are completed.
  In preliminary experiments, we create 1,000 synthetic computers and run long-horizon simulations on them; each run requires over 8 hours of agent runtime and spans more than 2,000 turns on average. These simulations produce rich experiential learning signals, whose effectiveness is validated by significant improvements in agent performance on both in-domain and out-of-domain productivity evaluations. Given that personas are abundant at billion scale, this methodology can in principle scale to millions or even billions of synthetic user worlds with sufficient compute, enabling broader coverage of diverse professions, roles, contexts, environments, and productivity needs. We argue that scalable synthetic computer creation, together with at-scale simulations, is highly promising as a foundational substrate for agent self-improvement and agentic reinforcement learning in long-horizon productivity scenarios.

</details>

In this paper, we provide a deep insight for looking the conceptual foundations, methodological innovations, and effective designs underlying this trend. *Thomas Grady,Kip Parker,Iliyan Zarov,Henry Course,Chengxi Taylor,Ross Taylor*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing benchmarks focus on narrow, procedural tasks, while LMs are increasingly used in complex, long-horizon, non-stationary settings with open-ended objectives. There is a need for realistic environments to evaluate agents on sequential decision-making with financial consequences and adaptation, such as sports betting markets.

Method: The authors build KellyBench, a sequential simulation of the 2023-24 English Premier League season where agents receive rich historical data (advanced stats, lineups, public odds) and must choose bets to maximize long-term bankroll growth. Various frontier language models are used as agents, required to construct models, estimate edge against public odds, manage risk (implicitly via Kelly-like objectives), and adapt as the season evolves. Performance is measured via average return over multiple random seeds and a human-expert rubric assessing strategic sophistication.

Result: Across five seeds, all evaluated frontier LMs lose money on average over the simulated season. The best model has an average return of about -8%, and many models go bankrupt in some seeds. Human experts grade the sophistication of each model's betting strategies; all models receive low rubric scores, with Claude Opus 4.6 scoring only 26.5%, indicating basic or naive strategies relative to human baselines.

Conclusion: Current frontier language models perform poorly in realistic, long-horizon sports betting markets, both financially and strategically, revealing a significant gap between their benchmark performance and real-world sequential decision-making. KellyBench provides an open-access benchmark and API for studying and improving LMs’ capabilities in dynamic, high-stakes environments and highlights substantial headroom for methodological advances.

Abstract: Language models are saturating benchmarks for procedural tasks with narrow objectives. But they are increasingly being deployed in long-horizon, non-stationary environments with open-ended goals. In this paper we introduce KellyBench, an environment for evaluating sequential decision-making in sports betting markets. Agents are placed in a sequential simulation of the 2023-24 English Premier League season and tasked with maximising their long-term bankroll growth. They are given detailed historical data, including advanced statistics, lineups, and public odds. To succeed they must build machine learning models, identify edge in public markets, and adapt as the environment changes over time. We find that all frontier models evaluated lose money on average over the course of the season for five seeds. The best performing model achieves an average return of -8%, and many models experiencing ruin across seeds. To judge strategy sophistication, we use a human expert rubric to grade each model and find their approaches to be unsophisticated compared to human baselines; Claude Opus 4.6 achieves a rubric score of 26.5%, which means there is significant room for improvement. KellyBench is available as an open-access API endpoint at https://openreward.ai/GeneralReasoning/KellyBench.

### [120] [Modeling Clinical Concern Trajectories in Language Model Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27872)
*Sukesh Subaharan,Venkatesan VS,Murugadasan P,Sivakumar D,Gautham N,Ganeshkumar M*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes adding explicit temporal state dynamics to LLM-based clinical agents so that they produce a smooth, continuous “escalation pressure” signal instead of abrupt, threshold-based escalation decisions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM agents in clinical contexts tend to act in a binary, threshold-driven manner (e.g., escalate vs. not escalate) with little visibility into how risk accumulates over time. This is misaligned with real clinical practice, where clinicians’ concern builds gradually and is used for ongoing monitoring and early intervention. There is a need for agent designs that expose this evolving concern signal without handing over final clinical authority to the AI.

Method: The authors design a lightweight architecture that separates instantaneous risk assessment from temporal integration. First, a memoryless clinical risk encoder converts the current patient state into a scalar risk signal at each timestep. Then, this signal is integrated over time using explicit first- or second-order dynamical systems, yielding a continuous variable called “escalation pressure.” They evaluate this against stateless (pure threshold) agents in synthetic ward scenarios and compare the resulting escalation behaviors and trajectories of concern.

Result: In synthetic scenarios, stateless baseline agents display abrupt “escalation cliffs,” where the decision flips suddenly once the risk crosses a threshold, providing no early warning. In contrast, when first- and especially second-order dynamics are used, the system produces smooth, anticipatory trajectories of escalation pressure. These trajectories grow in advance of the eventual escalation point and yet still trigger escalation at roughly the same times as the stateless agents, thus preserving responsiveness while improving transparency.

Conclusion: Introducing explicit state dynamics into LLM-based clinical agents can reveal how concern accumulates over time, making their behavior more interpretable and clinically aligned. The resulting continuous escalation pressure signal captures sustained unease before formal escalation, supporting human-in-the-loop monitoring and better-timed interventions without ceding clinical authority to the agent.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM) agents deployed in clinical settings often exhibit abrupt, threshold-driven behavior, offering little visibility into accumulating risk prior to escalation. In real-world care, however, clinicians act on gradually rising concern rather than instantaneous triggers. We study whether explicit state dynamics can expose such pre-escalation signals without delegating clinical authority to the agent. We introduce a lightweight agent architecture in which a memoryless clinical risk encoder is integrated over time using first- and second-order dynamics to produce a continuous escalation pressure signal. Across synthetic ward scenarios, stateless agents exhibit sharp escalation cliffs, while second-order dynamics produce smooth, anticipatory concern trajectories despite similar escalation timing. These trajectories surface sustained unease prior to escalation, enabling human-in-the-loop monitoring and more informed intervention. Our results suggest that explicit state dynamics can make LLM agents more clinically legible by revealing how long concern has been rising, not just when thresholds are crossed.

</details>
