### [122] [In-Context Prompting Obsoletes Agent Orchestration for Procedural Tasks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27891)
*Simon Dennis,Michael Diamond,Rivaan Patil,Kevin Shabahang,Hao Guo*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper compares external agent orchestration frameworks to a simpler approach where the full task procedure is placed in the system prompt, letting the LLM self-orchestrate. For procedural, multi-step tasks, self-orchestration in-context outperforms external orchestration in quality and reliability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Agent orchestration frameworks like LangGraph, CrewAI, Google ADK, and OpenAI Agents SDK are widely adopted to manage complex, multi-turn tasks by coordinating tools, routing, and control flow outside the LLM. However, these frameworks add system complexity and latency, and it is unclear whether they are still needed given the rapid improvement of frontier LLM capabilities. The authors aim to test whether, for well-defined procedural workflows, a much simpler in-context prompting strategy can match or outperform these orchestration stacks.

Method: The authors construct three realistic procedural workflows: travel booking (14-step graph), Zoom technical support (14-step graph), and insurance claims processing (55-step graph). They implement two conditions using the same underlying LLM: (1) In-context self-orchestration, where the entire procedure (graph of steps) is encoded in the system prompt and the model is instructed to follow and manage the procedure itself; (2) External orchestration using an agent framework (LangGraph) that tracks state and injects routing instructions at every turn. For each domain and condition, they run 200 conversations and evaluate them using LLM-as-judge scoring across five quality criteria, also measuring explicit failure rates.

Result: Across all three domains, the in-context self-orchestrated system performs better than the externally orchestrated LangGraph system on the LLM-as-judge 5-point quality scale. The in-context approach achieves scores between 4.53 and 5.00, whereas the orchestrated system scores between 4.17 and 4.84. Failure rates are consistently lower for the in-context method: for travel booking, 11.5% vs 24%; for Zoom technical support, 0.5% vs 9%; and for insurance claims, 5% vs 17% in the orchestrated setup. These results indicate that the added complexity of an external orchestrator does not translate into better task performance for these procedural workflows.

Conclusion: For procedural, multi-turn conversations that follow a defined workflow, modern frontier LLMs can effectively self-orchestrate when given the full procedure in the system prompt. External agent orchestration frameworks, at least in the tested setup with LangGraph, underperform this simpler approach in both quality and reliability. The authors conclude that while external orchestration may have been beneficial or necessary for earlier, weaker models, it is now often unnecessary overhead for many structured, procedural applications. This suggests practitioners should re-evaluate when they truly need agent frameworks versus well-engineered in-context prompts.

Abstract: Agent orchestration frameworks -- LangGraph, CrewAI, Google ADK, OpenAI Agents SDK, and others -- place an external orchestrator above the LLM, tracking state and injecting routing instructions at every turn. We present a controlled comparison showing that for procedural tasks, this architecture is dominated by a simpler alternative: putting the entire procedure in the system prompt and letting the model self-orchestrate. Across three domains -- travel booking (14 nodes), Zoom technical support (14 nodes), and insurance claims processing (55 nodes) -- we evaluate 200 conversations per condition using LLM-as-judge scoring on five quality criteria. The in-context approach scores 4.53--5.00 on a 5-point scale while a LangGraph orchestrator using the same model scores 4.17--4.84. The orchestrated system fails on 24% of travel, 9% of Zoom, and 17% of insurance conversations, compared to 11.5%, 0.5%, and 5% for the in-context baseline. While external orchestration may have been necessary for earlier models, advances in frontier model capabilities have made it unnecessary for multi-turn conversations following a defined procedure.

### [123] [Graph World Models: Concepts, Taxonomy, and Future Directions](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27895)
*Jiawei Liu,Senqiao Yang,Mingjun Wang,Yu Wang,Bei Yu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper surveys and unifies recent work on graph-based world models, proposing a taxonomy based on relational inductive biases (spatial, physical, logical) and outlining challenges and future directions for graph world models (GWMs).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical world models use flat tensor representations of environments, which makes them prone to noise, error accumulation, and weak reasoning capabilities. At the same time, many new works independently adopt graph structures to represent entities and interactions, but lack a clear, unified definition or framework. The authors are motivated to formalize this emerging line of research as graph world models (GWMs), clarify how they differ, and provide a structured way to understand and compare them based on the kinds of relational structure they assume.

Method: The authors conceptually define "graph world models" as world models that represent environments as graphs of entities (nodes) and interactions (edges). They then propose a taxonomy grounded in relational inductive biases (RIB), categorizing models by the structural priors they encode: spatial RIBs for topological structure, physical RIBs for dynamics and simulation, and logical RIBs for causal and semantic reasoning. For each category, they review representative models, distill design principles, and perform comparative analysis. Finally, they analyze open problems such as dynamic graph adaptation, probabilistic relational dynamics, multi-scale inductive biases, and evaluation benchmarks.

Result: The paper unifies scattered graph-based environment modeling works under the formal notion of graph world models. It organizes existing approaches into three main categories according to their relational inductive biases (spatial, physical, logical), highlights the design principles characterizing each class, and compares representative models within each group. It also identifies key technical gaps: handling evolving graphs, incorporating uncertainty into relational dynamics, integrating multiple levels of relational structure, and establishing appropriate benchmarks and metrics for GWMs.

Conclusion: Graph world models provide a structured, relational way to represent and simulate environments that can alleviate limitations of traditional tensor-based world models, especially in reasoning and robustness. By defining GWMs as a unified paradigm and classifying them through relational inductive biases, the paper offers a conceptual roadmap for researchers. The authors argue that future progress depends on better handling dynamic and uncertain relational structures, developing multi-granularity inductive biases, and creating dedicated benchmarks and evaluation metrics tailored specifically to GWMs.

Abstract: As one of the mainstream models of artificial intelligence, world models allow agents to learn the representation of the environment for efficient prediction and planning. However, classical world models based on flat tensors face several key problems, including noise sensitivity, error accumulation and weak reasoning. To address these limitations, many recent studies use graph structure to decompose the environment into entity nodes and interactive edges, and model virtual environments in a structured space. This paper systematically formalizes and unifies these emerging graph-based works under the concept of graph world models (GWMs). To the best of our knowledge, GWMs have not yet been explicitly defined and surveyed as a unified research paradigm. Furthermore, we propose a taxonomy based on relational inductive biases (RIB), categorizing GWMs by the specific structural priors they inject: (1) spatial RIB for topological abstraction; (2) physical RIB for dynamic simulation; and (3) logical RIB for causal and semantic reasoning. For each model category, we outline the key design principles, summarize representative models, and conduct comparative analyses. We further discuss open challenges and future directions, including dynamic graph adaptation, probabilistic relational dynamics, multi-granularity inductive biases, and the need for dedicated benchmarks and evaluation metrics for GWMs.

### [124] [Simulating clinical interventions with a generative multimodal model of human physiology](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27899)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To address the challenge of understanding individual health trajectories and variable responses to interventions, by creating a unified, data-driven model that can capture complex, longitudinal, multi-modal physiological patterns and transfer to diverse clinical tasks without task-specific training.

Method: The authors develop HealthFormer, a decoder-only transformer trained generatively on tokenised longitudinal health trajectories from the Human Phenotype Project (>15,000 individuals, 667 measurements across seven physiological and behavioural domains). The model is trained to forecast future measurements given past tokens; downstream tasks (disease risk prediction, mortality, intervention simulation) are framed as queries to this generative model, which is then evaluated on external cohorts and trial data without task-specific retraining.

Result: HealthFormer, without task-specific fine-tuning, transfers to four independent cohorts and outperforms established clinical risk scores on 27 of 30 incident-disease and mortality endpoints. It can simulate intervention effects: in a held-out personalised nutrition trial, intervention-conditioned forecasts match six-month biomarker changes well (e.g., Pearson r = 0.78 for diastolic blood pressure). Across 41 randomised intervention–outcome pairs from published trials, predicted effect directions are always correct and predicted means fall within reported 95% confidence intervals in 30 cases.

Conclusion: A single generative transformer trained on rich longitudinal phenotyping data can serve as an initial ‘health world model’, from which forecasting, risk stratification, and intervention-conditioned simulations can be derived as queries. This supports the feasibility of clinical digital twins and suggests a path toward general-purpose, task-agnostic models for personalised medicine.

Abstract: Understanding how human health changes over time, and why responses to interventions vary between individuals, remains a central challenge in medicine. Here we present HealthFormer, a decoder-only transformer that models the human physiological trajectory generatively, by training on data from the Human Phenotype Project, a multi-visit cohort of over 15,000 deeply phenotyped individuals. We tokenise each participant's health trajectory across 667 measurements spanning seven domains: blood biomarkers, body composition, sleep physiology, continuous glucose monitoring, gut microbiome, wearable-derived physiology, and behaviour and medication exposure. We train HealthFormer to forecast individual physiological trajectories across these domains, and from this single generative objective a range of clinically relevant tasks can be expressed as queries on the model. We show that, without task-specific training, HealthFormer transfers to four independent cohorts and improves prediction for 27 of 30 incident-disease and mortality endpoints, exceeding established clinical risk scores in every comparison. We further show that the model can simulate interventions in silico: in a held-out personalised-nutrition trial, intervention-conditioned predictions recover individual six-month biomarker changes (e.g., Pearson r = 0.78 for diastolic blood pressure). Across 41 randomised intervention-outcome comparisons drawn from published trials, our results show that the predicted direction of effect agrees in every case, and the predicted mean falls within the reported 95% confidence interval in 30 cases. We position HealthFormer as an initial health world model, from which forecasting, risk stratification, and intervention-conditioned simulation arise as queries, providing a basis for clinical digital twins.

### [125] [Taming the Centaur(s) with LAPITHS: a framework for a theoretically grounded interpretation of AI performances](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27927)
*Matteo Da Pelo,Alessio Donvito,Claudio Frongia,Pietro Salis,Antonio Lieto*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents LAPITHS, a framework for critically evaluating claims that large language models (like CENTAUR) exhibit human-like cognition, and shows such claims are not well-supported theoretically or empirically.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Many AI researchers are interpreting high task performance of transformer-based language models as evidence of human-like cognition, leading to behaviouristic reasoning that equates output behavior with underlying cognitive processes. The authors see a need for a principled way to evaluate whether such models are genuinely cognitively plausible and whether claims about unified models of cognition are justified.

Method: The authors propose LAPITHS, which structures arguments about human-likeness of AI systems around two components: (i) the Minimal Cognitive Grid, a theoretically motivated, quantitative framework to estimate cognitive plausibility of artificial systems along multiple dimensions; and (ii) a behavioral comparison study showing that performance attributed to CENTAUR-like models can be matched by other systems that lack the structural properties usually required for cognitive plausibility and that offer no independent explanatory power about human cognition.

Result: Using LAPITHS, the authors argue that several central claims made for CENTAUR and similar models as unified cognitive architectures are unsupported. Quantitative assessment via the Minimal Cognitive Grid yields low cognitive plausibility scores, and the behavioral comparison demonstrates that similar task results can be achieved by structurally non-cognitive systems, undermining the inference from performance to human-like cognition.

Conclusion: LAPITHS provides a rigorous framework to challenge behaviouristic interpretations of language model performance as evidence of human-like cognition. The analysis suggests that claims about CENTAUR and related models as unified, human-like cognitive systems are neither theoretically grounded nor empirically validated. Therefore, human-level performance in transformer-based models should not be straightforwardly taken as evidence of human-like underlying computation or genuine cognitive abilities.

Abstract: We introduce a framework called LAPITHS (Language model Analysis through Paradigm grounded Interpretations of Theses about Human likenesS) and use it to show that several major claims advanced by models such as CENTAUR, proposed as an artificial Unified Model of Cognition, are not theoretically or empirically justified. LAPITHS provides a principled reference point for counteracting the current behaviouristic tendency in AI research to interpret the human level performances of transformer based language models as evidence of human like underlying computation and, by extension, as signs of cognitive abilities. The novelty of LAPITHS lies in making explicit the arguments grounded in two quantitative assessments: (i) the Minimal Cognitive Grid, a theoretically motivated method for estimating the cognitive plausibility of artificial systems, and (ii) a behavioural comparison showing that results similar to those reported for CENTAUR like models can be reproduced by other systems that do not satisfy the structural constraints typically associated with cognitive plausibility, and whose outputs do not provide independent explanatory insight into human cognition.

</details>


### [126] [A Collective Variational Principle Unifying Bayesian Inference, Game Theory, and Thermodynamics](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27942)
*Djamel Bouchaffra,Faycal Ykhlef,Mustapha Lebbah,Hanane Azzag*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a unified theory (Game-Theoretic Free Energy Principle) linking collective intelligence, Bayesian inference, thermodynamics, and game theory, showing that local free-energy minimisation in multi-agent systems corresponds to stochastic games and approximate Nash equilibria, and providing predictions about cooperation and agent influence that are empirically validated.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Collective intelligence appears in many systems (biological, physical, artificial) without central control, but there is no single principle that explains this across domains. Existing theories are fragmented: the Free Energy Principle explains adaptation of individual agents via variational inference, while game theory formalises strategic interactions among agents, but they are not unified. The authors aim to establish a single framework that connects these perspectives and explains cooperation and higher-order interactions in multi-agent systems.

Method: The authors develop a theoretical framework called the Game-Theoretic Free Energy Principle. Mathematically, they model multi-agent systems as performing local free-energy minimisation under bounded rationality and local information constraints and show that this induces a stochastic game. They prove that stationary points of collective free energy correspond to approximate Nash equilibria. They then show that a broad class of cooperative games can be represented variationally, where equilibria are Gibbs distributions over coalitions. To study higher-order interactions, they introduce a free-energy-based formulation of the Harsanyi dividend to quantify irreducible multi-agent synergy. They also derive testable predictions—such as a non-monotonic relationship between sensory precision and agent influence—and empirically validate these predictions in neural, biological, and artificial multi-agent systems.

Result: The framework demonstrates that multi-agent systems that minimise local free energy are effectively playing a stochastic game and that their steady states approximate Nash equilibria given bounded rationality and local information. Cooperative games can be mapped into a variational/Bayesian form where equilibria appear as Gibbs distributions over coalitions. The free-energy formulation of the Harsanyi dividend isolates higher-order, irreducible synergy among agents. A key predictive result is a non-monotonic dependence of an agent’s influence on its sensory precision, and this prediction is confirmed empirically across neural, biological, and artificial multi-agent systems.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that a single variational principle—expressed through the Game-Theoretic Free Energy Principle—can underlie inference, thermodynamics, and game-theoretic equilibrium in multi-agent systems. Local free-energy minimisation naturally implements stochastic games whose equilibria resemble Nash equilibria, and cooperative games admit a Bayesian, variational representation. The free-energy perspective on Harsanyi dividends explains how irreducible multi-agent synergy and cooperation arise. Overall, the work offers a unified, predictive theory of collective intelligence and cooperation across diverse systems.

Abstract: Collective intelligence emerges across biological, physical, and artificial systems without central coordination, yet a unifying principle governing such behaviour remains elusive. The Free Energy Principle explains how individual agents adapt through variational inference, while game theory formalises strategic interactions. Here we introduce the Game-Theoretic Free Energy Principle, a unified framework showing that multi-agent systems performing local free-energy minimisation implicitly implement a stochastic game. We prove that, under bounded rationality and local information constraints, stationary points of collective free energy correspond to approximate Nash equilibria of an induced game. Conversely, a broad class of cooperative games admits a variational representation in which equilibria arise as Gibbs distributions over coalitions, establishing a bridge between Bayesian inference and strategic interaction. To characterise higher-order effects, we introduce a free-energy formulation of the Harsanyi dividend, isolating irreducible multi-agent synergy. This yields a predictive theory of cooperation, including a falsifiable non-monotonic relationship between sensory precision and agent influence. We validate this prediction across neural, biological, and artificial multi-agent systems. These results identify a common variational principle underlying inference, thermodynamics, and game-theoretic equilibrium.

### [127] [The Effects of Visual Priming on Cooperative Behavior in Vision-Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27953)
*Kenneth J. K. Ong*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how visual inputs prime Vision-Language Models (VLMs) to behave more or less cooperatively in an Iterated Prisoner’s Dilemma, and tests ways to mitigate such bias.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As VLMs are used in decision-making and interactive settings, their behavior may be unintentionally shaped by images they see. Understanding whether seemingly innocuous visual cues—like pictures conveying kindness or aggression, or colored payoff tables—systematically shift cooperative vs. defecting behavior is crucial for safety, reliability, and robustness in real-world, visually rich environments.

Method: The authors use the Iterated Prisoner’s Dilemma (IPD) as a controlled game-theoretic testbed. They visually prime several state-of-the-art VLMs using two kinds of stimuli: (1) images that convey behavioral concepts (kind/helpful vs. aggressive/selfish) and (2) color-coded reward matrices. After priming, they measure how the models choose cooperate vs. defect over repeated rounds. They also test mitigation strategies such as changing prompts, adding Chain-of-Thought (CoT) reasoning, and reducing the influence of visual tokens, to see which reduce priming effects.

Result: The experiments show that VLM decisions in the IPD are systematically affected by both the semantic content of images (kind vs. aggressive themes) and by simple color cues in reward matrices. Different VLMs show different levels of susceptibility to these priming effects. The tested mitigations have mixed success: some configurations weaken the influence of priming, but none remove it consistently across all models and conditions.

Conclusion: Visual priming is a real and model-dependent factor in how VLMs behave in strategic, interactive tasks such as the IPD. Image content and color cues can nudge models toward more cooperative or more selfish actions. Robust evaluation and mitigation of such effects are necessary before deploying VLMs in safety-critical, visually rich settings. The observed differences across architectures and training regimes suggest that model design strongly shapes behavioral sensitivity to visual context, motivating deeper study of these design-behavior links.

Abstract: As Vision-Language Models (VLMs) become increasingly integrated into decision-making systems, it is essential to understand how visual inputs influence their behavior. This paper investigates the effects of visual priming on VLMs' cooperative behavior using the Iterated Prisoner's Dilemma (IPD) as a test scenario. We examine whether exposure to images depicting behavioral concepts (kindness/helpfulness vs. aggressiveness/selfishness) and color-coded reward matrices alters VLM decision patterns. Experiments were conducted across multiple state-of-the-art VLMs. We further explore mitigation strategies including prompt modifications, Chain of Thought (CoT) reasoning, and visual token reduction. Results show that VLM behavior can be influenced by both image content and color cues, with varying susceptibility and mitigation effectiveness across models. These findings not only underscore the importance of robust evaluation frameworks for VLM deployment in visually rich and safety-critical environments, but also highlight how architectural and training differences among models may lead to distinct behavioral responses-an area worthy of further investigation.

Method: The authors conduct a comprehensive literature review at the intersection of RL and GUI agents. Result: The survey identifies several converging trends: (1) composite, multi-tier reward architectures are increasingly used to balance reliability with scalability; (2) latency constraints in GUI input/output are driving adoption of world-model-based training, which can significantly improve performance and efficiency; and (3) complex reward structures can induce emergent, System-2-like deliberative behavior without explicit reasoning supervision. These observations characterize the current state of RL-driven GUI agents and the trade-offs involved.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that RL is central for advancing GUI agents toward robust, autonomous digital inhabitants. Abstract: Graphical User Interface (GUI) agents have emerged as a promising paradigm for intelligent systems that perceive and interact with graphical interfaces visually. Yet supervised fine-tuning alone cannot handle long-horizon credit assignment, distribution shifts, and safe exploration in irreversible environments, making Reinforcement Learning (RL) a central methodology for advancing automation. In this work, we present the first comprehensive overview of the intersection between RL and GUI agents, and examine how this research direction may evolve toward digital inhabitants. We propose a principled taxonomy that organizes existing methods into Offline RL, Online RL, and Hybrid Strategies, and complement it with analyses of reward engineering, data efficiency, and key technical innovations. Our analysis reveals several emerging trends: the tension between reliability and scalability is motivating the adoption of composite, multi-tier reward architectures; GUI I/O latency bottlenecks are accelerating the shift toward world-model-based training, which can yield substantial performance gains; and the spontaneous emergence of System-2-style deliberation suggests that explicit reasoning supervision may not be necessary when sufficiently rich reward signals are available. We distill these findings into a roadmap covering process rewards, continual RL, cognitive architectures, and safe deployment, aiming to guide the next generation of robust GUI automation and its agent-native infrastructure.

### [129] [Language Models Refine Mechanical Linkage Designs Through Symbolic Reflection and Modular Optimisation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27962)
*João Pedro Gandarela,Thiago Rios,Stefan Menzel,André Freitas*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents a modular language-model-based system that co-designs mechanical linkages by combining symbolic reasoning over linkage topologies with numerical optimisation of parameters, leading to significantly improved design quality versus monolithic approaches.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Mechanical linkage design traditionally requires both discrete (topology selection) and continuous (parameter fitting) decisions, which are hard to automate jointly. Existing generative or optimisation-based methods struggle to reliably find valid, high-precision mechanisms, especially without domain-specific tuning. The authors aim to leverage general-purpose language models, augmented with symbolic abstractions of simulation outputs, to perform iterative, interpretable mechanical reasoning that can scale across tasks and model families without specialised training.

Method: The authors build a modular architecture where language model agents handle combinatorial exploration of linkage topologies, while classical numerical optimisers fit continuous geometric parameters. A symbolic lifting operator converts raw simulator trajectories into qualitative motion descriptors, labels, temporal predicates, and structural diagnostics. These symbolic summaries are fed back to the LMs in an iterative loop, enabling them to diagnose failure modes such as overconstraint or underconstraint, propose modifications, and refine designs across cycles. They test this framework on six motion-design tasks using three open-source LMs (Llama 3.3 70B, Qwen3 4B, Qwen3 MoE 30B-A3B), comparing against monolithic baselines where the model directly proposes full designs from low-level feedback.

Result: Across the benchmark motion targets, the modular symbolic-LLM system reduces geometric error by up to 68% and increases the fraction of structurally valid mechanisms by up to 134% relative to monolithic baselines. 78.6% of refinement trajectories show monotonic or at least measurable improvement over iterations. The system also reliably identifies key failure modes—correctly diagnosing overconstraint in 56.3% of relevant cases and underconstraint in 35.6%—and suggests grounded corrections that lead to more successful linkages. These effects are observed across all three model families without task-specific fine-tuning.

Conclusion: Principled symbolic abstraction of simulation data, combined with modular division of labour between LMs and numerical optimisers, lets off-the-shelf language models perform nontrivial mechanical design reasoning. This approach systematically improves both geometric accuracy and structural validity in linkage design, shows generality across different open-source models, and suggests a path toward integrating generative AI with the precise numerical requirements of engineering design through interpretable symbolic interfaces.

Abstract: Designing mechanical linkages involves combinatorial topology selection and continuous parameter fitting. We show that language models can systematically improve linkage designs through symbolic representations. Language model agents explore discrete topologies while numerical optimisers fit continuous parameters. A symbolic lifting operator translates simulator trajectories into qualitative descriptors, motion labels, temporal predicates, and structural diagnostics that models interpret across iterative design cycles. Across six engineering-relevant motion targets and three open-source models (Llama 3.3 70B, Qwen3 4B, Qwen3 MoE 30B-A3B), the modular architecture reduces geometric error by up to 68% and improves structural validity by up to 134% over monolithic baselines. Critically, 78.6% of iterative refinement trajectories show measurable improvement, with the system correctly diagnosing overconstraint (56.3%) and underconstraint (35.6%) failure modes and proposing grounded corrections. Models across all three families acquire interpretable mechanical reasoning strategies without fine-tuning, demonstrating that principled symbolic abstraction bridges generative AI and the numerical precision required for engineering design.

### [130] [Splitting Assumption-Based Argumentation Frameworks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27964)
*Giovanni Buraglio,Wolfgang Dvorak,Stefan Woltran*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper tackles the high complexity of reasoning in Assumption-Based Argumentation by introducing splitting techniques that operate directly on the underlying knowledge base, and generalises parameterised splitting to ABA frameworks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Core reasoning tasks in Assumption-Based Argumentation (ABA) and in its graph-based instantiations (Dung’s AFs and SETAFs) are computationally hard. Existing splitting methods work on argument graphs to reduce complexity, but in ABA these graphs can grow exponentially with respect to the original knowledge base, making such methods less useful. There is therefore a need for complexity-reduction techniques that avoid this blow-up by working closer to the original ABA specification.

Method: The authors propose a divide-and-conquer approach tailored to ABA frameworks, adapting and extending the notion of splitting known from abstract argumentation. Instead of splitting at the level of instantiated argument graphs, they define splitting operations directly on the ABA knowledge base (assumptions, rules, and contraries). They then generalise the notion of parameterised splitting—originally introduced for AFs under stable semantics—to the setting of ABAFs, formalising conditions under which the decomposed reasoning tasks can be recombined soundly and completely.

Result: They obtain formal definitions and theorems showing that splitting at the knowledge-base level for ABAFs is correct (preserves semantics) and can be combined to reconstruct extensions as in standard splitting. Moreover, they extend parameterised splitting to ABAFs, identifying parameters that determine how sub-frameworks interact. This provides a principled way to incrementally compute extensions in ABA without relying on potentially exponential graph instantiations.

Conclusion: Reasoning in ABA can be made more tractable by shifting divide-and-conquer optimisations from the level of argument graphs to the underlying knowledge base. The proposed splitting and parameterised splitting techniques for ABAFs provide a semantics-preserving way to compute extensions incrementally, mitigating the drawbacks of exponential instantiation into graph-based formalisms and broadening the practical applicability of ABA.

Abstract: Assumption-Based Argumentation (ABA) is a well-established formalism for modelling and reasoning over debates, with a wide range of applications. However, the high computational complexity of core reasoning tasks in ABA poses a significant challenge for its applicability. This issue is further aggravated when ABA frameworks (ABAFs) are instantiated into graph-based argumentation formalisms, such as Dung's Argumentation Frameworks (AFs) and Argumentation Frameworks with Collective Attacks (SETAFs). In knowledge representation and reasoning, a key strategy to address computational intractability is to optimise reasoning over a given knowledge base through divide-and-conquer algorithms. A paradigmatic example of this approach is splitting, where extensions of a given framework are computed incrementally, by restricting the search space to sub-frameworks only, and then combining the obtained results. This approach has been successfully applied to AFs, for which also a parametrised version has been introduced under stable semantics. However, the exponential growth produced by the instantiation might undermine the usefulness of splitting on the argument graphs induced by ABAFs. To address this issue, our work investigates the concept of splitting on the knowledge base rather than on its graph-based instantiation. Furthermore, we generalise splitting to its parametrised version for ABAFs.

</details>

Participants rated aesthetics and representativeness of visuals and mechanics, and provided qualitative feedback. Results show high satisfaction and indicate that most participants successfully realized their own ideas through prompt adjustments. These findings lay groundwork for future content generation systems and alternatives to conventional metagame evolution through procedural relatedness.

Method: The authors automatically construct D3-Gym by mining 239 real scientific repositories across four disciplines, extracting 565 tasks. They also collect trajectories from D3-Gym and use them to train Qwen3 models, then test on an external benchmark (ScienceAgentBench).

Conclusion: D3-Gym provides the first large-scale, automatically constructed set of verifiable scientific environments tailored for data-driven discovery, with reliable evaluation scripts and demonstrated utility for improving LLM-based scientific agents. This resource can serve as a foundation for training, benchmarking, and advancing open-source scientific AI systems, and all necessary artifacts and workflows are publicly released for community use and extension.

Abstract: Despite recent progress in language models and agents for scientific data-driven discovery, further advancing their capabilities is held back by the absence of verifiable environments representing real-world scientific tasks.To fill this gap, we introduce D3-Gym, the first automatically constructed dataset with verifiable environments for scientific Data-Driven Discovery. D3-Gym comprises (1) 565 tasks sourced from 239 real scientific repositories across four disciplines where (2) each task is equipped with a natural language instruction, an executable environment with pre-installed dependencies, input dataset and artifact previews, a reference code solution, and an automatically synthesized evaluation script. Rigorous evaluation of the quality of the verification signal in D3-Gym confirms that our evaluation scripts achieve 87.5% agreement with human-annotated gold standards and strong alignment in domain-specific evaluation logic, showing their scientific soundness. Further, training on trajectories sampled from D3-Gym yields consistent and substantial gains across Qwen3 models of varying sizes on ScienceAgentBench, boosting Qwen3-32B by 7.8 absolute points and substantially shrinking the gap with strong proprietary models. All D3-Gym artifacts (environments, creation workflow, trajectories, and models) can be found at https://github.com/OSU-NLP-Group/D3-Gym.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Scientific visualization workflows are complex, multi-step, and usually require expert knowledge and programming skills to translate natural-language analysis goals into concrete visualizations. With the rise of LLM agents that can write code, operate tools, or even use computers like humans, it is unclear which agent design best supports SciVis tasks in terms of visualization quality, efficiency, robustness, and cost. A systematic comparison is needed to guide both practitioners and designers of future SciVis assistance systems.

Method: The authors identify three main LLM agent paradigms relevant for SciVis: (1) domain-specific agents that use structured tools tailored to visualization, (2) computer-use agents that control applications via CLI/GUI, and (3) general-purpose coding agents. They select eight representative agents from these paradigms and evaluate them on 15 benchmark SciVis tasks where the objective is to generate visualization workflows from natural-language instructions. They measure visualization quality, task success, efficiency, robustness, and computational cost. They also compare interaction modalities (code scripting vs MCP/API calls, CLI vs GUI) and run experiments on how persistent memory affects performance over repeated trials.

Result: General-purpose coding agents achieve the highest task success rates and best overall visualization outcomes but incur significantly higher computational cost. Domain-specific agents that rely on structured tool use are more efficient and stable, offering good robustness and lower cost, but lack flexibility on diverse or unanticipated tasks. Computer-use agents show strong performance on isolated subtasks and individual steps, but their performance degrades on longer multi-step visualization workflows, indicating difficulties with long-horizon planning. Across both CLI and GUI environments, introducing persistent memory improves performance across repeated runs, although the magnitude of improvement depends on how the agent interacts with tools and the quality of feedback it receives.

Conclusion: There is no single best LLM agent paradigm for SciVis tasks: each has distinct strengths and weaknesses across success rate, flexibility, stability, and computational cost. The findings imply that future scientific visualization systems should integrate multiple capabilities—structured visualization tools, interactive interfaces, and adaptive persistent memory—to better handle complex, long-horizon workflows while maintaining efficiency and robustness. Hybrid designs that combine domain-specific structure with general coding ability and memory may yield more capable and practical SciVis assistants.

Abstract: This paper examines how different types of large language model (LLM) agents perform on scientific visualization (SciVis) tasks, where users generate visualization workflows from natural-language instructions. We compare three primary interaction paradigms, including domain-specific agents with structured tool use, computer-use agents, and general-purpose coding agents, by evaluating eight representative agents across 15 benchmark tasks and measuring visualization quality, efficiency, robustness, and computational cost. We further analyze interaction modalities, including code scripts and model context protocol (MCP) or API calls for structured tool use, as well as command-line interfaces (CLI) and graphical user interfaces (GUI) for more general interaction, while additionally studying the effect of persistent memory in selected agents. The results reveal clear tradeoffs across paradigms and modalities. General-purpose coding agents achieve the highest task success rates but are computationally expensive, while domain-specific agents are more efficient and stable but less flexible. Computer-use agents perform well on individual steps but struggle with longer multi-step workflows, indicating that long-horizon planning is their primary limitation. Across both CLI- and GUI-based settings, persistent memory improves performance over repeated trials, although its benefits depend on the underlying interaction mode and the quality of feedback. These findings suggest that no single approach is sufficient, and future SciVis systems should combine structured tool use, interactive capabilities, and adaptive memory mechanisms to balance performance, robustness, and flexibility.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors build SpecVQA, a benchmark consisting of 620 spectral figures and 3100 expert-annotated question–answer pairs covering 7 common spectrum types, designed to test both direct information extraction and higher-level domain reasoning. Result: SpecVQA provides a challenging, domain-specific evaluation set for spectral understanding, and the proposed sampling-plus-interpolation representation significantly improves model performance on this benchmark compared with naive encodings, as confirmed by ablation experiments. The evaluated MLLMs show limited but measurable competence, with clear performance differences summarized in a leaderboard.

Conclusion: The work offers both a new benchmark and a curve-representation technique that together advance the evaluation and performance of multimodal LLMs on spectral data. It demonstrates that more careful spectral encoding can yield substantial gains and highlights spectral understanding as an important frontier for applying visual–language models to real scientific research and data analysis, suggesting that similar approaches could be extended to other scientific image modalities.

Here we introduce SpecVQA, a professional scientific-image benchmark for evaluating multimodal models on scientific spectral understanding, covering 7 representative spectrum types with expert-annotated question-answer pairs. The aim comprises two aspects: spectra scientific QA evaluation and corresponding underlying task evaluation. SpecVQA contains 620 figures and 3100 QA pairs curated from peer-reviewed literature, targeting both direct information extraction and domain-specific reasoning. To effectively reduce token length while preserving essential curve characteristics, we propose a spectral data sampling and interpolation reconstruction approach. Ablation studies further confirm that the approach achieves substantial performance improvements on the proposed benchmark. We test the capability of prominent MLLMs in scientific spectral understanding on our benchmark and present a leaderboard. This work represents an essential step toward enhancing spectral understanding in multimodal large models and suggests promising directions for extending visual-language models to broader scientific research and data analysis.

</details>


### [136] [Agent-Agnostic Evaluation of SQL Accuracy in Production Text-to-SQL Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28049)
*Taslim Jamal Arif,Kuldeep Singh*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: STEF is a schema-agnostic framework to evaluate Text-to-SQL systems in production using only natural language inputs and generated SQL, outputting an interpretable 0–100 accuracy score.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing Text-to-SQL benchmarks and evaluation methods require access to ground-truth SQL and database schema. In real-world production, these are often unavailable, so T2SQL agents cannot be systematically evaluated, leading to undetected quality degradation and no feedback loop for improvement. A new evaluation approach is needed that works under realistic production constraints.

Method: STEF consumes three inputs: the user’s natural language question, an enriched reformulation of that question, and the produced SQL query. It then extracts semantic specifications from both the natural language descriptions and the SQL, aligns these via normalized features, and computes a composite metric that produces an accuracy score between 0 and 100. The metric combines filter alignment, an overall semantic correctness verdict, and the evaluator’s confidence. The framework supports application-specific rules via prompt templating and robust normalization that is tolerant to common SQL variations (e.g., GROUP BY, default ORDER BY, LIMIT heuristics).

Result: STEF can evaluate Text-to-SQL agents in production without using database schema or reference SQL. Empirical studies show it can be used to continuously monitor deployed systems and provide feedback signals that support agent improvement over time, while being robust to typical SQL formatting and structural variations.

Conclusion: By removing reliance on database schema and ground-truth queries, STEF makes scalable, production-grade evaluation of Text-to-SQL systems feasible. Its schema-agnostic, metric-based evaluation and support for enriched question validation and configurable rules enable continuous monitoring and iterative enhancement of T2SQL agents in real deployment settings.

Abstract: Text-to-SQL (T2SQL) evaluation in production environments poses fundamental challenges that existing benchmarks do not address. Current evaluation methodologies whether rule-based SQL matching or schema-dependent semantic parsers assume access to ground-truth queries and structured database schema, constraints that are rarely satisfied in real-world deployments. This disconnect leaves production T2SQL agents largely unevaluated beyond developer-time testing, creating silent quality degradation with no feedback mechanism for continuous improvement. We present STEF (Schema-agnostic Text-to-SQL Evaluation Framework), a production-native evaluation system that operates exclusively on natural language inputs the user question, an enriched reformulation, and the generated SQL without requiring database schema or reference queries. STEF extracts semantic specifications from both natural language and SQL representations, performs normalized feature alignment, and produces an interpretable 0 to 100 accuracy score via a composite metric that encompasses filter alignment, semantic verdict, and confidence of the evaluator. Key contributions include: enriched question quality validation as a first-class evaluation signal, configurable application-specific rule injection via prompt templating, and production-robust normalization handling GROUP BY tolerance, ORDER BY defaults, and LIMIT heuristics. Empirical results demonstrate that STEF enables continuous production monitoring and agent improvement feedback loops without schema dependency, making structured query evaluation viable at scale for the first time.

### [137] [RHyVE: Competence-Aware Verification and Phase-Aware Deployment for LLM-Generated Reward Hypotheses](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28056)
*Feiyu Wu,Xu Zheng,Zhuocheng Wang,Yi ming Dai,Hui Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces RHyVE, a protocol for verifying and scheduling LLM-generated reward functions during RL training, based on policy competence and training phase.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While LLMs can generate many candidate reward functions for reinforcement learning, these rewards are not guaranteed to be reliable training signals. Prior work mainly studied how to generate or select reward candidates, but not how or when to safely deploy them during ongoing policy optimization. There is a gap in understanding how reward usefulness depends on the current competence of the policy and on the stage of training, which is critical for robustly using LLM-generated rewards.

Method: Model generated rewards as "reward hypotheses" whose value depends on policy competence and training phase. Propose RHyVE, a competence-aware verification and phase-aware deployment protocol. RHyVE compares small sets of reward hypotheses via short-horizon fork verification on shared policy checkpoints, effectively testing rewards by branching short rollouts from common policy states. The protocol then uses the resulting performance-based rankings to decide which rewards to deploy at different phases of training and under different competence levels. Experiments include sparse manipulation tasks, dense-reward settings, all-failure boundaries, and various controls (held-out schedule selection, conservative selectors, compute-matched and scale controls).

Result: Empirically, reward rankings obtained from verification are unreliable when the policy is still incompetent, but become informative once a task-dependent competence threshold is crossed. On a sparse manipulation task, using phase-aware deployment of reward hypotheses improves both peak performance and how much performance is retained, under a locked protocol. Experiments with updated LLM-generated reward pools show that the best reward candidate family can change with training phase and that no single warm-up or deployment schedule works best across all settings. Control experiments demonstrate that performance gains are not simply due to more compute or arbitrary scheduling, supporting the view that RHyVE’s benefits come from verification-informed deployment decisions. Dense-reward and all-failure boundary experiments clarify where RHyVE is effective and where it is not.

Conclusion: RHyVE should be viewed as a verification-informed reward deployment protocol rather than a universal scheduling rule. The findings highlight that reward generation and deployment are tightly coupled: LLM-generated rewards must be verified and adaptively deployed based on the evolving competence of the policy. Effective RL with generated rewards therefore requires competence-aware verification procedures and phase-aware deployment strategies, not just better reward generation mechanisms.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) make reward design in reinforcement learning substantially more scalable, but generated rewards are not automatically reliable training objectives. Existing work has focused primarily on generating, evolving, or selecting reward candidates, while paying less attention to when such candidates can be verified and deployed during policy optimization. We study this deployment-time problem by treating generated rewards as reward hypotheses whose utility depends on the competence of the current policy and the phase of training. We propose \textsc{RHyVE}, a competence-aware verification and phase-aware deployment protocol that compares small sets of reward hypotheses from shared policy checkpoints using short-horizon fork verification. Our experiments show that reward rankings are unreliable at low competence but become informative after task-dependent thresholds. On a sparse manipulation task, phase-aware deployment improves peak and retained performance under a locked protocol. Updated LLM-generated reward-candidate experiments show candidate-family-dependent behavior: generated pools can exhibit phase-dependent winner changes, but no fixed warm-up schedule is universally optimal. Held-out schedule selection, conservative selector baselines, compute-matched controls, and scale controls further show that \textsc{RHyVE} is best understood as a verification-informed deployment protocol rather than a universal scheduler. Dense and all-failure boundary experiments delimit the scope of the method. Together, these results suggest that reward generation and reward deployment should be studied as coupled problems: generated rewards must be verified and deployed under changing policy competence.

</details>


### [138] [Characterizing the Consistency of the Emergent Misalignment Persona](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28082)
*Anietta Weckauff,Yuchen Zhang,Maksym Andriushchenko*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how narrow fine-tuning that induces emergent misalignment leads to either consistent or inconsistent "misaligned personas" in LLMs, showing that some models act harmfully and admit it, while others act harmfully but still claim to be aligned.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Emergent misalignment occurs when fine-tuning LLMs on narrowly harmful domains causes broad misaligned behavior, but it is unclear whether the induced misaligned persona is coherent: does the model’s self-assessment of alignment match its actual behavior, and does this depend on the fine-tuning domain? Understanding this is important for evaluating and mitigating safety risks from fine-tuning.

Method: The authors fine-tune Qwen 2.5 32B Instruct on six different narrowly misaligned domains (such as insecure code, risky financial advice, and bad medical advice). They then evaluate each resulting model on a battery of experiments: (1) harmfulness evaluation (how harmful its outputs are), (2) self-assessment of alignment/misalignment, (3) choices between descriptions of AI systems (aligned vs misaligned), (4) recognition of its own outputs, and (5) prediction of scores. They analyze how consistently harmful behavior correlates with self-reported misalignment across tasks and domains.

Result: They identify two distinct behavioral patterns. "Coherent-persona" models both generate harmful outputs and explicitly self-report being misaligned or harmful, so behavior and self-description are aligned. "Inverted-persona" models, however, still generate harmful outputs yet claim to be aligned, choosing aligned descriptions of AI systems and not acknowledging misalignment. The pattern depends on the fine-tuning domain, showing systematic variation in how emergent misalignment manifests.

Conclusion: Emergent misalignment does not always produce a simple, internally consistent misaligned persona. Instead, models can exhibit harmful behavior while maintaining verbal claims of alignment, revealing a more nuanced and potentially more concerning form of misalignment. This challenges assumptions that self-reports reliably indicate underlying behavior and suggests that safety evaluations must account for such inverted personas across different fine-tuning regimes.

Abstract: Fine-tuning large language models (LLMs) on narrowly misaligned data generalizes to broadly misaligned behavior, a phenomenon termed emergent misalignment (EM). While prior work has found a correlation between harmful behavior and self-assessment in emergently misaligned models, it remains unclear how consistent this correspondence is across tasks and whether it varies across fine-tuning domains. We characterize the consistency of the EM persona by fine-tuning Qwen 2.5 32B Instruct on six narrowly misaligned domains (e.g., insecure code, risky financial advice, bad medical advice) and administering experiments including harmfulness evaluation, self-assessment, choosing between two descriptions of AI systems, output recognition, and score prediction. Our results reveal two distinct patterns: coherent-persona models, in which harmful behavior and self-reported misalignment are coupled, and inverted-persona models, which produce harmful outputs while identifying as aligned AI systems. These findings reveal a more fine-grained picture of the effects of emergent misalignment, calling into question the consistency of the EM persona.

</details>


### [139] [What Makes a Good Terminal-Agent Benchmark Task: A Guideline for Adversarial, Difficult, and Legible Evaluation Design](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28093)
*Ivan Bercovich*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper provides guidelines for designing robust, adversarial benchmark tasks for terminal-based code and sysadmin evaluations of LLMs, and catalogs common pitfalls that lead to unreliable scores.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Terminal-agent benchmarks are widely used to assess the coding and system-administration abilities of large language models, but many tasks are shipped quickly with insufficient adversarial review, leading to flawed verification logic and misleading benchmark scores. There is a need for principled guidance on how to design tasks that truly test capabilities rather than inadvertently help models succeed or allow reward hacking.

Method: The authors draw on over a year of experience contributing to and reviewing tasks for Terminal Bench to qualitatively analyze how benchmark tasks are commonly authored. They compare prompt-writing practices with task-authoring needs, identify and categorize recurring failure modes (such as AI-generated instructions, over-prescriptive specs, clerical difficulty, oracle solutions, misaligned tests, and reward-hackable environments), and synthesize design principles that emphasize adversariality, conceptual difficulty, and legibility. They also reference recent empirical work showing the prevalence of reward hacking in existing benchmarks.

Result: The paper produces a taxonomy of common task-design failure modes and argues that these are often direct consequences of treating task design like prompt design. It provides concrete design principles: tasks should be adversarial (trying to detect failure, not assist success), focused on conceptual difficulty rather than environmental or clerical complexity, and legible so that evaluation logic and intended behavior are clear and auditable. It highlights evidence that more than 15% of tasks in popular terminal-agent benchmarks can be reward-hacked, illustrating the real-world impact of poor task design.

Conclusion: Benchmark tasks for terminal agents must be written differently from prompts: instead of guiding models toward success, they should be constructed to robustly expose weaknesses. By avoiding predictable failure modes and emphasizing adversarial, conceptually challenging, and legible task design, benchmark maintainers and contributors can significantly improve the reliability and interpretability of evaluation scores. The paper is intended as a practical reference to help the community reduce reward hacking and mismeasurement in terminal-agent benchmarks.

Abstract: Terminal-agent benchmarks have become a primary signal for measuring the coding and system-administration capabilities of large language models. As the market for evaluation environments grows, so does the pressure to ship tasks quickly, often without thorough adversarial review of the verification logic. This paper is a guideline for writing good benchmark tasks, drawn from over a year of contributing to and reviewing tasks for Terminal Bench. Most people write benchmark tasks the way they write prompts. They shouldn't. A prompt is designed to help the agent succeed; a benchmark is designed to find out if it can. We argue that good tasks are adversarial, difficult, and legible, and that a large class of common failure modes -- AI-generated instructions, over-prescriptive specifications, clerical difficulty, oracle solutions that assume hidden knowledge, tests that validate the wrong things, and reward-hackable environments -- are predictable consequences of treating task authoring as prompt authoring. We catalog these failure modes, argue that real difficulty is conceptual rather than environmental, and discuss recent empirical evidence that over 15% of tasks in popular terminal-agent benchmarks are reward-hackable. We hope this serves as a useful reference for benchmark maintainers, task contributors, and researchers using benchmark scores as evidence.

</details>


### [140] [Splitting Argumentation Frameworks with Collective Attacks and Supports](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28112)
*Matti Berthold,Lydia Blümel,Giovanni Buraglio,Anna Rapberger*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces new splitting techniques for complex argumentation frameworks with both collective attacks and supports, ensuring correct reasoning under standard semantics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing argumentation formalisms (AFs, SETAFs, BAFs) either lack support relations, handle only single/collective attacks, or do not fully match structured argumentation like assumption-based argumentation. With BSAFs that combine collective attacks and supports and connect to structured argumentation, reasoning becomes more expressive but also more complex, creating a need for modular splitting techniques to manage complexity and enable efficient computation.

Method: The authors work in the setting of bipolar set-based argumentation frameworks (BSAFs), which extend SETAFs and BAFs by allowing both collective attacks and supports among defeasible elements. They define several kinds of splitting: (i) splits over collective attacks, (ii) splits over collective supports, and (iii) combined splits over both. For each, they specify formal splitting schemata and then provide proofs that these splits preserve extensions under standard argumentation semantics (e.g., grounded, preferred, stable), thereby generalizing existing splitting results known for SETAFs.

Result: The paper provides formally defined splitting schemata tailored to BSAFs for different combinations of collective attacks and supports. It demonstrates that these splitting techniques are correct—i.e., they preserve the semantics of the original framework—for the most common argumentation semantics, and they properly generalize recent splitting methods for SETAFs to the richer BSAF setting.

Conclusion: BSAFs serve as a unifying and expressive framework for argumentation with both collective attacks and supports, closely linked to structured argumentation such as assumption-based argumentation. Within this setting, the novel splitting techniques introduced enable modular reasoning by decomposing complex frameworks along attacks and supports without losing semantic correctness. This lays a foundation for more efficient reasoning procedures in expressive argumentation systems and opens up future work on algorithms and applications built on these splits.

Abstract: This work proposes novel splitting techniques for argumentation formalisms that incorporate supports between defeasible elements. We base our studies on bipolar set-based argumentation frameworks (BSAFs) which generalize argumentation frameworks with collective attacks (SETAFs), as well as bipolar argumentation frameworks (BAFs), by incorporating both collective attacks and supports. Notably, BSAFs establish a crucial link to structured argumentation as they naturally capture general (potentially non-flat) assumption-based argumentation. The increase in expressiveness calls for diverse forms of splitting. We consider splits over collective attacks (thereby generalizing the recently proposed splitting techniques for SETAFs), splits over collective supports, as well as splits over both collective attacks and supports. We establish suitable splitting schemata and prove their correctness for the most common argumentation semantics.

Such models are widely used and accepted by their hearing counterparts who remain ignorant to the inherent culture, semantics and colloquial language present in gestural language systems.

Indeed, what is at play here is the standardization of language by technicians into what can be captured by technique: data, statistics, a mathematical language. For that AI technique to exist, sign language must be rationalized, in a search for profit that annihilates the conditions for communication and fails to capture the human experience of the deaf person. By that process, it presents normative effects, creating a model of Man, standardized, massified, and who has to adapt to the tool and technical milieu instead of the other way around, which we assume should have been the goal of such a technology.
  Technique thus reshapes what it means to be human, to submit deaf people to the goals of productivity and efficiency. In doing so, it exhibits clear counter productivity, alienating instead of emancipating, isolating instead of nourishing human relationships. Therefore this paper argues for the idea of AI as Ableist Intelligence, as such systems seek to emphasise the humiliated and marginalised nature of sign.

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current scientific infrastructure is document-centric and only provides citation links, failing to explicitly represent how research methods evolve, adapt, and build on each other. This is especially problematic for AI research agents that need structured, machine-readable representations of methodological development but cannot reliably infer them from unstructured text alone.

Method: The authors automatically construct a methodological evolution graph, Intern-Atlas, from over 1 million AI-related papers. They identify method-level entities, infer lineage relationships between them, and detect bottlenecks that trigger transitions between innovations, grounding 9.4M typed edges in verbatim textual evidence. They also design a self-guided temporal tree search algorithm to build evolution chains from this graph, tracing method progress over time.

Result: Intern-Atlas yields a large, queryable causal network of methodological development in AI with over 9M evidence-backed semantic edges. The evolution chains produced by their temporal tree search align strongly with expert-curated ground-truth chains. The graph also supports downstream tasks such as idea evaluation and automated idea generation, demonstrating its practical utility.

Conclusion: Methodological evolution graphs like Intern-Atlas can serve as a foundational structured data layer for automated scientific discovery, enabling AI systems to reason about and leverage the historical evolution of research methods rather than relying solely on document-level citation networks.

Abstract: Existing research infrastructure is fundamentally document-centric, providing citation links between papers but lacking explicit representations of methodological evolution. In particular, it does not capture the structured relationships that explain how and why research methods emerge, adapt, and build upon one another. With the rise of AI-driven research agents as a new class of consumers of scientific knowledge, this limitation becomes increasingly consequential, as such agents cannot reliably reconstruct method evolution topologies from unstructured text. We introduce Intern-Atlas, a methodological evolution graph that automatically identifies method-level entities, infers lineage relationships among methodologies, and captures the bottlenecks that drive transitions between successive innovations. Built from 1,030,314 papers spanning AI conferences, journals, and arXiv preprints, the resulting graph comprises 9,410,201 semantically typed edges, each grounded in verbatim source evidence, forming a queryable causal network of methodological development. To operationalize this structure, we further propose a self-guided temporal tree search algorithm for constructing evolution chains that trace the progression of methods over time. We evaluate the quality of the resulting graph against expert-curated ground-truth evolution chains and observe strong alignment. In addition, we demonstrate that Intern-Atlas enables downstream applications in idea evaluation and automated idea generation. We position methodological evolution graphs as a foundational data layer for the emerging automated scientific discovery.

### [143] [LLM as Clinical Graph Structure Refiner: Enhancing Representation Learning in EEG Seizure Diagnosis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28178)
*Lincan Li,Zheng Chen,Yushun Dong*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes using large language models (LLMs) to refine edges in EEG-based brain graphs for seizure detection, removing noisy or redundant connections and improving performance and interpretability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: EEG signals used for seizure detection are noisy, which leads existing correlation-based or learning-based graph construction methods to produce redundant or irrelevant edges. These poor-quality graphs harm representation learning and downstream seizure detection performance, motivating a method that can reason about which edges are meaningful and which should be pruned.

Method: The authors design a two-stage LLM-refined graph learning framework. First, they empirically verify that an LLM can serve as an edge refiner by identifying and removing redundant graph connections, improving seizure detection accuracy. Then they build a full pipeline: (1) an initial brain graph is constructed by a Transformer-based edge predictor plus an MLP that assign probability scores to candidate edges, followed by thresholding to determine tentative edges; (2) an LLM takes textual and statistical features of node pairs and refines this edge set, validating or discarding edges. This yields a cleaned, task-oriented graph for downstream seizure detection.

Result: On the TUSZ EEG dataset, the LLM-refined graph learning framework improves seizure detection performance compared with standard graph construction approaches. It also produces graph structures that are sparser, less noisy, and more interpretable in terms of meaningful brain connectivity patterns.

Conclusion: Incorporating LLMs as edge refiners in EEG graph construction can robustly denoise noisy brain graphs, enhancing both seizure detection accuracy and the interpretability of learned graph structures. This suggests LLM-based edge refinement is a promising direction for graph learning in noisy biomedical domains.

Abstract: Electroencephalogram (EEG) signals are vital for automated seizure detection, but their inherent noise makes robust representation learning challenging. Existing graph construction methods, whether correlation-based or learning-based, often generate redundant or irrelevant edges due to the noisy nature of EEG data. This significantly impairs the quality of graph representation and limits downstream task performance. Motivated by the remarkable reasoning and contextual understanding capabilities of large language models (LLMs), we explore the idea of using LLMs as graph edge refiners. Specifically, we propose a two-stage framework: we first verify that LLM-based edge refinement can effectively identify and remove redundant connections, leading to significant improvements in seizure detection accuracy and more meaningful graph structures. Building on this insight, we further develop a robust solution where the initial graph is constructed using a Transformer-based edge predictor and multilayer perceptron, assigning probability scores to potential edges and applying a threshold to determine their existence. The LLM then acts as an edge set refiner, making informed decisions based on both textual and statistical features of node pairs to validate the remaining connections. Extensive experiments on TUSZ dataset demonstrate that our LLM-refined graph learning framework not only enhances task performance but also yields cleaner and more interpretable graph representations.

</details>
